المقصد السادس : مدى تأثير الفعل على الفعل ] ( الممنوع عن الفعل هل هو قادر عليه حال كونه ممنوعاً عنه ( منعه الأشاعرة إذ القدرة ) عندهم ( مع الفعل ) فلا يتصور كون الممنوع عن فعل قادر عليه في حالة المنع إذ لا فعل حينئذ فلا قدرة عليه ( وقال به أي بكون الممنوع قادراً على الفعل ( المعتزلة ) وفرقوا بين العجز والمنع حيث ( قالوا : العجز يضاد القدرة دون المقدور قوله: (المقدور) أي ذات المقدور لا من حيث أنه مقدور فإن المرتعش عاجز أي ليس له قدرة على الحركة مع حصولها اضطراراً . ( والمنع ) بعكسه، فإنه لا يضاد القدرة ، بل يضاد (المقدور ) وينافيه مع بقاء القدرة، سواء كان المنع ( وجودياً مضاداً) بنفسه (للمقدور) كالسكون بالنسبة إلى الحركة المقدورة ( أو ) وجودياً ( مولداً لضده أي ضد المقدور كالاعتمادات السقلية المولدة للحركة السفلية المضادة للحركة العلوية ( أو ) كان (عدمياً) كانتفاء شرط من شرائط المقدور، مثل انتفاء العلم بالفعل المحكم، فإنه ينافي وجود الأحكام دون القدرة عليه ( كل واحد منهما غير قادر على الحركة والانتقال من مكانه لكن الضرورة العقلية شاهد بالفرق بينهما، وليس ذلك إلا بأن المقيد قادر على الحركة دون صاحبه، وقالوا أيضاً : إن الصحيح السالم عن الآفات إذا قيد كان قادراً على الحركة، كما كان قادراً عليها قيل القيد ) وذلك لأنه لم يتبدل ذاته ولا صفته، ولم يطرأ عليه ضد أضداد من القدرة حال القيد الذي ليس هو ضداً لها فوجب بقاء قدرته قطعاً (و) الجواب عن الأول أن يقال : ( عندنا لا فرق بينهما إلا) ما يعود إلى جريان العادة ) من ( بخلق الفعل) مع القدرة (فيه) أي في المقيد حال ارتفاع القيد فإن هذا الارتفاع معتاد ( وعدمه ) أي عدم جريان العادة بخلق الفعل مع القدرة في فيه حال كونه مطلقاً ماشياً ) ولا حاجة) لانتفاء القدرة في المقيد ( إلى طرو ضد ( من أضدادها عليه، بل يكفيه انتفاء خلقها فيه قوله: (والجواب عن الأول إلخ) منع قول المستدل وليس ذلك إلا بأن المقيد قادر على الحركة دون ،صاحبه أي لا نسلم انحصار الترادف بينهما باعتبار وجود القدرة وعدمها لم لا يجوز أن يكون بجريان العادة بخلق الفعل والقدرة في المقيد، إلا أن ارتفاع قيده معناه دون الآخر لأن ارتفاع زمانية، أعني : العجز أو ملزومه غير معناه إلا أنه عبر عنه بصورة الدعوى ترويجاً للمنع وإشارة إلى أنه مبني على ما تقرر عندنا من أن الزمن عاجز دون المقيد وإن كان له منها غير قادر قوله : ( لا فرق بينهما إلا ما يعود إلخ) أي لا فرق بوجود القدرة في أحدهما دون الآخر، وإن وجد الفرق بالنسبة إلى وجود صفة وجودية في أحدهما، وهي العجز دون الآخر فالحصر إضافي، وبهذا ظهر إمكان الجواب عن الأول بوجود الفرق بهذا الاعتبار أيضاً، وحاصل الفرق الذي ذكره المصنف أن زوال القيد معناه دون الزمانة وبناء على ذلك حصول الفعل من أحدهما معتاد دون الآخر.
The sixth objective: the extent of the effect of the act on the act. (The one prevented from the act, is he capable of it while being prevented from it? The Ash'aris denied it, because for them power is with the act, so it is inconceivable that the one prevented from an act is capable of it in the state of prevention, since there is no act then, so there is no power over it. The Mu'tazilites affirmed it, i.e., that the one prevented is capable of the act, and they distinguished between inability ('ajz) and prevention (man'), where they said: 'Inability opposes power without opposing the thing empowered (al-maqdur).' His saying: '(al-maqdur) i.e., the essence of the thing empowered, not in so far as it is empowered, for the one with a tremor is unable, i.e., he has no power over motion while it occurs involuntarily.' And prevention is the opposite of that, for it does not oppose power, but rather opposes the thing empowered and conflicts with it while power remains, whether the prevention is existent opposing the thing empowered itself, like stillness with respect to the empowered motion, or existent generating its opposite, i.e., the opposite of the thing empowered, like heavy pressures generating downward motion which opposes upward motion, or it is non-existent, like the absence of a condition among the conditions of the thing empowered, such as the absence of knowledge of the skilled act, for it precludes the existence of the skilled results without precluding power over it. And they claimed necessity in the difference between the paralyzed (zamin) and the restrained (muqayyad). They said: 'If the one prevented were not capable of what he is prevented from, there would be no difference between the paralyzed, from whom motion is inconceivable, and the restrained healthy person free from defects that prevent motion, because each of them is not capable of motion and moving from his place. But intellectual necessity witnesses a difference between them, and that is only because the restrained is capable of motion while the other is not.' And they also said: 'The healthy person free from defects, when restrained, is capable of motion just as he was capable of it before the restraint, because his essence and attribute have not changed, and no opposite among the opposites of power has befallen him in the state of restraint, which is not an opposite of power. Therefore it is necessarily necessary that his power remains.' And the answer to the first: it is said: 'With us there is no difference between them except what goes back to the running of habit, from the creation of the act with power in the restrained at the time of the removal of the restraint, for this removal is customary, and its absence, i.e., the absence of the running of habit in creating the act with power in the paralyzed, for the removal of his paralysis is not customary. And this amount of difference is sufficient, as witnessed by immediate intuition.' And the answer to the second: we deny that his attributes do not change during the restraint, for God Most High did not create power in him while he is restrained, and created it in him while he is free and walking. And there is no need, for the absence of power in the restrained, for the occurrence of an opposite among its opposites; rather, the absence of its creation in him suffices. His saying: '(And the answer to the first etc.)' is a denial of the claim of the seeker of proof that 'that is only because the restrained is capable of motion without his companion,' i.e., we do not accept the confinement of the difference between them to the consideration of the existence or non-existence of power. Why is it not permissible that it be by the running of habit in creating the act and power in the restrained, except that the removal of his restraint is customary without the other, because the removal of paralysis, i.e., inability or its necessary condition, is not customary, except that he expressed it in the form of the claim to promote the denial and to indicate that it is based on what is settled with us: that the paralyzed is unable (not capable) while the restrained is not even if he has [power]? His saying: '(No difference between them except what goes back etc.)' i.e., no difference by the existence of power in one without the other, although there is a difference in terms of the existence of a qualitative attribute in one, which is inability, not in the other, so the confinement is relative. By this, the possibility of answering the first by the existence of difference in this respect also appears. And the summary of the difference that the author mentioned is that the removal of the restraint is customary while the removal of paralysis is not, and based on that, the occurrence of the act from one is customary without the other.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is an extended theological argument about whether a person who is prevented (mamnū') from performing an act can be described as capable (qādir) of that act while still prevented. The Ash'aris hold that power is simultaneous with the act, so if the act does not occur (due to prevention), there is no power. The Mu'tazilites disagree and distinguish between inability ('ajz) and prevention (man'): inability directly opposes power, while prevention opposes the object of power (maqdūr) without negating power itself. The Mu'tazilites present two arguments. First, a 'necessity' argument: we intuitively know there is a difference between a paralyzed person (zamin) who cannot move and a healthy person who is restrained (muqayyad), and this difference must be that the restrained person retains power over motion. Second, an argument from unchanged attributes: the restrained person's essence and attributes are unchanged, so his power must remain. The Ash'ari responds to the first argument by denying that the difference lies in power; rather, it lies in the custom (jaryān al-'āda): God habitually creates the act and power in the restrained person when the restraint is removed, whereas removing paralysis is not customary. This is sufficient to explain the intuitive difference. To the second argument, the Ash'ari responds by denying that attributes are unchanged: God simply did not create power in the person while restrained; no opposite of power needs to arise. The commentary elaborates that the difference is relative (idāfī): the restrained person has the attribute of 'ajz? Actually the commentary clarifies that the paralyzed has 'ajz (inability) while the restrained does not, but that is not a difference in power itself but in a qualitative attribute. So the confinement of difference to power is not absolute. The lesson thus presents a full objection-response pair.

قادر
عجز
منع
ضد
مقدور
زمن
مقيد

Misconception: That prevention always negates the ability to act.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites argue that prevention only blocks the act, not the underlying power; the prevented person remains capable.

Checkpoint

B) Both oppose power.
C) Both oppose the object of power.
D) Inability opposes the object of power, prevention opposes power.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites distinguish between inability (which negates power) and prevention (which only negates the act), allowing a prevented person to remain capable.

The paralyzed and the restrained appear similar, yet one has capacity and the other does not. Which one are you in your spiritual journey? What would change if you truly believed you retain the capacity to obey even when prevented?
?
You are restrained by your habits, your environment, or your own sins. Does that restraint make you like the paralyzed? Why does the author's insistence on retained power matter for your immediate next step of obedience?

The Ash'ari Response: Habit and Divine Creation
Presents the second Mu'tazilite argument and both Ash'ari responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari responses to the Mu'tazilite arguments, particularly the role of custom (jaryān al-‘āda) and God's direct creation of power.

Core Idea
The difference between paralyzed and restrained is due to custom, not power.

The Mu'tazilites presented two arguments for the prevented person's capacity: the intuitive difference between paralyzed and restrained, and the argument from unchanged attributes. The Ash'aris respond by denying that power remains and instead explaining the intuitive difference by reference to God's custom of creating the act when the restraint is removed, and by asserting that God directly creates power at the moment of action.

Guided Path
1
Read the second Mu'tazilite argument
The Mu'tazilites argue that since the restrained person's essence and attributes are unchanged, his power must remain.
This is an argument from continuity.
2
Read the first Ash'ari response
The Ash'ari response to the first argument: the difference is not in power but in the custom of God creating the act when the restraint is removed.
This reinterprets the intuitive difference.
3
Read the second Ash'ari response
To the second argument, the Ash'aris deny that attributes are unchanged: God simply did not create power in the restrained state, and no opposite of power is needed.
Power is created directly by God, not inherent.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this debate ties into the broader Ash'ari occasionalist framework, where all acts are directly created by God.

إلخ) أي لا فرق بوجود القدرة في أحدهما دون الآخر، وإن وجد الفرق بالنسبة إلى وجود صفة وجودية في أحدهما، وهي العجز دون الآخر فالحصر إضافي، وبهذا ظهر إمكان الجواب عن الأول بوجود الفرق بهذا الاعتبار أيضاً، وحاصل الفرق الذي ذكره المصنف أن زوال القيد معناه دون الزمانة وبناء على ذلك حصول الفعل من أحدهما معتاد دون الآخر.
The sixth objective: the extent of the effect of the act on the act. (The one prevented from the act, is he capable of it while being prevented from it? The Ash'aris denied it, because for them power is with the act, so it is inconceivable that the one prevented from an act is capable of it in the state of prevention, since there is no act then, so there is no power over it. The Mu'tazilites affirmed it, i.e., that the one prevented is capable of the act, and they distinguished between inability ('ajz) and prevention (man'), where they said: 'Inability opposes power without opposing the thing empowered (al-maqdur).' His saying: '(al-maqdur) i.e., the essence of the thing empowered, not in so far as it is empowered, for the one with a tremor is unable, i.e., he has no power over motion while it occurs involuntarily.' And prevention is the opposite of that, for it does not oppose power, but rather opposes the thing empowered and conflicts with it while power remains, whether the prevention is existent opposing the thing empowered itself, like stillness with respect to the empowered motion, or existent generating its opposite, i.e., the opposite of the thing empowered, like heavy pressures generating downward motion which opposes upward motion, or it is non-existent, like the absence of a condition among the conditions of the thing empowered, such as the absence of knowledge of the skilled act, for it precludes the existence of the skilled results without precluding power over it. And they claimed necessity in the difference between the paralyzed (zamin) and the restrained (muqayyad). They said: 'If the one prevented were not capable of what he is prevented from, there would be no difference between the paralyzed, from whom motion is inconceivable, and the restrained healthy person free from defects that prevent motion, because each of them is not capable of motion and moving from his place. But intellectual necessity witnesses a difference between them, and that is only because the restrained is capable of motion while the other is not.' And they also said: 'The healthy person free from defects, when restrained, is capable of motion just as he was capable of it before the restraint, because his essence and attribute have not changed, and no opposite among the opposites of power has befallen him in the state of restraint, which is not an opposite of power. Therefore it is necessarily necessary that his power remains.' And the answer to the first: it is said: 'With us there is no difference between them except what goes back to the running of habit, from the creation of the act with power in the restrained at the time of the removal of the restraint, for this removal is customary, and its absence, i.e., the absence of the running of habit in creating the act with power in the paralyzed, for the removal of his paralysis is not customary. And this amount of difference is sufficient, as witnessed by immediate intuition.' And the answer to the second: we deny that his attributes do not change during the restraint, for God Most High did not create power in him while he is restrained, and created it in him while he is free and walking. And there is no need, for the absence of power in the restrained, for the occurrence of an opposite among its opposites; rather, the absence of its creation in him suffices. His saying: '(And the answer to the first etc.)' is a denial of the claim of the seeker of proof that 'that is only because the restrained is capable of motion without his companion,' i.e., we do not accept the confinement of the difference between them to the consideration of the existence or non-existence of power. Why is it not permissible that it be by the running of habit in creating the act and power in the restrained, except that the removal of his restraint is customary without the other, because the removal of paralysis, i.e., inability or its necessary condition, is not customary, except that he expressed it in the form of the claim to promote the denial and to indicate that it is based on what is settled with us: that the paralyzed is unable (not capable) while the restrained is not even if he has [power]? His saying: '(No difference between them except what goes back etc.)' i.e., no difference by the existence of power in one without the other, although there is a difference in terms of the existence of a qualitative attribute in one, which is inability, not in the other, so the confinement is relative. By this, the possibility of answering the first by the existence of difference in this respect also appears. And the summary of the difference that the author mentioned is that the removal of the restraint is customary while the removal of paralysis is not, and based on that, the occurrence of the act from one is customary without the other.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is an extended theological argument about whether a person who is prevented (mamnū') from performing an act can be described as capable (qādir) of that act while still prevented. The Ash'aris hold that power is simultaneous with the act, so if the act does not occur (due to prevention), there is no power. The Mu'tazilites disagree and distinguish between inability ('ajz) and prevention (man'): inability directly opposes power, while prevention opposes the object of power (maqdūr) without negating power itself. The Mu'tazilites present two arguments. First, a 'necessity' argument: we intuitively know there is a difference between a paralyzed person (zamin) who cannot move and a healthy person who is restrained (muqayyad), and this difference must be that the restrained person retains power over motion. Second, an argument from unchanged attributes: the restrained person's essence and attributes are unchanged, so his power must remain. The Ash'ari responds to the first argument by denying that the difference lies in power; rather, it lies in the custom (jaryān al-'āda): God habitually creates the act and power in the restrained person when the restraint is removed, whereas removing paralysis is not customary. This is sufficient to explain the intuitive difference. To the second argument, the Ash'ari responds by denying that attributes are unchanged: God simply did not create power in the person while restrained; no opposite of power needs to arise. The commentary elaborates that the difference is relative (idāfī): the restrained person has the attribute of 'ajz? Actually the commentary clarifies that the paralyzed has 'ajz (inability) while the restrained does not, but that is not a difference in power itself but in a qualitative attribute. So the confinement of difference to power is not absolute. The lesson thus presents a full objection-response pair.

Misconception: That the Ash'ari response denies any difference between paralyzed and restrained.
Correction: The Ash'aris affirm the difference but explain it by God's custom, not by inherent power.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response to the first argument, what explains the difference between the paralyzed and the restrained?
A) The restrained has power, the paralyzed does not.
B) God's custom: removal of restraint is habitual, removal of paralysis is not.

D) The paralyzed has a stronger prevention.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris explain the intuitive difference between paralyzed and restrained by God's custom, not by an inherent power that persists through prevention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you consider the difference between a restrained person and a paralyzed person, do you feel a sense of helplessness that reminds you of your total dependence on Allah's creation of ability moment by moment? How can you transform that feeling into a constant awareness of His power in your daily activities?
?
The Ash'aris argue that the intuitive difference we perceive is not due to inherent power but to God's custom. Have you ever mistaken a habit of nature for a necessary law, forgetting that Allah could break it at any moment? What would change in your life if you truly internalized that everything happens by His direct will and custom?
?
The second response states that God does not create power in a restrained person, and only creates it when he is free. When you face obstacles, do you assume that God has already given you the power to overcome them, or do you sincerely beg Him to create that power in you at the moment? How can you cultivate this dependence in your supplications and actions?

The Difference Between Paralyzed and Restrained
Al-Jurjani presents and answers two objections regarding the difference between the paralyzed and the restrained.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school explains the difference between a paralyzed person and a restrained person without appealing to inherent power.

Core Idea
The difference lies in divine custom, not in the presence or absence of power.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents a key objection against the Ash'ari position on power: if the restrained person is not truly powerful, how is he different from the paralyzed? The objection seems intuitive, but the Ash'ari answer reveals a deeper point about divine custom. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the objectors' claim that there is no difference between the paralyzed and the restrained if the restrained is not powerful.
The objection appeals to necessary knowledge (ḍarūra).
2
Understand the two answers
The passage provides two answers. The first answer appeals to divine custom (ʿāda): God customarily creates the act when the restraint is removed. The second answer denies that the restrained's attributes remain unchanged: God does not create power in him while restrained.
3
Reflect on the role of custom in Ash'ari thought
Ask yourself why custom is important. The Ash'ari school uses custom to explain regularities in the world without necessitating causality. This shows how they reconcile divine omnipotence with apparent differences in human capacity.
This connects to the learning objective.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد السادس : مدى تأثير القدرة على الفعل أصلاً، والمنع بعكسه، فإنه لا يضاد القدرة، بل يضاد المقدور وينافيه مع بقاء القدرة، سواء كان المنع وجودياً مضاداً بنفسه للمقدور كالسكون بالنسبة إلى الحركة المقدورة، أو وجودياً مولداً لضده أي ضد المقدور كالاعتمادات السقلية المولدة للحركة السفلية المضادة للحركة العلوية، أو كان عدمياً كانتفاء شرط من شرائط المقدور، مثل انتفاء العلم بالفعل المحكم، فإنه ينافي وجود الأحكام دون القدرة عليه. وادعوا الضرورة في الفرق بين الزمن والمقيد، أي قالوا: لو لم يكن الممنوع قادراً على ما منع منه لم يكن فرق بين الزمن الذي لا يتصور منه الحركة وبين المقيد الصحيح السالم عن الآفات المانعة عن الحركة، لأن كل واحد منهما غير قادر على الحركة والانتقال من مكانه لكن الضرورة العقلية شاهدة بالفرق بينهما، وليس ذلك إلا بأن المقيد قادر على الحركة دون صاحبه. وقالوا أيضاً: إن الصحيح السالم عن الآفات إذا قيد كان قادراً على الحركة، كما كان قادراً عليها قبل القيد، وذلك لأنه لم يتبدل ذاته ولا صفته، ولم يطرأ عليه ضد من أضداد القدرة حال القيد الذي ليس هو ضداً لها فوجب بقاء قدرته قطعاً. والجواب عن الأول أن يقال: عندنا لا فرق بينهما إلا ما يعود إلى جريان العادة من خلق الفعل مع القدرة فيه أي في المقيد حال ارتفاع القيد، فإن هذا الارتفاع معتاد، وعدمه أي عدم جريان العادة بخلق الفعل مع القدرة في الزمن، فإن ارتفاع زمانته غير معتاد، وهذا المقدار من الفرق كاف بشهادة البديهة. والجواب عن الثاني: أنا نمنع عدم تبدل صفاته حال القيد، فإن الله تعالى لم يخلق فيه القدرة حال كونه مقيداً، وخلقها فيه حال كونه مطلقاً ماشياً، ولا حاجة لانتفاء القدرة في المقيد إلى طرو ضد من أضدادها عليه، بل يكفيه انتفاء خلقها فيه.
Fourth type - Sixth topic: The extent of the effect of power on the act originally. And prevention is the opposite of that, for it does not oppose power, but rather opposes the object of power and contradicts it while power remains, whether the prevention is existential directly opposed to the object of power, like rest in relation to the intended motion, or existential generating its opposite, i.e., the opposite of the object of power, like downward tendencies generating downward motion opposite to upward motion, or it is non-existential, like the absence of a condition of the object of power, such as the absence of knowledge of the exact act, for it precludes the existence of the judgments without power over it. And they claimed necessary knowledge regarding the difference between the paralyzed and the restrained, i.e., they said: If the restrained person were not capable of that from which he is prevented, there would be no difference between the paralyzed, from whom motion cannot be conceived, and the restrained, healthy, free from impediments that prevent motion, because each of them is unable to move and change place, but rational necessity witnesses the difference between them, and that is only because the restrained is capable of motion while the other is not. And they also said: The healthy, free from impediments, when restrained, is capable of motion, just as he was capable of it before restraint, because his essence and attribute have not changed, nor has any opposite of power occurred to him in the state of restraint, which is not an opposite to power, so his power necessarily remains. And the answer to the first is that we say: In our view, there is no difference between them except that which goes back to the customary course of creating the act with power in him, i.e., in the restrained at the time of the removal of the restraint, for this removal is customary, and its absence, i.e., the absence of the customary creation of the act with power in the paralyzed, for the removal of his paralysis is not customary, and this amount of difference is sufficient as attested by intuition. And the answer to the second: We deny that his attributes do not change at the state of restraint, for God Almighty did not create power in him while he was restrained, and He created it in him while he was free and walking, and there is no need for the non-existence of power in the restrained to [require] the occurrence of an opposite from its opposites upon it, rather it is sufficient that He did not create it in him.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer passage. The author presents the objection of those who argue that the restrained person must be powerful because otherwise there would be no difference between the paralyzed and the restrained. The objectors claim that the difference is due to the presence or absence of power. The author gives two answers. First, the difference is only in the customary action of God: God customarily creates the act (e.g., motion) along with power in the restrained when the restraint is removed, whereas for the paralyzed, the removal of paralysis is not customary. Thus the difference lies in the custom of divine creation, not in the power itself. Second, the attributes of the restrained do change: God does not create power in him while restrained, but creates it when he is free. Thus the non-existence of power in the restrained is not due to an opposite occurring, but simply because God does not create it. The passage concludes that this suffices as an answer.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari school denies that the restrained person has any power at all.
Correction: The Ash'ari school affirms that the restrained person has power, but God does not create the act (e.g., motion) along with that power while the restraint remains; the act is created only when the restraint is removed, according to divine custom.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari answer in this passage, what explains the difference between the paralyzed and the restrained?
A) The paralyzed has no power while the restrained has power.
B) The custom of God creating the act in the restrained at the removal of restraint.
C) The restrained has a different attribute.
D) The paralyzed is weak.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that power is present in the restrained person, but God customarily creates the act only when the restraint is removed; the lack of act is due to non-creation, not absence of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to do something you intended, do you immediately assume you lacked the ability, or do you consider that God simply did not create the act at that moment? How would this shift in perspective change your reaction to failure?
?
If your power to act is entirely dependent on God's creation of it at each moment, what does that mean for your pride in your achievements? Can you genuinely attribute any success to your own efforts without forgetting the source?
?
The passage says the restrained person is capable of motion only when the restraint is removed by God's custom. Is there a 'restraint' in your life—a habit, a fear, a circumstance—that you believe God will remove in His time? How does trusting in His custom rather than your own power affect your patience?

Single Power and Opposite Acts
Al-Jurjani states the Ash'ari argument and contrasts with Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that a single power cannot relate to two opposites, and how it differs from the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
A single power cannot cause two opposites without logical contradiction.

This passage presents a central debate in Islamic theology: can one power produce two opposite actions? Al-Jurjani explains the Ash'ari stance, rooted in their view that power accompanies the act, and contrasts it with the Mu'tazila view. The discussion highlights the logical constraints on divine and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic original and the English translation to get an overview of the argument. Note the key terms: قدرة (power), ضدان (opposites), مقدور (object of power).
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the complete argument given.
2
Identify the Ash'ari claim
Find the statement that a single power does not relate to two opposites. Notice the conditional proof: if it did, the opposites would have to coexist.
The Ash'aris base this on their principle that power is with the act, not before it.
3
Understand the supporting reasons
The passage gives two reasons: (1) logical impossibility of opposites coexisting, and (2) empirical observation that the power we find at one act differs from the power at another act.
This extends to any two objects of power, not just opposites.
4
Contrast with Mu'tazila
See how the Mu'tazila hold that a servant's power can relate to all his possible acts, including opposites. Note the hesitation of Abu Hashim, which shows internal disagreement.
This contrast highlights the key difference in how power relates to action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد السابع : القدرة مع الفعل لا تتعلق بالضدين. قال الشيخ وأكثر أصحابه بناء على كون القدرة عندهم مع الفعل لا قبله إنها أي القدرة الواحدة لا تتعلق بالضدين وإلا لزم اجتماعهما لوجوب مقارنتهما لتلك القدرة المتعلقة بهما بل قالوا إن القدرة الواحدة لا تتعلق بمقدورين مطلقاً سواء كانا متضادين أو متماثلين أو مختلفين لا معاً ولا على سبيل البدل بل القدرة الواحدة لا تتعلق إلا بمقدور واحد وذلك لأنها مع المقدور ولا شك أن ما نجده عند صدور أحد المقدورين منا مغاير لما نجده عند صدور الآخر. وقالت المعتزلة أي أكثرهم قدرة العبد تتعلق بجميع مقدوراته المتضادة وغير المتضادة. وقول أبي هاشم من بينهم متردد تردداً فاحشاً فقال مرة:
Fourth type - Seventh topic: Power with the act does not relate to two opposites. The Shaykh and most of his companions, based on the position that power is with the act and not before it, said: That (i.e., a single power) does not relate to two opposites; otherwise the two opposites would necessarily coexist due to their necessary concomitance with that power that relates to them. Rather, they said: A single power does not relate to two objects of power absolutely, whether they are opposites, similars, or different, neither simultaneously nor alternatively; rather a single power relates only to one object of power, because it is with the object of power, and there is no doubt that what we find in ourselves at the occurrence of one object of power is different from what we find at the occurrence of the other. And the Mu'tazila (most of them) said: The power of the servant relates to all of his objects of power, opposites and non-opposites. And the statement of Abu Hashim among them is extremely hesitant: he said once:
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof passage. It opens by declaring the Ash'ari doctrine: a single power does not relate to two opposites. The reasoning is that if it did, the two opposites would have to coexist because they would both necessarily accompany that power that relates to both—this is a logical impossibility. Moreover, the Ash'aris extend this to any two objects of power, whether opposites or not: a single power cannot relate to two objects at all, whether simultaneously or alternatively, because power is simultaneous with its object, and our experience shows that the power we find when performing one act is different from when performing another. The passage then mentions the Mu'tazila position, that a servant's power relates to all his possible acts including opposites, and notes that Abu Hashim's view among them is extremely hesitant, but does not complete his statement.

Key Terms
قدرة
ضدان
مقدور
بدل
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari position denies human choice, but it only denies that a single power can be the source of two simultaneous or alternative acts.
Correction: The passage does not deny human agency; it argues that each distinct act requires a distinct power, which is consistent with the Ash'ari occasionalist framework.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari argument, why cannot a single power relate to two opposites?
A) Because power is created before the act.
B) Because opposites would then coexist.
C) Because power is with the act and limited.
D) Because the Mu'tazila are wrong.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argue that a single power is specific to one act, preventing logical contradictions like coexisting opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your power (qudra) can only be directed at one act at a time, how will you stop dividing your heart between worship and worldly distractions? This week, choose one sincere act of obedience and commit to it fully—don't let your focus fragment.
?
The Ash'aris argue that the power you feel when doing one act is different from when doing another. When you sin, do you claim 'I couldn't help it'? This passage forces you to see that your power is entirely tied to the act you choose. Will you take ownership of your deeds starting today?
?
The Mu'tazila say your power can relate to all your possible acts—then why do you so often fail to do what is good? Are you hiding behind a false sense of ability while neglecting your duties? Repentance requires action now, not empty potential.

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Power and Opposites
Al-Iji refutes the Mu'tazila claim that a single power relates to opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational objection against the Mu'tazila claim that a single power can relate to two opposites, and how their attempted defense fails.

Core Idea
A power cannot simultaneously relate to opposites like knowledge and forgetfulness.

In this passage, al-Iji presents a classic Ash'ari refutation of the Mu'tazila doctrine that a single power (qudra) can relate to two opposite actions. He argues that if that were true, then the very power that causes knowledge would also cause its opposite, forgetfulness, which is contrary to what every rational person knows. The Mu'tazila attempt to escape by defining forgetfulness as a mere absence, but al-Iji shows that this still leads to a contradiction within their own framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila assert that a single power can relate to two opposites simultaneously. Locate this claim in the passage.
This is the position being refuted.
2
Understand the objection
If a single power relates to opposites, then the power for knowledge would also produce its opposite, forgetfulness, which is not what we experience.
The author uses common sense as evidence.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila response
The Mu'tazila respond that forgetfulness is not an opposite but an absence of the faculty of knowledge. The author concedes this for argument but then presents a counter-argument based on their own principles.
The counter-argument relies on the principle that accidents are only removed by an opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وقالت المعتزلة) يرد عليهم أن السهو مضاد للعلم ويلزم من كون القدرة الواحدة متعلقة بالضدين معاً أن تكون القدرة بالعلم متعلقة بالسهو، وهذا خلاف ما يجده كل عاقل من من أن السهو ليس بمقدور كما يجد من نفسه عدم القدرة على الألوان والطعوم فإن أجابوا بأن السهو عدم ملكة للعلم لا ضد له قلنا بعد التنزل عن لزوم كون الشك سهواً: إنكم اتفقتم على أن العلم وجميع الأعراض لا تنتفي إلا بطريان ضد عليها بالمعنى المتعارف فيلزمكم أن لا ينتفى العلم بطريان السهو، وللمعتزلة عن هذا الإلزام اختلافات مذكورة في أبكار الأفكار لا يناسب ذكرها في هذا المختصر .
القدرة القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بجميع (متعلقاتها كالاعتقادات والإرادات ونحوها ( دون ) القدرة ( القائمة بالجوارح ) فإنها لا تتعلق بجميع مقدوراتها من الاعتمادات والحركات وغيرها (و) قال تارة أخرى : كل واحدة منهما، أي من قدرة القلب وقدرة الجوارح ) تتعلق بجميع متعلقاتها دون متعلقات الأخرى (و) قال ( تارة) ثالثة : ( ( كل واحدة منهما تتعلق بمتعلقاتها التي هي أفعال القلوب والجوارح ( جميعاً غير ) أن كلاً منهما ( لا يؤثر في متعلقات الأخرى لعدم (الآلة) أي يمتنع إيجاد أفعال الجوارح بالقدرة القائمة بالقلب لعدم الآلات والبنية المخصوصة المناسبة لتلك الأفعال، وكذا العكس ( و ) قال ( مرة ) رابعة : ( القدرة القلبية تتعلق بمتعلقاتهما) معاً ( دون ) القدرة (العضوية) فإنها تتعلق بأفعال الجوارح دون أفعال القلوب
His saying: (And the Mu'tazila said) - He refutes them: that forgetfulness (sahw) is opposite to knowledge, and it would follow from the single power being related to both opposites together that the power that is through knowledge would be related to forgetfulness. This goes against what every rational person finds: that forgetfulness is not within power, just as one finds from oneself the inability to control colors and tastes. If they answer that forgetfulness is the absence of the faculty of knowledge, not an opposite to it, we say, after conceding the necessity of doubt being forgetfulness: You have agreed that knowledge and all accidents are not removed except by the occurrence of an opposite upon them in the conventional sense, so it necessarily follows for you that knowledge cannot be removed by the occurrence of forgetfulness. And the Mu'tazila have variations concerning this objection mentioned in Abkār al-Afkār, which are not appropriate to mention in this abridgement.
The power established in the heart relates to all its objects, such as beliefs, volitions, and the like, but not the power established in the limbs, for it does not relate to all its objects, such as dependencies, movements, and others. And he said another time: Each one of them, i.e., the power of the heart and the power of the limbs, relates to all its objects but not to the objects of the other. And he said a third time: Each one of them relates to its objects which are the acts of hearts and limbs entirely, except that each one does not affect the objects of the other due to the lack of the instrument (āla), i.e., it is impossible to produce acts of the limbs by the power established in the heart due to the lack of the instruments and the specific structure suitable for those acts, and likewise the reverse. And he said a fourth time: The power of the heart relates to the objects of both together, but not the organic power, for it relates to the acts of the limbs but not the acts of the hearts.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila view and a refutation, followed by the four contradictory statements of Abu Hashim. The refutation begins by noting the Mu'tazila claim that a single power can relate to opposites. The author objects: if so, then the power for knowledge would also affect forgetfulness, which is its opposite, but we know forgetfulness is not within our power. The Mu'tazila might respond that forgetfulness is merely an absence of knowledge, not an opposite. The author concedes that point (after some debate) but then argues: you (Mu'tazila) hold that accidents like knowledge are only removed by the occurrence of an opposite; thus knowledge cannot be removed by forgetfulness (since it is not an opposite), which contradicts experience. The author notes there are other refutations not mentioned. Then the text shifts to the statements of Abu Hashim. He first said the power of the heart relates to all its objects (beliefs, volitions) but not the power of the limbs; second, each power relates only to its own objects; third, each power relates to all objects of both kinds but lacks the instruments to affect the other domain; fourth, the heart's power relates to both, but the limb power only to limb acts. These four positions are inconsistent, showing the confusion within the Mu'tazila on this issue.

Key Terms
السهو
العلم
الضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Forgetfulness is a positive opposite to knowledge, so the power for knowledge can also produce forgetfulness.
Correction: Even if forgetfulness were a positive opposite, the argument would still work; but the Mu'tazila define it as an absence, yet their own principles still lead to contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the refutation against the Mu'tazila claim that a single power relates to opposites?
A) It would require the power for knowledge to also produce forgetfulness, which is not possible.
B) It contradicts the Ash'ari doctrine.
C) Abu Hashim denied it.
D) The Qur'an forbids it.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila claim that a single power relates to opposites leads to absurdity: the power for knowledge would also produce forgetfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If forgetfulness is not within your power, how often do you flatter yourself that you can simply 'forget' a sin or a wrong thought by your own will? Have you truly turned to Allah to erase what you cannot erase yourself?
?
You rely on your memory for your faith—to remember Allah, His commands, and His signs. Since you cannot control forgetfulness, who holds the keys to your knowledge? What does that demand of you in your daily prayers and supplications?
?
Consider the moments you desperately wanted to forget a painful word or a shameful action but could not. Does that not prove that your power is limited and that only Allah can bring true forgetfulness or true remembrance? How will you live differently knowing this about yourself today?

Abu Hashim's Four Statements on the Scope of Power
Al-Iji lists Abu Hashim's four conflicting statements on the powers of heart and limbs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four contradictory positions of Abu Hashim regarding whether the power of the heart and the power of the limbs relate to the same or different objects.

Core Idea
Abu Hashim gave four conflicting accounts of how heart-power and limb-power relate to their objects.

Here, al-Iji presents the inconsistent statements of the Mu'tazili scholar Abu Hashim on the scope of two types of power: the power residing in the heart (qalb) and the power residing in the limbs (jawārih). Abu Hashim first said the heart-power relates to all its objects but not the limb-power; then he changed to each relating only to its own; then to each relating to all but lacking the instruments; and finally to heart-power relating to both while limb-power only to limb acts. The contradictions show the confusion in the Mu'tazila position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two powers
Note that there are two powers: the power of the heart (qudra qalbiyya) and the power of the limbs (qudra 'udwiyya).
The heart-power deals with beliefs and volitions; limb-power deals with actions and movements.
2
List the four statements in order
Read through the passage and write down each of the four statements as they are presented.
They are contradictory, so pay attention to the differences.
3
Understand the role of the instrument (āla)
In the third statement, Abu Hashim introduces the idea that each power relates to all objects but cannot affect the other's domain due to lack of suitable instruments.
This is a nuanced attempt to reconcile the two powers.
4
Observe the contradictions
Compare the statements: the first says heart-power relates to all its objects but limb-power does not; the second says each relates only to its own; the third says both relate to all but without instruments; the fourth says heart-power relates to both and limb-power only to limb acts.
These cannot all be true simultaneously.

?

Will you investigate one such contradiction today and seek coherence?
?

Reflect on a time you attempted something beyond your 'instruments' — intellect, skill, or circumstances. Did you blame the lack of means or your own misconception of power? How does this lesson urge you to assess your true capabilities?
?

When your body acts against your convictions, do you recognize a lapse in your heart's power? What will you do today to align your limbs with your beliefs?

Sleep and Ability: The Core Question and Consensus
Poses the question and reports the agreement on sleeper's action limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central question about sleep and ability, and the agreement between Mu'tazila and Ash'aris on the sleeper's capacity for precise actions.

Core Idea
Sleep restricts ability but does not completely nullify it for simple actions.

This lesson introduces the tenth goal of the philosophers: the relationship between sleep and human ability. The text poses a key question: is sleep the opposite of ability? It then reports a consensus that a sleeper cannot perform numerous precise actions, but can perform a few—based on experience.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
Read the opening question carefully: Is sleep the opposite of ability or not? Note that the answer determines whether the sleeper's actions are within his power.
This question structures the whole debate.
2
Identify the consensus
Find where the Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris agree: they say a sleeper cannot do many precise actions, but can do a few. This is based on experience (tajriba).
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction between many and few actions matters. It shows that ability is not completely absent during sleep, but is limited.
This prepares for the disagreement in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد العاشر : قول الحكماء حول النوم والقدرة ] هل النوم ضد للقدرة فلا يكون حينئذ فعل النائم مقدوراً له أو ليس ضداً لها، فجاز أن يكون فعله مقدوراً له؟ فنقول : ( اتفقت المعتزلة وكثير منا على امتناع صدور الأفعال المتقنة الكثيرة من النائم وجواز ) صدور الأفعال المحكمة (القليلة) منه ) بالتجربة ثم اختلف المجوزون في هذه الأفعال القليلة فقيل : هي مقدورة له وإن كان لا علم له بها.
The Fourth Type - The Tenth Goal: The statement of the philosophers concerning sleep and ability] Is sleep the opposite of ability, so that the sleeper's action is not within his power, or is it not opposite to it, so that his action may be within his power? We say: (The Mu'tazila and many of us agree on the impossibility of numerous precise actions issuing from a sleeper, but allow) the issuance of precise (few) actions from him (by experience). Then those who allowed differed concerning these few actions: it was said that they are within his power, even if he has no knowledge of them.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage defines the tenth goal, which examines the relationship between sleep and ability. The central question is: is sleep the opposite (ḍidd) of ability, in which case the sleeper's actions are not within his power (maqdūr), or is it not opposite, so that his actions may be considered within his power? The author reports that both the Mu'tazila and many of 'us' (i.e., Ash'aris) agree, based on experience, that a sleeper cannot perform numerous precise actions, but he can perform a few precise actions. However, even among those who allow the possibility of few actions, there is disagreement: some hold that these few actions are within the sleeper's power (maqdūr lahu) even though he has no knowledge of them (lā ʿilm lahu bihā). The passage thus sets up a key debate about the role of knowledge and will in the attribution of power over actions.

Key Terms
النوم
القدرة
ضد
مقدور
فعل متقن
التجربة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sleep completely eliminates all ability, so the sleeper cannot do any action at all.
Correction: The text shows that the Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris agree that a sleeper can perform a few precise actions, based on experience.

Checkpoint
What do the Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris agree on regarding the sleeper's actions?
A) The sleeper cannot perform any actions.
B) The sleeper can perform numerous precise actions.
C) The sleeper cannot perform numerous precise actions but can perform a few precise actions.
D) The sleeper's actions are always unintentional.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris agree that a sleeper can perform a few precise actions, but not many, based on experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have experienced sleep and moments of low energy. Based on this lesson, what 'few precise actions' can you still commit to when you feel completely exhausted? Will you use the limited ability you still have, or will you use it as an excuse to abandon all responsibility?
?
The Mu'tazila and Ash'aris agreed from experience that a sleeper can still perform some actions. When you find yourself in a state of spiritual laziness, do you still hold yourself accountable for the few acts of worship or kindness you could easily do? What is stopping you from doing them right now?
?
This lesson teaches that ability is not entirely removed by sleep. Think of a time you felt you 'couldn't' do something due to tiredness. Was that truly a lack of ability, or a lack of will? How will you test your own limits today by attempting one precise action you thought you couldn't do?

Disagreement on the Sleeper's Few Actions: Are They Within His Power?
Reports the disagreement on the sleeper's few actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement among scholars about whether a sleeper's few precise actions are within his power, and the opinion that they are even without knowledge.

Core Idea
Some scholars hold that a sleeper's actions can be within his power even if he has no knowledge of them.

This lesson continues from the previous one. After establishing that a sleeper can perform few precise actions, the text reports a disagreement: some scholars argue that these actions are within the sleeper's power (maqdūr lahu), despite him having no knowledge of them. This raises deeper questions about the relationship between knowledge, will, and ability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the point of disagreement
After the consensus, the text says 'then those who allowed differed'. Find what they differed about: the status of the few precise actions.
2
Understand the opinion that they are within his power
One group said these actions are 'within his power' even though he has no knowledge of them. This is a key claim: ability does not require conscious awareness.
This challenges the common link between knowledge and ability.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this matters: if actions without knowledge can be within one's power, then the sleeper is responsible for them? This connects to debates about moral responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد العاشر : قول الحكماء حول النوم والقدرة ] هل النوم ضد للقدرة فلا يكون حينئذ فعل النائم مقدوراً له أو ليس ضداً لها، فجاز أن يكون فعله مقدوراً له؟ فنقول : ( اتفقت المعتزلة وكثير منا على امتناع صدور الأفعال المتقنة الكثيرة من النائم وجواز ) صدور الأفعال المحكمة (القليلة) منه ) بالتجربة ثم اختلف المجوزون في هذه الأفعال القليلة فقيل : هي مقدورة له وإن كان لا علم له بها.
The Fourth Type - The Tenth Goal: The statement of the philosophers concerning sleep and ability] Is sleep the opposite of ability, so that the sleeper's action is not within his power, or is it not opposite to it, so that his action may be within his power? We say: (The Mu'tazila and many of us agree on the impossibility of numerous precise actions issuing from a sleeper, but allow) the issuance of precise (few) actions from him (by experience). Then those who allowed differed concerning these few actions: it was said that they are within his power, even if he has no knowledge of them.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage defines the tenth goal, which examines the relationship between sleep and ability. The central question is: is sleep the opposite (ḍidd) of ability, in which case the sleeper's actions are not within his power (maqdūr), or is it not opposite, so that his actions may be considered within his power? The author reports that both the Mu'tazila and many of 'us' (i.e., Ash'aris) agree, based on experience, that a sleeper cannot perform numerous precise actions, but he can perform a few precise actions. However, even among those who allow the possibility of few actions, there is disagreement: some hold that these few actions are within the sleeper's power (maqdūr lahu) even though he has no knowledge of them (lā ʿilm lahu bihā). The passage thus sets up a key debate about the role of knowledge and will in the attribution of power over actions.

Correction: The text reports that some scholars allow that a sleeper's actions are within his power even without knowledge.

A) They are not within his power because he lacks knowledge.
B) They are within his power even though he has no knowledge of them.
C) They are within his power only if he is aware of them.
D) They are not actions at all.

Key Takeaway
Some scholars maintain that a sleeper's few precise actions are within his power despite his lack of knowledge, separating ability from conscious awareness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

After reading that some scholars say such actions can still be within your power, does that change how you view your own accountability for them? Will you now be more mindful of your unconscious habits, knowing they may still be attributed to you?
?

Does this lesson expose an assumption you've been hiding behind? Honestly, are there areas of your life where you've used 'lack of awareness' as a shield from responsibility, and how will you respond to this teaching going forward?

Dispute Over Two Persons Carrying a Weight Together
Al-Iji presents Mu'tazila disagreement on joint action by two powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila disagreement on whether two independent powers can act on the same object, and the theological implications of attributing joint causation.

Core Idea
Two powers cannot act on one indivisible object without contradiction.

This lesson delves into a classic kalam problem: when two persons, each capable of carrying a 100-weight, attempt to carry it together, what role does each power play? The Mu'tazila were divided on this, and their arguments reveal fundamental assumptions about causality and divine power. We will explore the two main opinions and the objections raised against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage orienting to the dispute
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow: first the majority view, then the minority view, then objections.
The passage is dense; take time to follow the argument.
2
Identify the two opinions
Note the two groups: the majority (أكثرهم) and 'Abbad and al-Ka'bi. How does each view the allocation of power?
3
Examine the objection on indivisibility
The majority view leads to problems if the object is indivisible: either no effect or division of the indivisible.
This is a key argument in kalām against multiple independent causes.
4
Consider the objection on specification
The minority view cannot specify which part belongs to which power, since all parts are equally related.
5
Reflect on the commentator's conclusion
The truth is that the carrying occurs by the combined powers as a totality, a view the Mu'tazila did not adopt.
This resolution hints at later Ashʿarī doctrines on acquisition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الواقعة في المحال المتلاصقة صادرة من قدرة مساوية بعدد الأجزاء التي لا تتجزئ ولا يجوز أن يقع من قدرة واحدة. الخ يعني المتلاصقتين محال مجتمعة، لا يكفي في حملها قدرة واحدة لكن يتوجه عليهم بعد تسليم فلا يتعين شيء منها لفعل أحدهما .
The second branch: Two persons each able to carry one hundred, when they both together carry it, i.e. carrying the hundred and they both carry it together, the Mu'tazila differ here. Among them is one who said—and the majority of them—that its being carried occurs by the power of each one individually, so each of them does in every part of the hundred at the time of combination what he used to do at the time of individuality. And there follows from it the conjunction of two independent powers on a single object of power, so each can dispense with the other. And perhaps this proponent allowed their conjunction although it is extremely unlikely, rather impossible. And among them is one who said—and he is 'Abbad al-Dimayri and al-Ka'bi—that this one carries some part such that the other does not share in it. He said: Your starting point [requires] the necessity of the attachment of power to all objects of power conditional on that condition. He said: (Some of them said etc.) in it that they did not say that it occurs by the totality of the two powers such that each of them in the form of concurrency is a fully effective cause, because the carrying of the whole is only carried by the other; so when they both come together on an indivisible [object], then either it is said that there is no effect for either of them together with the totality—and this is clearly false because the part of the totality is nothing other than the two powers—or it is said that the effect is distributed in it, and then the division of the part follows. He said: (And perhaps he allowed etc.) by the difference between the two obligations and the two powers, for power follows will, so it is permissible that he intend the occurrence of a single object of power by agreement even if each of them is sufficient for its occurrence. The single power [is such that] movements occurring in separate loci are generated by a single power, since there is no evidence for the existence of more than that; and movements occurring in contiguous loci come from a power equal in number to the indivisible parts, and it is not permissible that they occur from a single power. That is, contiguous loci are combined loci; one power is not sufficient to carry them, but the objection follows them after granting its foundation on generation, by transferring the discussion to carrying its hundred combined parts. The most apparent in the exposition here is what al-Abhari said: 'And if they were to separate from this saying that the movement subsisting in two contiguous hundreds has a single locus, they would not be able to separate in the case of two separate hundreds.' Reflect. He said: (Some of them said etc.) the truth is that one should say: The carrying occurs by the totality of the two powers insofar as it is a totality, and neither of the two persons is independent in carrying in the manner that occurred by their agreement. The most that can be said is that each of them is independent in carrying it in general, as will come in the discussions of generation in theology, but the Mu'tazila did not say this, so the refutation was brought against them. The hundred: (And that one is a carrier of some part) similarly, so there are not established two acts in a single part of the carried object. And one cannot conceal the arbitrariness in it, because the act of each of them must be in some [?] specific in the very matter, and there is no way to that. For the relation of each part of the mentioned hundred to each of the two capable individuals is equal, so none of them is determined for the act of either one.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the second branch of a discussion on power (qudra), focusing on the case of two persons each capable of carrying a hundred-weight, attempting to carry it together. It is a doctrinal exposition of a Mu'tazilite dispute. First, the majority opinion holds that each power acts on every part of the weight, implying two independent powers on one object, which is deemed extremely unlikely. Second, the opinion of 'Abbad al-Dimayri and al-Ka'bi holds that each person carries a specific part, not shared. The commentary then addresses the first opinion: if each power is fully effective, then when they combine on an indivisible, either neither has an effect (absurd) or the effect is divided (forcing division of the indivisible). On the second opinion, the objection is that there is no way to specify which part belongs to which power, as all parts have equal relation to both. The commentator then presents al-Abhari's argument linking this to the generation (tawallud) of movements, and finally a reconciliation: the truth is that the carrying occurs by the combined powers as a totality, neither acting independently in that specific manner, though each could independently carry in general—but the Mu'tazila rejected this, hence the refutation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila believed two powers could act independently on one object.
Correction: In reality, the Mu'tazila were divided; some held that each power acts on every part (implying two independent powers on one object), while others insisted each power acts on a distinct part, avoiding the problem.

Checkpoint
According to the majority opinion among the Mu'tazila in this passage, what happens when two persons each capable of carrying 100 jointly carry a 100-weight?
A) Each person carries a specific part of the weight exclusively.
B) Every part is carried by both powers acting independently on every part.
C) The total weight is carried by a single combined power.
D) Only one of the two persons actually carries the weight.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila dispute on joint action illustrates the challenge of attributing a single effect to multiple independent causes, a key issue in kalām about divine and created agency.

The commentator says the truth is that the carrying occurs by the totality of the two powers. In your next act of worship—prayer, charity, or patience—will you consciously attribute your ability to Allah alone, acknowledging that your power is not independent but part of a greater totality? Name one specific prayer you will say to affirm this.
?
The minority opinion arbitrarily assigned parts to each power without justification. Do you ever assume credit for successes or blame for failures without seeing Allah's overarching power? The Rule of Single Power in Separate vs. Contiguous Loci
Al-Iji states the third branch and gives the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun argued that a single power can generate multiple movements in separate parts but requires distinct powers for contiguous parts.

Core Idea
A single power can move separate parts to different directions, but not contiguous parts, on pain of absurdity.

This lesson examines a key argument in the theory of generation (tawallud): how many powers are needed to move multiple objects? The passage presents a distinction between separate and contiguous loci, and uses a reductio ad absurdum to prove the necessity of multiple powers for adjacent parts. We will unpack the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage contrasts separate loci (e.g., objects struck by a hand and scattered) with contiguous loci (adjacent parts). Notice the ruling in each case.
Separate: one power can generate multiple movements. Contiguous: one power cannot.
2
Understand the proof for contiguous
The author argues that if a single power could move two adjacent parts, then it could move any number, leading to the absurd conclusion that a gnat could move a mountain. Trace this logical chain.
The key premise is that there is no priority among numbers.
3
Recognize the stakes
This argument is not about divine power but about human acquired power (kasb) and the theory of generation. It aims to show that the number of powers must match the number of contiguous parts moved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع ( الثالث : ) وهو مبني على تأثير القدرة الحادثة والتوليد أيضاً ( قالوا : القدرة الواحدة قد تولد في محال متفرقة حركات ) متعددة (إلى جهات مختلفة) فيجوز أن يحرك الشخص بقدرة واحدة جزءاً إلى جهة وجزءاً آخر إلى جهة أخرى وجزءاً ثالثاً إلى جهة ثالثة وهكذا بأن يضرب مثلاً يده عليها دفعة فتتفرق في تلك الجهات ( وأما في محال مجتمعة كأجزاء متلاصقة ( فلا يجوز أن تولد القدرة الواحدة فيها حركات متعددة بأن تتحرك معا إلى جهة واحدة ( بل يجتمع على عشرة أجزاء مجتمعة ) متلاصقة ) عشرة أجزاء من القدرة فالقدرة على تحريك كل جزء ) تلك العشرة المجتمعة ( غير القدرة على تحريك ( الجزء (الآخر) فيكون هناك عشر قدر إزاء عشرة أجزاء، وبالجملة يجب أن يكون عدد القدرة القائمة بالقادر على التحريك مساوياً لعدد الأجزاء المجتمعة ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم تكن القدرة على تحريك جزء غير القدرة على تحريك الجزء الآخر، بل جاز أن يكون القدرة على تحريك جزء قدرة على تحريك جزئين ( لكان ) أي تلك القدرة وذكرها بتأويل التمكن ) قدرة على تحريك الأجزاء بالغة ما بلغت ( إذ ليس عدد أولى من عدد فيلزم أن تقدر البقة على تحريك الجبل وهو باطل بالضرورة وقد عرفت بطلان عدم الأولوية. قال الآمدي : هذا الفرع مما اتفق عليه القائلون بالتوليد، وهو من قبيل من قوله : ( مختلقة) قيد اتفاقي فإن الأجزاء المتفرقة بعد الضرب يتحرك كل منها إلى جهة من الجهات الاعتبارية، وكذا وحدة الجهة المتلاصقة فإن تحركهما لتلاصقهما يكون إلى جهة واحدة. قوله : (قال الآمدي الخ) يعني أن كل واحد من الأجزاء واقعة بطريق التوليد بتوسط فعل واحد مباشر في الصورتين، وهو كضرب اليد مثلاً، فالقول بوقوعها في حال التفرق بقدرة واحدة وامتناعها حال الاجتماع تحكم والفرق الذي ذكروه من لزوم قدرة البقة على تحريك الجبل باطل لابتنائه على مقدمة باطلة . قوله : ( إلى جهات مختلفة) قيل : إنما خص الجهات المختلفة بالذكر لأن صدور الحركات إلى جهات مختلفة من القدرة الواحدة أبعد من صدورها إلى جهة واحدة لأنها أثر واحد فنبه على ذلك بطريق الأولى وقيل : بل لأنهم لا يجوزون توليد القدرة الواحدة حركات المحال تجویزهم المتفرقة إلى جهة واحدة .
The third branch: and it is built on the effect of the originated power and generation as well. They said: The single power may generate in separate loci multiple movements to different directions. So it is permissible for a person to move with a single power a part to a direction, another part to another direction, a third part to a third direction, and so on, for example by striking his hand upon them all at once so they scatter in those directions. But in contiguous loci, like adjacent parts, it is not permissible for a single power to generate multiple movements in them, whether they move together to a single direction. Rather, for ten adjacent parts, ten parts of power come together. So the power to move each of those ten contiguous parts is not the power to move the other part, so there are ten powers corresponding to ten parts. In general, the number of power subsisting in the capable agent for moving must equal the number of contiguous parts. Otherwise—i.e., if the power to move a part were not other than the power to move another part, but rather it were permissible that the power to move a part be a power to move two parts—then that power, mentioning it by the interpretation of capability, would be a power to move parts however many they be, since no number is more fitting than another. Then it would follow that a gnat [or soul?] would have power to move a mountain, which is necessarily false, and you have known the falsity of absence of priority. Al-Amidi said: This branch is among what those who affirm generation have agreed upon, and it is of the kind of... He said: (different) as an accidental qualification, for the separate parts after the strike each move to a direction of the conventional directions; similarly the unity of direction of contiguous parts, for their movement due to their adjacency is to one direction. He said: (Al-Amidi said etc.) meaning that each of the parts occurs by way of generation through the mediation of a single direct act in both cases, like the striking of the hand for example. So the statement that they occur in the state of separation by a single power and their impossibility in the state of conjunction is arbitrary. And the difference they mentioned—the necessity of a gnat's power to move a mountain—is false because it is based on a false premise. He said: (to different directions) It is said that he specified different directions because the occurrence of movements to different directions from a single power is more remote than their occurrence to one direction because it is a single effect, so he hinted at that in an a fortiori way. And it is said that it is because they do not permit a single power to generate movements of loci that they permit separate to a single direction.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the third branch, which builds on the theory of generation (tawallud) and the effect of originated power. It is an argument about the number of powers required for moving parts. First, the author states that a single power can generate multiple movements in separate loci (e.g., striking a hand on multiple objects causing them to scatter in different directions). This is allowed. However, for contiguous loci (adjacent parts), a single power cannot generate multiple movements. Instead, a separate power is needed for each contiguous part, so that the number of powers equals the number of parts. The reasoning is that if a single power could move two contiguous parts, then it could move any number of parts, leading to the absurd conclusion that a gnat could move a mountain. This is because there is no reason to prioritize one number over another (lack of 'firstness'). Al-Amidi then comments that this branch is agreed upon by proponents of generation, but he critiques it as arbitrary: since each part's movement occurs by generation through a single direct act (like a hand strike) in both cases, there is no principled difference. The distinction of different directions is noted: mentioning different directions emphasizes the oddity of multiple movements from one power, but some say it is because they do not permit a single power to generate movements to a single direction in separate loci.

محال متفرقة
محال مجتمعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is about God's power, implying a limitation on divine omnipotence.
Correction: The passage deals with human acquired power (kasb) and the theory of generation, not divine power. The argument is about how many human powers are needed to move contiguous parts.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why cannot a single power generate multiple movements in contiguous parts?
A) Because contiguous parts cannot move at all.
B) Because if a single power could move two contiguous parts, it could move any number, leading to absurdities like a gnat moving a mountain.
C) Because generation only works for separate loci.
D) Because the power must be infinitely multiplied.

Key Takeaway
A single human power can move separate parts to different directions, but contiguous parts require distinct powers for each part to avoid the absurd consequence of unlimited capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a single human power were not limited by Allah's design, it could move a mountain—do you live as if your own capacities are unlimited, relying on your own strength rather than His? In what specific area of your life today will you consciously acknowledge that your power is limited and that all ability comes from Allah?
?
The argument shows that without a divine ordering, a single power would claim to do everything. Where in your heart do you stubbornly assume that your own plans, efforts, or will are sufficient—ignoring that true power belongs to Allah alone? Will you repent of that arrogance and beg Him to grant you success in even the smallest act?
?
You know intellectually that a gnat cannot move a mountain—yet do you act as if your own abilities are limitless? When have you recently forgotten that even a single movement of your hand is only possible because Allah sustains it? What tangible step will you take this week to remind yourself that you are utterly reliant on Him for every motion and every breath?
