Second Proof: Nutrition, Growth, and Decay Without Pain
Second proof using nutrition, growth, and decay to argue discontinuity is not a direct cause of pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof uses the everyday experiences of nutrition, growth, and bodily decay to refute the claim that discontinuity is a direct cause of pain.

Core Idea
If separation directly caused pain, eating and growing would hurt—but they don't, so separation is not the direct cause.

The second proof against the argument that discontinuity is the direct cause of pain draws on ordinary bodily functions. Nutrition, growth, and natural decay all involve separation of connected parts, yet they occur without any sensation of pain. This passage walks through the reasoning, addresses two key objections, and clarifies what it means for something to be an essential cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the first part of the passage where it states that nutrition requires separation, yet no pain is felt. Notice how this is used to conclude that discontinuity is not painful by essence.
2
Extend the argument to growth and decay
Observe how the author adds growth and decomposition as further examples where separation occurs without pain, reinforcing the point.
3
Examine the first objection and response
Read the objection that the separations are too small to cause pain. Then see how the response argues that since these separations are pervasive across all parts, they would cause pain if separation were inherently painful.
Think about why smallness alone might not be a sufficient excuse if the effect is supposed to be essential.
4
Consider the second objection about habituation
The objection claims continuous pain could go unnoticed. The response redefines pain as something that is sensed and argues that if it is not sensed while senses are sound, it is not pain.
Also note the distinction between perception as harmful (munafir) and suitable (mula'im).
5
Reflect on the meaning of 'essential cause'
The commentary clarifies that an essential cause does not require an intermediate cause, but may still have conditions. This helps understand why the argument works.

النوع الخامس - المقصد الأول : اللذة والألم من بقية الكيفيات النفسانية
واحتج أيضاً على ذلك بأن التغذي مداخلة الغذاء لجميع الأجزاء ولا تتصور هذه المداخلة إلا بتفريق فيما بين الأجزاء فالغذاء إنما يصير جزءاً من المغتذي بالفعل بأن يفرق اتصال أجزاء المغتذي ويتوسط بينها ويتشبه بها والاغتذاء حاصل لأكثر أجزاء المغتذي في أكثر الأوقات فيكون التفرق أيضاً حاصلاً لأكثر الأجزاء في أكثر الأوقات فيجب أن يألم المتغذي وليس كذلك لأن المتغذي لا يجد ألماً أصلاً فلا يكون التفرق مؤلماً بالذات وكذا نقول إن النمو لا يحصل إلا بتفرق الاتصال مع أنه غير مؤلم بل نقول أن إعضاء البدن لا شك أنها دائماً في التحلل ولا معنى إلا أن ينفصل عن العضو ما كان متصلاً به وليس هذا التحلل مختصاً بظاهر العضو دون باطنه وذلك لأن المحلل هو الحرارة السارية في ظاهر العضو وباطنه فيكون تفرق الاتصال شاملاً لظواهره وأعماقه مع أنه لا ألم فيه فإن قيل التفرق الحاصل من التغذي والنمو والتحلل تفرق في أجزاء صغيرة جداً فلصغر هذا التفرق لم يحصل الألم قلنا إن كل واحد من تلك التفرقات وإن كان صغيراً جداً إلا أن تلك التفرقات كثيرة جداً لأن هذه الأمور الموجبة للتفرق لا تختص بجزء من البدن دون جزء بل هي حاصلة في جميع الأجزاء فالتفرق الناشئ منها يعم الأجزاء كلها فلو كان مؤلماً بالذات لعم الألم الأعضاء بأسرها لا يقال تلك التفرقات مؤلمة إلا أن آلامها لما استمرت لم يحس بها كسائر الكيفيات المستمرة لأنا نقول لا نعني بالألم إلا المعنى المخصوص الذي يجده الحي من نفسه فإذا لم يحس به مع سلامة الحس كسوء المزاج المتصف بالألم فيجوز أن يكون مشروطاً بشرط لا يوجد فيما نحن فيه كالشعور ويؤلم فإنما يوجب الألم ولو كان مدركاً من حيث أنه منافر وفيما نحن فيه ليس كذلك فإنه مدرك من حيث أنه ملائم لكونه منقياً للبدن وموصلاً إلى كماله ودافعاً للفضلات قوله قلنا إلخ فيه أن التفرقات وإن كانت كثيرة بحسب العدد صغيرة بالمقدار غير مؤلم كل واحد منها في موضعه فلا يكون الكل مؤلماً
قوله بأن التغذي مداخلة الغذاء أي لجميع الأجزاء أي لجميع أجزاء المتغذي به فلا ينافيه قول الشارح لأكثر أجزاء المتغذي على ما قيده بقوله في أكثر الأوقات وقد يجاب عن هذا وعن قوله فإن من عقر إلخ بأن المراد بالسبب الذاتي ما لا يحتاج إلى سبب متوسط بينه وبين المسبب فجاز أن يكون مشروطاً بشرط يتخلف عنه المسبب بفقده
قوله إلا أن تلك التفرقات كثيرة جدا إلخ قيل التفرق الحاصل في الأجزاء بالاغتذاء والنماء وإن كان كثيراً لكنه متصغر فلا يؤلم وكثرة التفرقات لا اعتبار لها لأن حاسة عضو إذا لم تدرك ألماً لتصغر التفرق لم يدركه حاسة عضو آخر لذلك فتأمل.
Fifth type - First intention: Pleasure and pain from the remaining psychic qualities. And he also argued against that by saying that nutrition is the interpenetration of food into all parts, and this interpenetration is not conceivable except by a separation among the parts. So the food only actually becomes part of the nourished thing by separating the connection of the parts of the nourished thing, and interposing between them, and becoming similar to them. And nutrition occurs in most parts of the nourished thing in most times, so discontinuity also occurs in most parts in most times. Thus the nourished thing must feel pain, but it is not so because the nourished thing does not find any pain at all. Therefore discontinuity is not painful by essence. And likewise we say that growth does not occur except by discontinuity of connection, while it is not painful. Rather we say that the organs of the body are undoubtedly always in decomposition, and it means nothing but that what was connected to the organ separates from it. And this decomposition is not specific to the exterior of the organ without its interior, because the decomposer is the heat penetrating in the exterior and interior of the organ, so discontinuity of connection includes its surfaces and depths, while there is no pain in it. If it is said: The discontinuity resulting from nutrition, growth, and decomposition is a discontinuity in very small parts, and due to the smallness of this discontinuity, pain does not occur. We say: Each one of those discontinuities, even if very small, yet those discontinuities are very many, because these things causing discontinuity are not specific to one part of the body to the exclusion of another, rather they occur in all parts. So the discontinuity arising from them encompasses all parts. Thus if it were painful by essence, pain would encompass all organs entirely. It should not be said: Those discontinuities are painful, but because their pains are continuous, they are not sensed, like other continuous qualities. For we say: We do not mean by pain except the specific meaning that the living being finds in itself. So if it is not sensed while the sense is sound, like the bad temperament characterized by pain, then it is permissible that it be conditional on a condition that does not exist in our case, such as consciousness. And it is painful, but it only necessitates pain if it is perceived as harmful, whereas in our case it is not so, because it is perceived as suitable, since it cleanses the body, leads to its perfection, and expels waste. His saying: 'We say, etc.' There is in it that the discontinuities, even if many in number, are small in magnitude, each one of them is not painful in its place, so the whole is not painful. His saying: 'that nutrition is interpenetration of food' i.e., into all parts, i.e., all parts of the nourished thing; thus it does not contradict the commentator's saying 'for most parts of the nourished thing' as he restricted it by his saying 'in most times'. And it may be answered to this and to his saying 'for one who is wounded, etc.' that what is meant by essential cause is that which does not need an intermediate cause between it and the effect, so it is permissible that it be conditional on a condition in whose absence the effect is absent. His saying: 'except that those discontinuities are very many, etc.' It was said: The discontinuity occurring in the parts by nutrition and growth, even if it is many, yet it is extremely small, so it does not cause pain; and the multiplicity of discontinuities is not considered, because the sense of an organ, if it does not perceive pain due to the smallness of the discontinuity, the sense of another organ will not perceive it either; so reflect.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that discontinuity is not a direct cause of pain, based on everyday bodily processes. The author argues that nutrition, growth, and natural decomposition all involve discontinuous separations of parts, yet we do not feel any pain during these processes. Therefore, discontinuity cannot be inherently painful. The argument proceeds step by step: nutrition requires food to interpenetrate parts, necessitating separation; this happens frequently, so if separation were painful, we would always be in pain, which we are not. Similarly, growth and decay involve constant small separations without pain. An objection is raised that these separations are too small to cause pain. The response is that they are numerous and pervasive, covering all organs, so if they were painful by essence, the entire body would feel pain. Another objection suggests that such constant pains are simply unnoticed due to habituation. The author counters that pain is defined as something sensed; if it is not sensed while the sense is sound, it is not pain. Moreover, the pain would require perception of harm, but these processes are beneficial (cleansing, growth, etc.), so they are perceived as suitable, not harmful. The commentary then clarifies that the essential cause does not require an intermediate cause and may be conditional, and that the smallness of each individual discontinuity prevents perception, and multiplicity does not change that because sense perception is local.

Misconception: The absence of pain in daily processes proves discontinuity is never a cause.
Correction: The text uses it to disprove direct causation, not indirect. Discontinuity may still be a cause under certain conditions.

Checkpoint
Why does the second argument conclude that discontinuity is not a direct cause of pain?

B) Because nutrition and growth involve discontinuity but no pain.
C) Because discontinuity only occurs in external organs.
D) Because pain is always present but unnoticed.

Key Takeaway
The absence of pain in nutrition, growth, and decay demonstrates that discontinuity is not an essential cause of pain, since an essential cause would produce pain whenever it occurs.

?
The lesson argues that even constant, small separations in the body do not cause pain because they are perceived as suitable. Where in your life do you endure small 'separations' (disappointments, frustrations) that you have come to accept as normal? Is that a sign of health, or have you become numb to what should be painful? What would it mean to truly sense them?

Why Separation Doesn't Always Cause Pain
Refutes intrinsic pain from separation using nutrition, growth, dissolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how everyday biological processes like nutrition, growth, and dissolution serve as counterexamples to the claim that separation of continuity is the intrinsic cause of pain.

Core Idea
Separation of bodily parts occurs constantly without pain—so separation cannot be the real cause of pain.

This passage challenges a common assumption about pain: that it is caused by the separation of continuous parts. The author presents three ordinary bodily processes—nutrition, growth, and dissolution—where such separation happens constantly, yet without any sensation of pain. By examining these counterexamples, we see that separation is not intrinsically painful, and pain must arise from something else, such as the perception of something repugnant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three processes
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that nutrition, growth, and dissolution are given as examples where separation occurs without pain.
These are everyday biological processes, not injuries.
2
Understand why each process involves separation
Nutrition requires food to interpose between parts, breaking continuity. Growth similarly involves expansion with separation. Dissolution is the constant loss of parts from the body.
The author argues that these are constant and widespread.
3
Consider the objection about smallness
The objection says that these separations are too tiny to cause pain. The passage answers that they are also many and cover all parts, so if separation were painful, the whole body would be in constant pain.
4
Reflect on the final refinement
The passage concludes that pain is only caused when separation is perceived as repugnant (munāfir), not when it is agreeable (mulāʾim) as in these processes.
This shows that the real cause is not separation per se, but the quality of perception.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج أيضاً على ذلك بأن التغذي مداخلة الغذاء لجميع الأجزاء، ولا تتصور هذه المداخلة إلا بتفريق فيما بين الأجزاء، فالغذاء إنما يصير جزءا من المغتذي بالفعل، بأن يفرق اتصال أجزاء المغتذي ويتوسط بينها ويتشبه بها والاغتذاء حاصل لأكثر أجزاء المغتذي في أكثر الأوقات، فيكون التفرق أيضاً حاصلاً لأكثر الأجزاء في أكثر الأوقات فيجب أن يؤلم المتغذي وليس كذلك لأن المتغذي لا يجد ألماً أصلاً، فلا يكون التفرق مؤلماً بالذات وكذا نقول : إن النمو لا يحصل إلا بتفرق الاتصال مع أنه غير مؤلم بل نقول: أن إعضاء البدن لا شك أنها دائماً في التحلل، ولا معنى إلا أن ينفصل عن العضو ما كان متصلاً به وليس هذا التحلل مختصاً بظاهر العضو دون باطنه، وذلك لأن المحلل هو الحرارة السارية في ظاهر العضو وباطنه، فيكون تفرق الاتصال شاملاً لظواهره وأعماقه مع أنه لا ألم فيه فإن قيل: التفرق الحاصل من التغذي والنمو والتحلل تفرق في أجزاء صغيرة جداً، فلصغر هذا التفرق لم يحصل الألم قلنا : إن كل واحد من تلك التفرقات وإن كان صغيراً جداً، إلا أن تلك التفرقات كثيرة جداً، لأن هذه الأمور الموجبة للتفرق لا تختص بجزء من البدن دون جزء، بل هي حاصلة في جميع الأجزاء فالتفرق الناشئ منها يعم الأجزاء كلها، فلو كان مؤلما بالذات لعم الألم الأعضاء بأسرها، لا يقال : تلك التفرقات مؤلمة إلا أن آلامها لما استمرت لم يحس بها كسائر الكيفيات المستمرة لأنا نقول : لا نعني بالألم إلا المعنى المخصوص الذي يجده الحي من نفسه، فإذا لم يحس به مع سلامة الحس كسوء المزاج المتصف بالألم، فيجوز أن يكون مشروطاً بشرط لا يوجد فيما نحن فيه كالشعور ويؤلم، فإنما يوجب الألم، ولو كان مدركاً من حيث أنه منافر، وفيما نحن فيه ليس كذلك فإنه مدرك من حيث أنه ملائم لكونه منقياً للبدن وموصلاً إلى كماله ودافعاً للفضلات.
And he also argued for that by saying: nutrition is the interpenetration of food into all parts, and this interpenetration is inconceivable except by a separation among the parts. Food only becomes a part of the nourished actually by breaking the connection of the parts of the nourished, interposing between them, and becoming similar to them. Nutrition occurs for most parts of the nourished in most times, so separation also occurs for most parts in most times. Thus it would necessarily pain the nourished, but it does not, because the nourished feels no pain at all. So separation is not painful intrinsically. And likewise we say: growth only occurs by separation of connection, yet it is not painful. Rather we say: the body’s limbs are undoubtedly always in dissolution, and this means nothing other than that what was connected to the limb becomes separated from it. This dissolution is not restricted to the exterior of the limb to the exclusion of its interior, because the dissolver is the heat permeating the exterior and interior of the limb. Thus the separation of connection includes its exteriors and depths, yet there is no pain in it. If it is said: the separation from nutrition, growth, and dissolution is a separation into very small parts; because of the smallness of this separation, pain does not occur. We say: each one of those separations, though very small, yet those separations are very many, because these causes of separation are not confined to one part of the body to the exclusion of another, but occur in all parts. So the separation arising from them encompasses all parts. So if it were painful intrinsically, pain would encompass all the limbs entirely. It may not be said: those separations are painful, but because their pains are continuous, they are not sensed, like other continuous qualities. For we say: we only mean by pain the specific meaning that the living being finds in itself. If it is not sensed while the sense is sound, like the bad temperament that is described as painful, then it is possible that it is conditioned by a condition not present in our case, such as feeling and being painful. For it only causes pain if it is perceived as repugnant. In our case it is not so, because it is perceived as agreeable, since it purifies the body, leads to its perfection, and expels waste.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the claim that separation of continuity (tafarruq al-ittisal) is the intrinsic cause of pain. The author presents three everyday biological processes as counterexamples: nutrition (taghadhdhi), growth (numuww), and dissolution (tahallul). In each case, separation of parts occurs constantly without pain. The objection that the separations are too small to be felt is answered by noting their multitude and ubiquity: they cover all parts, so if separation were intrinsically painful, the whole body would be in constant pain. The further objection that continuous pain goes unnoticed is refuted by defining pain as a specific sensation: if it is not sensed at all despite a sound faculty, it is not present. Moreover, the separations in these processes are perceived as agreeable (mulāʾim) because they serve beneficial purposes like purification and growth, whereas pain is only caused by what is perceived as repugnant (munāfir). Thus, separation is not the real cause of pain.

Bad Temperament as True Cause of Pain and Its Classification
Argues that pain is caused by bad temperament, not separation, and classifies bad temperament.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic philosophers considered bad temperament, not separation, as the proximate cause of pain, and how Ibn Sina classified bad temperament into consistent and varying types.

Core Idea
Pain stems from bad temperament, not separation; classification reveals nuances.

In this passage, the commentator defends the view that the real cause of pain is a disturbance in the body's temperament, not merely a tearing of tissue. He responds to an objection based on common experience and provides a deeper philosophical argument about the role of preparedness and emanation. Finally, Ibn Sina's comprehensive classification of bad temperament is introduced.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument against separation
Read the opening objection that separation is painful and the response that separation only leads to bad temperament, which is the real cause. Notice how the author avoids making a non-existent cause of an existent.
2
Understand the philosophical argument from preparedness
Move to the argument from al-Mulakhkhaṣ: all sublunar qualities come from a universal giver of forms, and a body's temperament provides the specific preparedness. Therefore temperament, not separation, is the proximate cause of pleasure and pain.
This argument is presented as stronger and binding.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's classification of bad temperament
Read Ibn Sina's addition: two proximate causes (separation and bad temperament). Then focus on the classification of bad temperament into consistent (muttafiq) and varying (mukhtalif). Note that consistent bad temperament can become painless.
The passage cuts off but the classification is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتوجه إلى إدراكه دل على عدمه قطعاً، فإن قيل : الحس شاهد بأن تفرق الاتصال مؤلم قلنا : تفرق الاتصال يستعقب سوء المزاج الذي هو المؤلم بالذات، فإن اختلاط العناصر لما زال بالتفرق عاد طبيعة كل منها إلى اقتضاء الكيفية الخارجة عن الاعتدال، فالفاعل للمزاج السيء هو طبائعها لا التفرق العدمي، فلا يلزمنا جعل العدمي سبباً للوجودي واحتج في الملخص بوجه آخر إلزامي، وهو أن الفلاسفة متفقون على أن الكيفيات والصور الحادثة في الأجسام التي تحت كرة القمر، إنما تحدث عن مبدأ عام الفيض، وإنما تختلف الأعراض، والصور في تلك الأجسام لاختلافها في الاستعداد فالجسم المركب يختص بصورة أو كيفية لأن مزاجه أفاده استعداداً لقبول تلك الصورة أو الكيفية من واهب الصور فعلى هذا يكون السبب القريب للذة، والألم ثبوتاً وانتفاء هو المزاج لا التفرق وزاد ابن سينا للألم سبباً آخر فقال : السبب القريب للألم أمران: أحدهما هو تفرق الاتصال على ما ذكره جالينوس، وثانيهما : هو سوء المزاج وهو على قسمين متفق ومختلف فالمتفق مزاج غير طبيعي يرد على العضو ويزيل مزاجه الطبيعي ويتمكن فيه بحيث يصير كأنه المزاج الطبيعي والمختلف مزاج غير طبيعي يرد عليه ولا يبطل مزاجه الطبيعي بل يخرجه عن.
And attention to its perception indicates its non-existence definitively. For if it is said: sensation testifies that separation of connection is painful. We say: separation of connection entails bad temperament (sūʾ al-mizāj), which is the intrinsically painful thing. For when the mixture of elements is removed by separation, the nature of each of them returns to requiring the quality outside of moderation. So the agent of the bad temperament is their natures, not the non-existent separation. Thus we are not forced to make a non-existent a cause of an existent. And he argued in al-Mulakhkhaṣ with another argument of the binding type: the philosophers agree that the qualities and forms occurring in the bodies under the sphere of the moon arise only from a universal principle of emanation. The accidents and forms differ in those bodies only due to their different preparedness. So a composite body is distinguished by a form or quality because its temperament gives it readiness to accept that form or quality from the giver of forms. Accordingly, the proximate cause of pleasure and pain, in existence and non-existence, is temperament, not separation. And Ibn Sīnā added another cause for pain, saying: the proximate cause of pain is two things: one is separation of connection as mentioned by Galen, and the second is bad temperament, which is of two types: the consistent (muttafiq) and the varying (mukhtalif). The consistent is a non-natural temperament that comes upon the organ, removes its natural temperament, and settles in it so that it becomes as if it were the natural temperament. The varying is a non-natural temperament that comes upon it but does not nullify its natural temperament; rather it removes it from.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents a further argument that the real cause of pain is bad temperament (sūʾ al-mizāj), not separation. The author begins by stating that the perception of separation as painful is definitively absent. An objection that sensation testifies to the painfulness of separation is answered: separation leads to bad temperament by causing the elements to separate and revert to their extreme qualities, and it is the resulting imbalance that is painful, not the separation itself. This avoids making a non-existent (the separation) a cause of an existent (pain). Then a second, binding argument is borrowed from al-Mulakhkhaṣ: philosophers hold that all qualities and forms in the sublunar world come from a universal giver of forms, and the body's temperament provides the specific preparedness (istiʿdād) to receive a particular form or quality. Therefore, the proximate cause of both pleasure and pain is temperament, not separation. Finally, Ibn Sīnā is quoted as combining both causes: separation (from Galen) and bad temperament. Bad temperament is then classified into two types: muttafiq (consistent), which replaces the natural temperament and becomes habitual (hence often painless), and mukhtalif (varying), which conflicts with the natural temperament without replacing it, causing pain due to the perceived opposition. The passage ends mid-sentence but the classification is clear.

Refuting Direct Causation of Pain by Separation
The author refutes the objection by attributing pain to bad temperament from separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the claim that separation of continuity directly causes pain, and his argument that pain is caused by the resulting bad temperament.

Core Idea
Pain is not from separation itself but from the bad temperament it causes.

In this passage, the author addresses a common objection from sense perception: that cutting or separating a limb is painful, implying that separation itself is the cause. He argues that separation only leads to bad temperament (sū' al-mizāj), which is the real cause. The discussion explores the role of elemental natures and the process of healing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the objection-and-answer structure.
The passage includes a main objection and a sub-objection.
2
Identify the objection and author's answer
Locate the objector's claim that sense testifies separation is painful, and the author's reply that separation leads to bad temperament.
The author explains why separation cannot be a direct cause.
3
Understand the further analysis
Consider the sub-objection about a severed limb and how the author responds, noting the reflective conclusion.
This deepens the causality argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتوجه إلى إدراكه دل على عدمه قطعاً، فإن قيل : الحس شاهد بأن تفرق الاتصال مؤلم قلنا : تفرق الاتصال يستعقب سوء المزاج الذي هو المؤلم بالذات، فإنَّ اختلاط العناصر لما زال بالتفرق عاد طبيعة كل منها إلى اقتضاء الكيفية الخارجة عن الاعتدال، فالفاعل للمزاج السيء هو طبائعها لا التفرق العدمي، فلا يلزمنا جعل العدمي سبباً للوجودي. قوله : (عاد طبيعة كل منها إلخ ) إذ لم يبق الاجتماع الذي كان حافظاً للمركب ومانعاً عما اقتضته طبائع العناصر. قوله : ( لما زال بالتفرق عاد طبيعة كل منها إلخ ) فإن قلت : العضو المقطوع المنفصل وإن اشتمل على العناصر، إلا أن الباقي أيضاً يشتمل على العناصر المختلطة، فكيف يعود طبيعة كل واحد منها إلى ما ذكر قلت : يجوز أن يكون للعناصر المنفصلة بالقطع مدخل في المزاج المخصوص الحاصل لما يجاور موضع القطع لكن هل يعود المزاج المعتدل له عند البرء أولا، بل إنما لا يحس بالألم لاستمرار المزاج السيء فيه تأمل والظاهر هو الأول وإن كان لا يخلو عن مناقشة، فتأمل.
And the focus on perceiving it indicates its non-existence definitively. For if it is said: Sense testifies that separation of continuity (tafarruq al-ittisāl) is painful, we say: Separation of continuity leads to bad temperament (sū' al-mizāj) which is intrinsically painful, because when the mixture of the elements is removed by separation, the nature of each returns to demanding the quality outside moderation. So the agent of the bad temperament is their natures, not the non-existent separation. So we are not forced to make the non-existent a cause of the existent. His saying: '(عاد طبيعة كل منها etc.)' means: because the union that was preserving the composite and preventing what the natures of the elements demanded has ceased. His saying: '( لما زال بالتفرق عاد طبيعة كل منها etc.)' - if you say: The severed separated limb, although it contains the elements, yet the remaining part also contains the mixed elements. So how does the nature of each return to what was mentioned? I say: It is possible that the elements separated by the cut have a role in the specific temperament that occurs adjacent to the cut site. But does the balanced temperament return to it upon healing or not? Rather, it does not feel pain due to the continuation of the bad temperament in it. Reflect, and the apparent is the first, although it is not free from discussion. So reflect.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector argues that sense perception shows that separation of continuity (e.g., cutting) is painful. The author replies that separation does not directly cause pain; rather, it leads to bad temperament (sū' al-mizāj), which is the real cause. The explanation: when the elements are separated, their natural tendencies return, resulting in a quality outside the balanced state (al-iktidāl). Thus, the cause is the natural disposition of the elements, not the separation itself, which is a non-entity. The commentary elaborates on two points: first, that the union that maintained the composite has been lost, allowing the elements' natures to act; second, a further objection about a severed limb: how does the nature of each element return? The answer is that the separated elements may affect the temperament near the cut, but the pain may persist due to continued bad temperament. The passage concludes with a reflective note.

تفرق الاتصال (tafarruq al-ittisāl)
سوء المزاج (sū' al-mizāj)
المزاج (al-mizāj)
الكيفية الخارجة عن الاعتدال (al-kayfiyya al-khārija 'an al-i'tidāl)
العدم (al-'adam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Separation of continuity directly causes pain.
Correction: Pain is caused by the bad temperament resulting from separation, not by the separation itself.

Checkpoint

A) The separation of continuity itself
B) The bad temperament resulting from separation
C) The loss of the limb
D) The natural tendency of elements

Key Takeaway
Pain from separation is not caused by the separation itself, but by the bad temperament it produces through the natural tendencies of the elements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson teaches that pain is not from the separation itself but from the resulting imbalance. What imbalances in your spiritual temperament might be the real source of your suffering? Commit to examining one recurring pain in your life and asking Allah to reveal its root.
?
The author argues that a non-existent thing cannot be a cause. Think about your regrets over what you lack—good health, wealth, relationships. Are you treating these non-existences as if they have power over your happiness? How can you shift your focus to what exists—the blessings and the nature Allah has given you—and let that define your emotional reality? Write down one thing you have been mourning as 'missing' that is actually a non-entity, and thank Allah for what He has given.

Temperament as Immediate Cause of Pain
A second argument from the philosophers that temperament is the immediate cause of pain via preparedness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that temperament (mizāj), not separation, is the immediate cause of pain because it determines preparedness to receive forms from the universal emanation.

Core Idea
Pain's immediate cause is temperament, not separation—according to philosophical consensus.

The author presents a second philosophical argument to shift the burden of proof onto the opponent. This argument builds on premises accepted by the philosophers: that all sublunar forms come from a universal principle via the preparedness of bodies. Therefore, temperament—which gives preparedness—is the immediate cause of pleasure and pain, not separation. Read carefully to see the logical structure and the commentator's critical note about the need for proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Note that this is an 'ilzāmī argument—it forces the opponent to accept the conclusion based on their own premises.
The argument is from the philosophers, not the author himself.
2
Identify the key claim
Find where the author states the conclusion: that the immediate cause of pleasure and pain is temperament, not separation (tafarruq).
This counters the earlier view that separation causes pain.
3
Understand the premises
The philosophers agree that all qualities and forms in the sublunar world come from a universal principle of emanation. Their diversity is due to differences in preparedness of the receiving bodies.
Preparedness (isti'dād) is a key concept here.
4
Notice the critical remark
The commentator points out that the argument requires proof that temperament actually gives preparedness. This is not self-evident and leaves the argument open to challenge.
This shows the author is presenting but not necessarily endorsing the argument.
5
Reflect on the argument's force
Think about why this argument is 'ilzāmī: it uses premises the opponent (the philosophers) accept to draw a conclusion that might be uncomfortable for them. How does this advance the discussion?
The argument forces the opponent to either accept that temperament is the cause or provide a counter-explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج في الملخص بوجه آخر إلزامي، وهو أنَّ الفلاسفة متفقون على أن الكيفيات والصور الحادثة في الأجسام التي تحت كرة القمر، إنما تحدث عن مبدأ عام الفيض، وإنما تختلف الأعراض، والصور في تلك الأجسام لاختلافها في الاستعداد فالجسم المركب يختص بصورة أو كيفية لأنَّ مزاجه أفاده استعداداً لقبول تلك الصورة أو الكيفية من واهب الصور فعلى هذا يكون السبب القريب للذة، والألم ثبوتاً وانتفاء هو المزاج لا التفرق. قوله : ( لأن مزاجه إلخ) لا يخفى أنَّ اللازم وما ذكر أن يكون كل صورة وكيفية للأجسام العنصرية بواسطة استعدادها، وأما أن مزاجه أفاد ذلك الاستعداد فلا بد له من دليل .
And he argued in al-Mulakhkhaṣ with another obligatory argument, which is that the philosophers agree that the qualities and forms that occur in bodies under the sphere of the moon only occur from a universal principle of emanation (mabda' 'āmm al-fayḍ), and the accidents and forms differ in those bodies due to their difference in preparedness (isti'dād). So a composite body is characterized by a form or quality because its temperament (mizāj) gave it a preparedness to accept that form or quality from the Giver of forms (wāhib al-ṣuwar). Accordingly, the immediate cause (sabab qarīb) of pleasure and pain, in existence and non-existence, is temperament, not separation. His saying: '( because its temperament etc.)' - it is not hidden that the necessary thing is what is mentioned: that every form and quality of elemental bodies comes through their preparedness. But that its temperament provided that preparedness requires a proof.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument (this time from the philosophers) to show that temperament is the immediate cause of pain. The argument is 'ilzāmī, meaning it compels the opponent based on premises they accept. The philosophers hold that all qualities and forms in the sublunar world come from a universal principle of emanation. Their diversity in bodies is due to differing preparedness (isti'dād) of those bodies. Therefore, a body's temperament gives it a specific preparedness to receive a particular form or quality from the Giver of forms (wāhib al-ṣuwar). Consequently, the immediate cause of pleasure and pain is temperament, not separation. The commentary then notes that this reasoning requires a proof that the temperament indeed gives the preparedness, which may not be self-evident.

Key Terms
مبدأ عام الفيض (mabda' 'āmm al-fayḍ)
الاستعداد (al-isti'dād)
واهب الصور (wāhib al-ṣuwar)
السبب القريب (al-sabab al-qarīb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the immediate cause of pain is the physical separation (tafarruq) of continuity in the body.
Correction: The philosophers argue that the immediate cause is temperament (mizāj), which gives the body the preparedness to feel pain from the universal emanation.

Checkpoint

B) The Giver of forms
C) Temperament via preparedness
D) The universal principle of emanation

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that temperament is the immediate cause of pleasure and pain because it determines the body's preparedness to receive forms from the universal emanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ibn Sina's Two Causes of Pain and Bad Temperament Classification
Ibn Sina enumerates two immediate causes of pain and classifies bad temperament into conforming and differing, with a note on active vs. passive qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's two immediate causes of pain and the distinction between conforming and differing bad temperaments, including which qualities are directly painful.

Core Idea
Pain arises from either separation of continuity or a bad temperament that opposes the natural state.

Ibn Sina expands on Galen's theory of pain by adding a second cause: bad temperament. He then divides bad temperament into two types based on how it interacts with the organ's natural temperament, and further distinguishes active from passive qualities to explain why not all bad temperaments are painful. This passage lays out a nuanced classification that refines our understanding of physical pain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two immediate causes of pain
Read the passage and note that Ibn Sina lists two immediate causes: separation of continuity (from Galen) and bad temperament (his addition).
The second cause is further subdivided.
2
Distinguish conforming and differing bad temperament
Understand the two types: conforming (muttāfiq) gradually replaces the natural temperament and is unfelt; differing (mukhtalif) opposes the natural temperament without replacing it, thus is felt.
The commentary clarifies that differing is not always painful.
3
Differentiate active vs. passive qualities
Learn that heat and cold per se (active qualities) cause pain directly, while wetness and dryness (passive qualities) do not; only dryness may cause pain indirectly.
This explains why not all differing bad temperaments are painful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزاد ابن سينا للألم ( سبباً آخر) فقال : السبب القريب للألم أمران: أحدهما هو تفرق الاتصال على ما ذكره جالينوس، (و) ثانيهما : ( هو سوء المزاج وهو على قسمين متفق ومختلف فالمتفق مزاج غير طبيعي يرد على العضو ويزيل مزاجه الطبيعي ويتمكن فيه بحيث يصير كأنه المزاج الطبيعي والمختلف مزاج غير طبيعي يرد عليه ولا يبطل مزاجه الطبيعي بل يخرجه عن الاعتدال. قوله : ( فالمتفق مزاج غير طبيعي وهو إنما يتمكن في العضو بتدريج ولذا لا يحس به. قوله : ( والمختلف إلخ) يعني أنَّ للأعضاء في جواهرها مزاجاً يعرض عليها مزاج غريب مضاد لذلك، حتى يكون أسخن من ذلك أو أبرد فتحس القوة الحاسة لورود ذلك المنافي فيتألم لكن كل سوء مزاج مختلف لا يكون مؤلماً بل الحار بالذات والبارد بالذات واليابس بالعرض والرطب بالعرض لا يؤلم البتة، لأنَّ الحار والبارد كيفيتان فعليتان والرطب واليابس كيفيتان انفعالیتان قوامهما ليس بأن يؤثر جسم في جسم، بل بأن يتأثر جسم وأما اليابس فإنما يؤلم
And Ibn Sina added for pain (another cause) and said: The immediate cause of pain is two things: one of them is the separation of continuity according to what Galen mentioned, and the second is bad temperament, and it is of two types: conforming (muttāfiq) and differing (mukhtalif). The conforming is an unnatural temperament that comes upon the organ and removes its natural temperament and establishes itself in it so that it becomes as if it were the natural temperament. The differing is an unnatural temperament that comes upon it and does not eliminate its natural temperament but removes it from moderation. His saying: '(the conforming is an unnatural temperament)' - it only becomes established in the organ gradually, and therefore it is not felt. His saying: '(and the differing etc.)' means that the organs in their substances have a temperament, and a strange temperament contrary to that comes upon it, so that it becomes hotter than that or colder, so the sensory faculty feels the occurrence of that opposite and feels pain. But not every differing bad temperament is painful; rather, the hot per se (al-ḥār bi-dhāt) and the cold per se, and the dry accidentally (al-yābis bi-l-'araḍ) and the wet accidentally do not cause pain at all. Because heat and cold are active qualities (kayfiyyat fi'liyya), while wetness and dryness are passive qualities (kayfiyyat infi'āliyya), whose essence is not that a body acts on a body, but that a body is acted upon. As for the dry, it causes pain
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's classification of the causes of pain. He adds a second cause to Galen's separation of continuity: bad temperament (sū' al-mizāj). He then divides bad temperament into two types: conforming (muttāfiq) and differing (mukhtalif). The conforming type gradually replaces the natural temperament so completely that it becomes like a second nature, and thus is not felt. The differing type, however, is an unnatural temperament that opposes the natural one without completely eliminating it; it maintains a contrast, causing the sensory faculty to perceive it and thus feel pain. The commentary then clarifies a nuance: not every differing bad temperament is painful; specifically, only heat and cold per se are active and cause pain directly, whereas wetness and dryness are passive qualities and only cause pain indirectly (the dry does so because it may lead to separation).

Key Terms
المتفق (al-muttāfiq)
المختلف (al-mukhtalif)
كيفيتان فعليتان (kayfiyyatān fi'liyyatān)
كيفيتان انفعاليتان (kayfiyyatān infi'āliyyatān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All differing bad temperaments cause pain equally.
Correction: Only those involving heat or cold per se (active qualities) directly cause pain; wetness and dryness do not.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, which type of bad temperament causes direct pain?
A) The conforming type
B) The differing type involving heat or cold per se
C) The differing type involving wetness or dryness
D) Both types equally

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's classification of pain causes shows that pain results from either physical separation or a qualitatively opposing temperament, and only active qualities directly cause pain.

?
Ibn Sina says only active qualities (hot/cold) cause pain directly, while passive ones do not. In your life, are you often passive, letting circumstances shape you without acting? Where do you need to take initiative in your relationship with Allah before the pain of regret becomes unbearable?
?
The differing bad temperament creates a contrast that the soul feels as pain. When you sin, do you still feel that pain of opposition to your fitra, or have you numbed your heart? What concrete step will you take today to restore that sensitivity?

Differing Bad Temperament as the Cause of Pain
Proof using scorpion sting and fever to show differing bad temperament causes pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the scorpion sting and fever examples demonstrate that pain is caused by a differing bad temperament, not merely by separation of continuity.

Core Idea
Pain arises from a change in temperament, not just from physical separation.

In this passage, the author presents two compelling arguments to prove that pain is due to a change in the humoral balance (differing bad temperament) rather than mere tissue separation. First, he compares the sting of a scorpion to a needle prick and a large wound. Then he contrasts two types of fevers to show that the intensity and persistence of pain correlate with the stability of the harmful temperament. A brief objection and clarification follow, reinforcing the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the argument. Notice the structure: a claim, two examples, and a critical follow-up.
The passage includes a potential objection in the commentary; understand the main argument first.
2
Analyze the scorpion sting analogy
The author argues that if pain were solely from separation, a scorpion sting (small separation) would not be more painful than a large wound. The extra pain must come from the differing bad temperament introduced by the venom.
Consider the assumption that the scorpion sting causes a different kind of separation – the commentary raises this point.
3
Understand the fever proof
The constant, intense heat of the 'crushed' fever (al-diq) is contrasted with the intermittent heat of tertian fever (al-ghibb). The former settles in the organs, dissolving them, while the latter comes and goes with an external humor. This shows that lasting bad temperament (the crushed fever) is painful, whereas temporary temperament (tertian) is less painful.
This is presented as a proof a priori (ānī) and a posteriori (limmī).
4
Consider the objection and correction
The commentary raises an objection: the scorpion's venom may cause a separation different from the needle, and it's not clear that the separation is less. The author acknowledges this, showing the argument relies on an assumption. This highlights the need for a more rigorous demonstration, like the fever proof.
Think about how the fever proof does not rely on such assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتدال، والمؤلم من هذين سوء المزاج المختلف، ولذلك أي ولأنَّ سوء المزاج المختلف سبب للألم ( تؤلم لسعة العقرب ما لا تؤلم الإبرة بل تلك اللسعة أشد إيلاماً من الجراحة الكبيرة، ولو كان المؤلم تفرق الاتصال فقط لم يكن الأمر كذلك ( بخلاف ) سوء المزاج ( المتفق فإنه لا يؤلم ويدل عليه برهان آني ولمي (إما آنيته فإنَّ حرارة المدقوق أكثر من حرارة صاحب الغب بكثير لأنَّ حرارة الدق مستقرة في الأعضاء الأصلية ومذيبة لها وحرارة الغب واردة من مجاورة خلط صفراوي على أعضاء هي على مزاجها الطبيعي، حتى إذا تنحى عنها ذلك الخلط كانت باقية على أمزجتها الأصلية (والثاني) من المذكورين أعني حرارة الغب ( مدرك دون جوهر. قوله : (تؤلم لسعة العقرب إلخ) يمكن أن يقول : العقرب بسميته المبردة يفرق تفريقاً غير تفريق دخول جرم إبرته ولا دليل على أنَّ هذا التفريق الحاصل من المجموع أدنى من تفريق الإبرة، ولا أنَّ تفريق جراحة إيلامها أنقص من إيلام نفس اللسعة . قوله : ( من حرارة صاحب الغب) الغب في الأصل أن ترد إبل الماء يوماً وتدعه يوماً، وكذا في الحمى والدق أيضاً نوع من الحمى وتفسيره يفهم من کلامه .
Moderation (al-i'tidāl), and the painful of these two is differing bad temperament. Therefore, because differing bad temperament is a cause of pain, the sting of a scorpion causes pain while the needle does not; rather that sting is more painful than a large wound. And if the painful were only separation of continuity, it would not be so. Contrary to conforming bad temperament, it does not cause pain, and it is demonstrated by a proof a priori and a posteriori (burhān ānī wa limmī). As for its a priori (āniyyatu-hu): the heat of the crushed (al-madqūq) is much greater than the heat of the tertian fever (ghibb) because the heat of the crushed is settled in the primary organs and dissolves them, while the heat of the tertian fever comes from the adjacency of a bilious humor to organs that are on their natural temperament, so that when that humor departs from them, they remain on their original temperaments. And the second of the mentioned, I mean the heat of the tertian fever, is perceived without essence (mudrak dūna jawhar). His saying: '(the sting of a scorpion pains etc.)' – it can be said: The scorpion with its cooling venom causes a separation different from the separation caused by the entry of the body of its needle, and there is no proof that this separation resulting from the combination is less than the separation of the needle, nor that the separation of the wound's pain is less than the pain of the sting itself. His saying: '(from the heat of the tertian fever)' – al-ghib originally means that camels come to water one day and leave it one day, and similarly in fever; and al-diq is also a type of fever, and its explanation is understood from his speech.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage provides experimental and rational proof that differing bad temperament is the direct cause of pain. The author cites the example of a scorpion's sting, which is more painful than a needle prick or even a large wound. If pain were solely due to separation of continuity, the scorpion's sting (which produces a small separation) would not be more painful than a large wound. The extra pain must come from the differing bad temperament introduced by the venom. The author then presents a proof a priori (ānī) and a posteriori (limmī) using the contrast between two fevers: the crushed (madqūq) fever, which is constant and intense due to settled heat, and the tertian (ghibb) fever, which is intermittent because it originates from an external humor that departs, leaving the organs on their natural temperament. This shows that pain arises from a differing temperament (the settled heat) and not from separation alone. The commentary raises a possible objection: the scorpion's venom causes a different kind of separation, and it is not obvious that this separation is less; the author notes that there is no proof that the separation from the sting is less than that from the wound, so the argument may not be decisive. The final note defines 'ghibb' and 'diq' as types of fevers.

Key Terms
برهان آني ولمي
الغب
الدق
سوء المزاج المختلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pain is caused solely by the physical tearing or separation of tissue.
Correction: The scorpion sting example shows that a small separation (sting) can be far more painful than a large wound, indicating that the venom’s differing bad temperament is the primary cause.

Checkpoint
Why does the scorpion's sting cause more pain than a needle prick, according to the author?
A) Because scorpion venom causes a larger separation
B) Because scorpion venom introduces a differing bad temperament
C) Because the needle does not break the skin
D) Because scorpion venom is hot

Key Takeaway
Differing bad temperament, not mere separation, is the direct cause of pain, as shown by the scorpion sting and fever examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain, do you immediately attribute it to a purely physical cause (like an injury), or do you consider that your body's inner temperament (amzija) might be out of balance? How does this lesson challenge you to rethink the source of your pain?
?
Think of a recurring ache or discomfort you have. Could it be that a 'differing temperament' in your organs is at work, rather than just an external factor? What would it mean for your healing if you took this internal cause seriously?
?
The scorpion sting proves that even a small external agent can cause disproportionate pain due to its temperament. What 'venoms' in your life – words, environments, habits – might be affecting your inner balance in ways you ignore? Will you investigate them now?

Wetness and Dryness as Indirect Causes of Pain
The author discusses that wetness and dryness are passive qualities and only indirectly cause pain through separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why wetness and dryness are considered passive qualities and only indirectly cause pain through separation of continuity.

Core Idea
Wetness and dryness are passive, not direct causes of pain.

This passage from a commentary on al-Mawaqif examines the role of wetness and dryness in causing pain. The author argues that these qualities are not active causes but rather passive, and pain arises indirectly through separation of continuity. We will explore the reasoning behind this and the subsequent discussion that refines the understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once, then its literal translation. Focus on the central claim that wetness and dryness are not direct causes of pain.
2
Grasp the core argument
Understand why the author insists that wetness and dryness are passive qualities (kayfiyyāt infi'āliyya) and not active. Pain arises only indirectly when they lead to separation of continuity.
The author distinguishes between wetness as preparedness vs moisture (balla).
3
Analyze the objections and discussion
Examine the two points raised in the discussion: first, whether dryness could directly cause pain; second, whether wetness could also cause pain through expansion. Note how the author resolves these by referring to matter as the cause.
This shows the nuanced reasoning in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشتهر أن بالعرض لأنه قد يسعه سبب من الجنس الآخر، وهو تفرق الاتصال كذا في القانون، يعني الرطوبة واليبوسة حقيقتها الاستعداد نحو القبول واللاقبول وليستا كيفيتين ملموستين على ما كذا حققه في الشفاء، وما قيل : إنَّ المزاج حاصل من كثير الكيفيات الأربع، فمعناه أنَّ للرطوبة واليبوسة مدخلا في حصول الكيفية المتوسطة، لا أنَّ لهما دخلاً على ما اشتهر بينهم، وبما ذكرنا اندفع ما قيل : إِنَّ الرطوبة قد تكون موجبة للاسترخاء الموجب لتفرق الاتصال فيكون سوء المزاج الرطب موجباً للألم بالعارض كاليابس، لأنَّ الرطوبة بمعنى سهولة قبول الإشكال لا تكون موجبة للاسترخاء ما لم تقارنه الرطوبة، فإنه بمعنى البلة . قوله : (سوء المزاج المختلف لكن اشترط في سوء المزاج المختلف المؤلم أن يكون حاراً أو بارداً لا رطباً ولا يابساً، بناء على أن الرطوبة واليبوسة من الكيفيات الانفعالية دون الفعلية ثم ذكر أن سوء المزاج اليابس قد يكون مؤلماً بالعرض لأنه قد يتبعه لشدة التقبض تفرق الاتصال المؤلم بالذات، وفيه بحث، أما أولاً : فلما تقرر في بحث المزاج أنَّ كلاً من الكيفيات الأربع فاعلة وإن كان الفعل في الحرارة والبرودة ،أقوى ولهذا سميتا بالفعليتين وبالجملة كما يجعل اليبوسة سبباً لتفرق الاتصال فليكن سبباً للوجع من غير توسط تفرق الاتصال، اللهم إلا أن يبني كلامه على أنهما ليسا بمحسوسين، كما مال إليه الشيخ في فصل الإسطقسات من الشفاء وإن كان مخالفاً للمشهور، ولما صرح به في مباحث النفس منه. وأما ثانياً : فلان الرطوبة أيضاً قد تستتبع اللازم لكثرة الرطوبة المحوجة إلى مكان أوسع، وقد يجاب عن هذا بأنَّ ذلك إنما يكون في الرطوبة التي مع المادة، فيكون الموجب هو المادة لا الرطوبة نفسها.
It is well-known that accidentally, because it may be encompassed by a cause from another genus, which is separation of continuity, as in the Canon. Meaning that wetness (ruṭūba) and dryness (yubūsa) are in reality preparedness (isti'dād) towards acceptance and non-acceptance, and they are not tangible qualities (kayfiyyat malmūsatān) according to what was verified in the Shifā'. And what was said: that temperament (mizāj) results from the many of the four qualities (kayfiyyāt arba'), its meaning is that wetness and dryness have a role in obtaining the intermediate quality, not that they have a role according to what is famous among them. And by what we have mentioned, what was said is refuted: that wetness may necessitate relaxation (istirkhā') which necessitates separation of continuity, so wet bad temperament becomes a cause of pain accidentally, like dry, because wetness in the sense of easy acceptance of shapes does not necessitate relaxation unless accompanied by wetness in the sense of moisture (balla). His saying: '(differing bad temperament, but it is stipulated that the differing bad temperament that is painful is hot or cold, not wet or dry)' based on that wetness and dryness are among the passive qualities (infi'āliyya) rather than the active (fi'liyya). Then he mentioned that dry bad temperament may be painful accidentally because it may be followed, due to severe contraction (taqabbuḍ), by separation of continuity which is painful per se. And there is a discussion: First, because it was established in the discussion of temperament that each of the four qualities is active (fā'ila), although the action in heat and cold is stronger, and for this they are called the two active ones. In short, just as dryness is made a cause for separation of continuity, so let it be a cause for pain without the mediation of separation of continuity, unless he bases his statement on that they are not sensible (maḥsūsātān), as the Shaykh inclined to in the chapter on elements from the Shifā', although it is contrary to the famous, and to what he clearly stated in the sections on the soul from it. Second: because wetness also may entail the necessary due to abundance of moisture requiring a wider space, and it may be answered that this only occurs in wetness that is with matter, so the necessitating factor is the matter, not wetness itself.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage delves into the nature of wetness (ruṭūba) and dryness (yubūsa) as causes of pain. It begins by noting that they are only indirect causes, possibly through separation of continuity, as stated in Ibn Sina's Canon. Their essence is preparedness for acceptance or non-acceptance, not tangible qualities. The common idea that temperament arises from the four qualities is reinterpreted: wetness and dryness contribute to the intermediate quality but are not directly involved. The author then refutes the objection that wetness could cause relaxation leading to separation and thus pain; he distinguishes between wetness as ease of accepting shapes (which does not cause relaxation) and wetness as moisture. The commentary further explains the stipulation that only hot or cold differing bad temperament is directly painful; wet and dry are passive qualities. Dryness can indirectly cause pain by leading to contraction and hence separation of continuity. However, the commentator raises a discussion: if dryness can cause separation, why not directly cause pain? He notes that Ibn Sina himself in the Shifā' considered wetness and dryness not sensible, contradicting his own statements elsewhere. Additionally, wetness might indirectly cause pain through expansion, but that is attributed to matter, not wetness itself.

اليبوسة (al-yubūsa)

الكيفيات الفعلية (al-kayfiyyāt al-fi'liyya)
الاسترخاء (al-istirkhā')
التقبض (al-taqabbuḍ)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wetness and dryness are direct causes of pain like heat and cold.
Correction: Wetness and dryness are passive qualities; they only lead to pain indirectly through separation of continuity or through matter.

Key Takeaway
Wetness and dryness are passive qualities that cause pain only indirectly through separation of continuity, not by their own nature.

The Claim of Necessary Existence of Relations and the Objection
Al-Iji presents the claim, objection, and answer regarding relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether relative entities like 'above-ness' exist externally or are merely non-existential descriptions, and how the objection and its answer rely on distinguishing attribution from existence.

Core Idea
Descriptive attribution does not entail external existence.

Al-Iji examines a key theological claim: that relations such as above-ness are necessarily existent regardless of any observer. He then presents a forceful objection, showing that this claim is not self-evident and that describing something as 'above' does not prove the external reality of above-ness. The argument is sharpened with a logical puzzle, and the answer clarifies a crucial distinction. This lesson will walk you through each move.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice the flow: a claim, an objection, a supporting argument, and an answer.
Focus on the distinction between 'description' (اتصاف) and 'existence' (وجود).
2
Identify the claim and the objection
The passage begins by stating the claim: relations are known necessarily to exist. Then an objector denies this, calling it the very point of dispute. Find the exact phrases that mark these positions.
The objection includes an example: Zayd's blindness.
3
Understand the argument from change
The objector gives a refined argument: if something becomes above after not being above, that new above-ness cannot be non-existential. Work through why that would lead to a contradiction (negation of negation).
The answer explains that 'becoming above' just means acquiring a description, not bringing a new existent into being.
4
Grasp the answer's logical move
See how the answer simply says: 'the occurrence of above-ness after it was not is an expression of the thing's being described by it after it was not described' – i.e., description changes without external existence changing.
This is the core lesson: relative attributes are matters of description, not of extra mental entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي نعلم بالضرورة أنها ثابتة حاصلة سواء وجد هناك فرض فارض واعتبار معتبر أو لم يوجد، ولقائل أن يقول : إن ادعيتم أن الفوقية مثلاً من الموجودات الخارجية منعناه، بل هذا هو المتنازع فيه، فكيف يدعى الضرورة فيه وإن قلتم : السماء موصوفة بالفوقية في الخارج، فلذلك لا يستلزم وجود الفوقية فيه لجواز اتصاف الأعيان الخارجية بالأمور العدمية، فإنَّ زيداً أعمى في الخارج وليس العمى موجوداً خارجياً، وقد يستدل على ذلك أيضاً بأنَّ الشيء، قد لا يكون فوقاً ثم يصير فوقاً، فالفوقية التي حصلت بعد العدم لا تكون عدمية وإلا كان نفي النفي نفيا وهو محال، ويجاب عنه : بأن حصول الفوقية بعد ما لم تكن عبارة عن اتصاف الشيء بها بعد ما لم يكن متصفاً، وذلك لا يستلزم وجودها كما عرفت
That is, we know necessarily that they (relative entities) are established and existent regardless of whether there exists a supposing supposer or a considering considerer. And someone may say: If you claim that above-ness, for example, is among external existents, we deny it; rather this is the disputed point. So how can necessity be claimed concerning it? And if you say: The sky is described by above-ness externally, that does not entail the existence of above-ness in it because of the permissibility of external entities being described by non-existential matters. Indeed, Zayd is blind externally while blindness is not an external existent. And it may also be argued by that a thing may not be above and then becomes above; so the above-ness that occurs after non-existence cannot be non-existential, otherwise the negation of negation would be negation, which is impossible. And it is answered: That the occurrence of above-ness after it was not is an expression of the thing's being described by it after it was not described, and that does not entail its existence, as you have known.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage begins with the claim that relative entities are known necessarily to exist regardless of any mental consideration. Then an objection is raised: if you claim that above-ness is external, we deny it; that is the very point of dispute. The objection continues: describing the sky as above does not entail the existence of above-ness because external objects can be described by non-existential attributes, like Zayd's blindness which is a description but not an existent. A further argument is given: something that becomes above after not being above – the above-ness that occurs after non-existence cannot be non-existential, otherwise the negation of negation (i.e., existence) would be negation, which is absurd. The answer to this is: the occurrence of above-ness after non-existence simply means the thing became described by it after not being described, and that does not require the existence of above-ness as an external entity. Thus, the objection is refuted by distinguishing between descriptive attribution and external existence.

Key Terms
الفوقية (above-ness)
الضرورة (necessity)
الاتصاف (description/attribution)
الأمور العدمية (non-existential matters)
نفي النفي (negation of negation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is described by a relative attribute, that attribute must exist as an external entity.
Correction: The passage shows that external objects can be described by non-existential matters (like blindness), and the change from not-above to above is merely a change in description, not the creation of a new existent.

Checkpoint
What does the example of Zayd's blindness illustrate in the objection?
A) That blindness is a real external entity.
B) That external objects can be described by non-existential matters.
C) That above-ness is a kind of blindness.
D) That necessity does not apply to relations.

Key Takeaway
Descriptive attribution of a relative term does not entail the external existence of that relation; it is merely a way of describing the object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If you were to apply the lesson's distinction to a conflict in your life—where you feel certain that someone 'owes you' something (a relation of obligation)—how might the claim that this relation may not be an external existent change your approach? Are you willing to treat it as a description that can be revisited?

The passage challenges claims of necessity (ḍarūra) in knowledge. Is there a belief about God, yourself, or others that you hold as 'obviously necessary' but that, upon reflection, might be a mere description you inherited? Will you examine one such belief today?

Classification of Relations and Resolution of Infinite Regress
Al-Iji classifies relations into external and mental, and shows how the first type terminates at a limit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun classify relations into external and mental, and how the first type terminates at a limit, thereby resolving the problem of infinite regress.

Core Idea
External relations must end at a final limit, halting the chain of regress.

In this passage, al-Iji presents the response of the mutakallimun to opponents who argue that all relations are mental. He first distinguishes between relations that exist externally and those invented by the intellect. He then argues that the external type terminates at a limit, preventing an infinite chain. This lesson will walk you through this classification and its logical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two types of relations
The passage divides relations into two categories. Find the defining characteristics of each.
The first type exists externally; the second is invented by the intellect.
3
Understand the termination argument
The passage claims that the first type of relation 'terminates at a limit'. Ask yourself what this means and why it is important.
Termination prevents an infinite series of real relations.
4
Connect to resolving infinite regress
See how the termination argument refutes the opponents' proofs that would force an infinite regress. The chain can end at an existent relation, with subsequent relations being merely mental.
This is the key insight of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجابوا عن أدلة الخصم بأنها إنما تنفي كون جميع النسب موجودة في الخارج أي هذه الأدلة تدل على سلب الموجبة الكلية ونحن نقول به فإنَّ من الإضافات والنسب أموراً موجودة في الخارج حقيقتها أنها إضافة كالفوقية والمقابلة ونظائرهما ومنها إضافات لا تحقق لها الخارج بل يخترعها العقل عند ملاحظة أمرين كالتقدم والتأخر بين أمرين لا يجوز اجتماعهما كأجزاء الزمان والقسم الأول من هذين ينتهي عند حد أي يجب انتهاؤه إلى حد لا يتجاوزه دون الثاني إذ لا يقف عند حد لا يمكن للعقل أن يتجاوزه ويفرض إضافة أخرى بعده، وعلى هذا فقد انجلت تلك الأدلة واندفع التسلسل في الأمور الخارجية لجواز أن تنتهي السلسلة إلى نسبة موجودة يكون ما بعدها من النسب اعتبارية، إذ ليس يلزم من وجود الفوقية في نفسها أن يكون حلولها في محلها أمراً موجوداً أيضاً، ولا من وجود حلولها وجود حلول الحلول، وكون هذه النسب متوافقة في الماهية لا يقتضي اشتراكها في الوجود لجواز أن يكون بعض أفراد الماهية موجوداً وبعضها معدوماً، وقد يجاب عن بعض تلك الأدلة بكونه منقوضاً بالأين
And they answered the proofs of the opponent by saying that they only negate that all relations exist externally, meaning these proofs indicate the negation of the universal affirmative, and we affirm that. For among relations and relative entities are things existing externally, their reality being that they are relation, like above-ness and opposition and their likes. And among them are relations that have no realization externally, but the intellect invents them when observing two things, like priority and posteriority between two things that cannot coexist, like parts of time. And the first of these two types terminates at a limit, i.e., it must reach a limit beyond which it cannot go, unlike the second type, since it does not stop at a limit that the intellect cannot go beyond and posit another relation after it. And according to this, those proofs have been clarified and infinite regress in external matters is repelled, because it is permissible that the chain ends at an existent relation such that what comes after it among relations are mental. For the existence of above-ness itself does not necessitate that its inherence in its locus is also an existent thing, nor does the existence of its inherence necessitate the existence of a inherence of inherence. And the fact that these relations agree in essence does not entail that they share in existence, because it is permissible that some individuals of the essence exist and some are non-existent. And it may be answered for some of those proofs by its being refuted by al-ayn (the category of where).
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's answer to the opponents' proofs. They accept that the opponents' proofs only show that not all relations exist externally—i.e., they negate the universal affirmative claim. The mutakallimun agree with this. They then classify relations into two types: first, relations that exist externally, whose reality is relation itself, such as above-ness and opposition; second, relations that have no external realization but are invented by the intellect when considering two things, such as priority and posteriority between things that cannot coexist (like parts of time). The first type terminates at a limit, meaning it must reach a final relation beyond which no further relation can be posited. This prevents infinite regress because the chain can end at an existent relation, and subsequent relations are merely mental. The reasoning is: the existence of above-ness does not require that its inherence in a locus is also an existent thing, nor does inherence itself require a further inherence of inherence. Also, agreement in essence does not imply agreement in existence; some individuals of a kind may exist while others do not. Finally, it is noted that some of the opponents' proofs can be refuted by pointing to the category of ayn (where).

Key Terms
الإضافات (relations)
النسب (relative entities)
الماهية (essence)
التسلسل (infinite regress)
الأين (the category of where/space)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All relations are either purely external or purely mental, and they cannot coexist or terminate.
Correction: The passage shows that both types exist, and external relations terminate at a limit, preventing infinite regress. Some relations are real and some are mental, and they do not all share existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is an example of a relation that exists externally?
A) Priority between parts of time
B) Above-ness
C) Inherence of inherence
D) All relations

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun resolve infinite regress by distinguishing external relations that terminate at a limit from mental relations that do not require termination.

?
In your daily life, do you ever treat mental relations (like 'priority' or 'importance') as if they were real, external facts? How might that affect your decisions and your reliance on Allah?
?
The passage shows that the chain of real relations must stop at a limit. When you consider the chain of causes and means in your own life—your job, health, family—do you eventually stop at Allah as the ultimate limit, or do you keep pushing further as if there is no end?
?
Al-Iji argues that not all relations are externally real. What 'mental relations' have you been acting upon as if they were absolute truths that might actually be illusions distracting you from the real? How can you re-center your reality on the Creator alone?

Al-Razi's Circularity Objection and Its Resolution
Al-Razi objects that substance's location and attribute's subsistence cause circularity; commentator answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's circularity objection regarding substance and attribute, and how the commentator refutes it by redefining qiyām and proposing an alternative causal chain.

Core Idea
Circularity can be avoided by defining qiyām as attributive specification, not spatialization.

This lesson presents a critical objection from Imam al-Razi concerning the relationship between a substance and its attribute. Al-Razi argues that if the occurrence of a substance in space is caused by the subsistence of an attribute, then circularity results. The commentator responds by clarifying the meaning of subsistence and offering an alternative causal chain. Understanding this debate is essential for grasping the kalam theory of causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the argument.
Pay attention to the terms 'حصول' and 'قيام'.
2
Identify the three elements
List the three things mentioned: substance, occurrence, and cause. Understand their roles.
3
Analyze al-Razi's circularity argument
Trace how al-Razi claims mutual dependence leads to circularity. What is his definition of qiyām?
4
Examine the commentator's two-part answer
First, understand the redefinition of qiyām as ikhtiṣāṣ nā‘it. Second, consider the alternative causal chain where the attribute's essence causes occurrence.
Notice that the commentator provides two independent refutations.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this debate is important for the broader kalam theory of causality. How does the definition of qiyām affect the argument?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الأول : إنكار المتكلمين سائر المقولات علة للحصول بالكون فهناك ثلاثة أشياء ذات الجوهر، وحصوله في الحيز وعلته قال الإمام الرازي : في الأربعين هذا عندنا باطل إذ حصول الصفة للشيء معناه تحيزها تبعاً لتحيزه فإذا فرض أنَّ حصول الجوهر في الحيز معلل بقيام صفة أخرى بالجوهر كان كل واحد من الحصول وتلك الصفة متوفقاً على الآخر فيلزم الدور والجواب ما قد عرفته في المرصد الأول من هذا الموقف، وهو أنا لا نسلم أن معنى القيام ما ذكر، بل هو الاختصاص الناعت مع أنه إِن سلم أن معنى القيام ذلك فلا نسلم لزوم الدور، لأنه قد تكون ذات الصفة لا قيامها بالجوهر علة للحصول ويكون تحيزها الذي هو قيامها معللاً به بالحصول فلا دور
The cause for occurrence in space: then there are three things: the substance itself, its occurrence in the locus, and its cause. Imam al-Razi said in al-Arba‘īn: This is invalid in our view, because the subsistence of an attribute in a thing means its being spatialized as a consequence of its spatialization. So if it is assumed that the occurrence of a substance in the locus is caused by the subsistence of another attribute in the substance, then each one of the occurrence and that attribute depends on the other, so circularity follows. The answer is what you already know from the first observatory of this position, namely that we do not accept that the meaning of subsistence is what was mentioned; rather it is attributive specification. Moreover, if it is accepted that the meaning of subsistence is that, we do not accept the necessity of circularity, because it may be that the essence of the attribute, not its subsistence in the substance, is the cause of the occurrence, and its spatialization which is its subsistence is caused by the occurrence, so there is no circularity.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding the relationship between a substance (jawhar) and its spatial location. The passage begins by stating that there are three things: the substance itself, its occurrence in a place, and the cause of that occurrence. Imam al-Razi then argues that if we say the occurrence of a substance in a place is caused by the subsistence of an attribute in that substance, then the occurrence depends on the subsistence and the subsistence depends on the occurrence, leading to circularity (dawr). The commentator responds by denying the premise that 'subsistence' (qiyām) means what al-Razi claimed. Instead, the commentator defines qiyām as 'attributive specification' (ikhtiṣāṣ nā‘it), which does not imply mutual dependency. Furthermore, even if we accept al-Razi's definition, circularity does not necessarily follow because it is possible that the essence of the attribute (dhāt al-ṣifa) itself—not its subsistence in the substance—is the cause of the occurrence. In that case, the attribute's own spatialization (which is its subsistence) is caused by the occurrence, breaking the circular dependence.

Key Terms
جوهر
حصول
حيز
صفة
قيام
دور
اختصاص ناعت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Circularity between substance and attribute is inevitable given the causal relationship.
Correction: The commentator shows that circularity only arises if one accepts a particular definition of qiyām. By redefining qiyām as 'attributive specification' or by offering an alternative causal chain, circularity is avoided.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the true meaning of qiyām (subsistence) of an attribute in a substance?
A) Taḥayyuz tābi‘ (dependent spatialization)
B) Ikhtiṣāṣ nā‘it (attributive specification)
C) Ta‘alluq (relation)
D) Ḥulūl (inherence)

Key Takeaway
The commentator resolves al-Razi's circularity objection by redefining 'subsistence' as attributive specification and proposing an alternative causal relationship where the attribute's essence, not its subsistence, causes the substance's occurrence.

Reflect on your own understanding of Allah's attributes: Is there an attribute you define in a way that might lead to a logical contradiction? What step can you take this week to refine that definition based on sound revelation?
?
The commentator offers two ways out of circularity: redefine a key term or reexamine the causal chain. When you face a confusion in your faith, do you tend to redefine concepts or rethink the causal sequence? Which approach is more needed in your life right now? Commit to one specific action to address a current spiritual dilemma.
