والبلقة عند السواد والبياض مع أنهما يقومان، وقد سبق ما فيه فتذكر.
Know that some mutakallimun said: motion is the sum of two kawns in two instants in two places, and stillness is the sum of two kawns in two instants in one place. It was refuted by saying that then the first kawn in the second place would be part of both motion and stillness, and they would not be distinguished essentially, despite their agreement on the existence of the four types of kawn, and that motion and stillness would not exist according to this view for those who do not hold the persistence of accidents. His statement: (these did not consider, etc.) So the division according to them is that the kawn in the place, if preceded by a kawn in another place without mediation, is motion; otherwise it is stillness, whether it is not preceded by any kawn at all or preceded by a kawn in that same place. So in stillness, being preceded is not considered; therefore a single kawn can be both stillness and motion under two aspects, as he will clearly state, and that is the meaning of his statement: so it follows that motion is composed of stillness, i.e., extended motion composed of stillnesses, not motion in the sense of a specific kawn, for it has no part. And this usage among the mutakallimun is like the usage of 'tawassut' and 'qat' among the philosophers. His statement: (for one of two opposites cannot be part of the other) because two opposites must alternate upon a single subject; if one were a part predicated of the other, they would coexist in that of which that one is true, and what is predicated of a thing is predicated of that thing if the predication is conventional; if it is a non-predicated part, they do not alternate at all. So the majority chose what the author mentioned: that motion is a first occurrence in a second place, and stillness is a second occurrence in a first place. And an objection was raised against both views holding the persistence of kawns, that it would follow that one and the same kawn would be both motion and stillness, the difference between them being like the difference between youth and old age, and this may be accepted based on their consensus that the difference between types of kawn is by accidental considerations (al-awarid al-i'tibariyya) not by constitutive essential differences. His statement: (these did not consider in stillness remaining (lubth), etc.) And according to this, what he mentioned in the method of division is not complete; rather the method is to say: if it is preceded by its occurrence in another place it is motion, otherwise stillness. His statement: (so it follows that motion is composed of stillness. If you say: not considering the remaining and being preceded by another kawn in that place in the kawn does not entail not considering the non-precedence by another kawn in another place, so the inference that extended motion is composed of stillness from their not considering them is not correct;) what the commentator mentioned is based on their view that the first and second occurrences in the first place are similar, and likewise in the second place, so the said inference is valid, as is not hidden. His statement: (for one of two opposites cannot be part of the other) it is said: this is a well-known statement of theirs, and they have no other proof for it; how about 'balqa' (?) concerning black and white, even though they are accidents? And what has preceded about it is remembered.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage debates the nature of motion and stillness, specifically whether extended motion can be composed of successive stillnesses (sakanat). The commentator begins by reporting a view of some mutakallimun that motion is the sum of two kawns in two instants in two places, and stillness is the sum of two kawns in two instants in one place. This is refuted because it would make the first kawn in the second place part of both motion and stillness, losing essential distinction. Moreover, those who deny the persistence of accidents would not allow motion and stillness to exist at all under this view. The commentary then explains the reasoning of those who do not consider 'remaining' (lubth) in stillness: for them, a kawn is motion if preceded by a kawn in another place without mediation; otherwise it is stillness, regardless of whether it is preceded by any kawn or by a kawn in the same place. Consequently, a single kawn could be both stillness and motion under two aspects, leading to the conclusion that extended motion is composed of stillnesses (sakanat). The commentator clarifies that this does not apply to motion as a specific kawn (which has no parts) but to extended motion, analogous to how philosophers distinguish 'tawassut' and 'qat'. The objector then argues that motion cannot be composed of stillness because they are opposites, and opposites cannot be parts of each other. The response is that motion and stillness are not absolute opposites; they can alternate. The majority ultimately chose the author's definition: motion as a first occurrence in a second place and stillness as a second occurrence in a first place. An objection against both views is raised: if kawns persist, the same kawn could be both motion and stillness, differing only accidentally (like youth and old age), which some accept. The commentary further refines the division method, emphasizing that being preceded by an occurrence in another place is the criterion for motion, otherwise stillness. It defends the inference that motion is composed of stillness based on the similarity of first and second occurrences in the same place. Finally, it notes that the claim that one opposite cannot be part of another is a well-known assertion without other proof, citing the example of 'balqa' (?) with black and white.

The First Property of Motion: Motion Entails a Sought-After Goal
Commentator explains first property of motion and refutes objection from dispositional potentiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first special property of motion is that it entails a sought-after goal not yet attained, and how this distinguishes motion from mere dispositional potentiality.

Core Idea
Motion is essentially a leading toward a goal that is not yet present.

The commentator begins by explaining the first property of motion from the matn: motion's reality is 'leading' (at-ta'dī), and from this it follows that motion necessitates a sought-after goal that is not yet obtained. He then compares motion to dispositional potentiality, which might seem similar but lacks the direction of seeking. The passage clarifies this distinction and answers an objection.

Guided Path
1
Understand Key Terms
Familiarize yourself with the Arabic terms: ta'dī (leading), matlub ghayr al-hasil (sought goal not yet obtained), and imkān isti'dādī (dispositional potentiality). These are central to the argument.
These terms are defined in the passage itself.
2
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the complete Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note how the commentator structures his explanation around quotes from the matn.
3
Trace the Argument
Follow the commentator's reasoning: motion's reality as leading entails a sought goal. Contrast this with dispositional potentiality, which does not necessitate a sought goal, only something unattained.
Pay attention to the objection and its refutation.
4
Reflect on the Distinction
Consider the commentator's concluding call to reflect (falyatamal). The key difference is that motion involves seeking (talab), while potentiality does not. Why is this important for theology?
This connects to broader discussions of causality and divine attributes.

قوله : ( أن حقيقتها هي (التأدي) أي لازم لها ذلك كأنه نفس حقيقتها. قوله : ( تستلزم إلخ ) بخلاف الإمكان الاستعدادي فإنه لا يستلزم حصول ما يستعد لشيء له وإن كان يترتب عليه . قوله : (وَلَيْسَ شَيْءٌ مِنْ سَائِرِ الْكَمَالَاتِ بِهَذِهِ الصِّفَةِ) فإن قلت: يرد عليه الإمكان الاستعدادي فإنه يستلزم أيضاً أن يكون المقبول غير حاصل معه بالفعل، فإنَّ التحقيق أنَّ الاستعداد سواء كان قريباً أو بعيداً يبطل مع الفعل قلت : الإمكان الاستعدادي وإن استلزم أن يكون هناك شيء غير حاصل لكن لا يستلزم أن يكون هناك مطلوب غير حاصل، إذ لا تعلق له بالطلب وأما استلزام الحركة للمطلوب الغير الحاصل فمن جهة أن حقيقتها التأدي إلى الغير وطلبه، وبالجملة الإمكان الاستعدادي يخرج بقوله : من حيث أن حقيقتها التأدي، فليتأمل.
His saying: ('that its reality is leading (at-ta'dī)') meaning it is necessary for it as if it is its reality. His saying: ('it necessitates, etc.') unlike dispositional potentiality (al-imkān al-isti'dādī), for it does not necessitate the obtainment of what one is disposed for, even if it follows from it. His saying: ('and nothing from the rest of perfections has this description') — if you say: the objection arises from dispositional potentiality, for it also necessitates that the recipient is not actually obtained with it, indeed the verification is that disposition, whether near or far, ceases with the act. I say: dispositional potentiality, even if it necessitates that there is something not obtained, does not necessitate that there is a sought thing not obtained, since it has no relation to seeking. As for motion's necessitation of the unattained sought thing, it is because its reality is leading to another and seeking it. In sum, dispositional potentiality is excluded by his saying: 'since its reality is leading', so reflect.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the first special property of motion: that motion entails a sought-after goal not yet attained. The commentator begins by quoting the matn phrase 'that its reality is leading (at-ta'dī)' and clarifies that this leading is necessary to motion, almost its very essence. Then he explains the phrase 'it necessitates' in the matn, distinguishing motion from dispositional potentiality (al-imkān al-isti'dādī): dispositional potentiality does not necessitate actual attainment of the thing for which one is disposed, while motion does. Next, he addresses an objection: dispositional potentiality also seems to entail something not yet obtained, so why is motion special? The commentator answers that dispositional potentiality does not entail a sought (مطلوب) goal; it is not directed toward seeking. Motion, however, is essentially leading (تأدي) and seeking (طلب) the goal. Thus the objection fails because the matn's condition 'from the aspect that its reality is leading' excludes dispositional potentiality. The commentator concludes with 'so reflect' (فليتأمل), signaling the depth of the distinction.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dispositional potentiality also entails a sought-after goal, making motion's property not unique.
Correction: Dispositional potentiality entails that something is not yet obtained, but it lacks the aspect of seeking (talab). Motion's property is unique because it is essentially leading and seeking a goal.

Checkpoint

C) Because dispositional potentiality is impossible.
D) Because motion is continuous.

Key Takeaway
Motion's unique property is that it leads toward a goal not yet attained, and this seeking is central to its nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
If motion without a sought goal is not true motion, what does that say about your spiritual state? Are you content with a life of potential (dispositional) without actually moving toward the goal of Allah's pleasure? What step can you take today to make your movement count?

The Second Property of Motion: Necessitating Potentiality
Commentator defines the second property and introduces dual potentiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how motion entails a dual potentiality relative to itself and its goal, and that the potentiality toward the goal is shared by both senses of motion.

Core Idea
Motion is always in potentiality until its goal is reached.

The commentator begins explaining the second property ascribed to motion: it requires potentiality. He first addresses a potential objection about the moment of arrival, then establishes that as long as the mover hasn't reached its destination, something of motion remains in potentiality. This leads to the insight that the essence of motion necessitates two potentialities: one relative to motion itself and one relative to the intended goal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the commentator sets up the property and the objection.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two potentialities
Find where the commentator says the essence of motion necessitates two potentialities. Understand what each refers to.
The first potentiality is relative to motion itself, the second to the intended goal.
3
Explore the common potentiality
The commentator explains the second potentiality (toward the goal) is common to both senses of motion. Reflect on why that is.

قوله : ( وثانيتهما أنها تقتضي إلخ) في ثبوت هذه الخصوصية للحركة وكونها خاصة لها على تقدير ثبوتها، لها بحث أما في الأول فلان الحركة تنعدم آن الوصول بلا شبهة، وذلك الآن طرف زمان ،الحركة، ففي زمان الحركة الجسم متحرك مع أنه لا قوة بعد ذلك الزمان بالنظر إلى بعض أجزاء الحركة لأنَّ الزمان تمام زمان ،الحركة، ويمكن أن يقال : هذه الخاصة للحركة بمعنى يكون متحركاً إذا لم يصل إلى المقصد، فإنه إذا وصل إليه فقد بالنظر إلى مجموع الحركة الواقعة في مجموع الزمان لم يرد إذ الاتصاف بالمجموع في وقت ما كما ستعرفه في المقصد الثاني، وإن أورد بالنظر إلى جزء من الحركة الواقع في جزء من الزمان لم يتجه أيضاً، إذ لا بد بعد His saying: ('and the second of them is that it necessitates, etc.') — concerning the existence of this specificity for motion and its being specific to it, assuming its existence, there is investigation. As for the first, because motion ceases at the moment of arrival without doubt, and that moment is the boundary of the time of motion, so in the time of motion the body is moving while there is no potentiality after that time with respect to some parts of motion because the time is the entire time of motion. And it can be said: this specificity for motion means that it is moving as long as it does not reach the destination, for when it reaches it its motion ceases, and as long as it has not reached, something of motion remains in potentiality. So the essence of motion necessitates that its subject, when described by it, contains two potentialities: a potentiality relative to motion itself, and another relative to what is intended by it. As for the potentiality relative to the intended object, it is common without difference between motion in the sense of cutting (al-qaṭ') and motion in the sense of intermediacy (at-tawassuṭ): for the body as long as it is in the distance does not reach the end, and when it reaches it, no motion remains at all. As for the other potentiality, there is a difference between them: for motion in the sense of cutting, when the mover is described by it, some of its parts are in potentiality and some in actuality, so potentiality and actuality are in one thing. Motion in the sense of intermediacy, when it occurs, is actual, and there is no potentiality belonging to its essence but rather to its relation to the boundaries of the distance, and those relations are external to its essence, accidental to it, as you will see. Thus it has become clear to you that motion is a perfection in the mentioned sense for the body that is in potentiality in that it contradicts some parts of motion being in potentiality, for every part supposed of motion is considered motion in potentiality until it reaches the end. His saying: 'so the essence (of motion)' — i.e., its personal essence existing in the external, and he only said that because the essence of the whole is not included in it. The aspect of cutting (al-qaṭ') as he stated, so this investigation if considered with respect to the total motion occurring in the total time is not valid, because the description with the total at some time as you will know in the second objective. And if considered with respect to a part of motion occurring in a part of time, it also does not apply, for after every supposed part of motion there must be another part of it; how not, for if nothing of motion remained in potentiality, the mover would be at the end, and then there would be no motion, as appears with minimal reflection. As for the second (i.e., the second aspect), because it holds for the parts of time, and time, even if it is not a perfection for the body since it is not an attribute of it, is a perfection for motion because it is a measure inhering in it. It cannot be said: the mentioned aspect is considered in this specificity too, so time is excluded because its reality is not leading to another, for we say: no, its consideration here is not, for it is meaningless to say: motion from the aspect that its reality is leading to another necessitates that something of it be in potentiality, since the mentioned aspect has no role in this necessitation. Yes, it can be answered that what is considered specific to motion is the necessitation of what mentioned for its essence, and the necessitation of time for it, if granted, is only by virtue of its correspondence to non-fixed motion and its being a measure for it. The verification in the answer is to say: the specificity is motion's necessitation that something of it be in potentiality for its subject itself, as indicated by his saying: 'so the essence of motion necessitates, etc.', and this is not realized in time because its subject is only motion, since it is its measure subsisting in it, and the part that is in potentiality from time does not have motion as its subject because it expires with the expiration of time, but rather another motion, so reflect. The second.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the second special property of motion: that motion requires something in potentiality. The commentator begins by quoting the matn's second property and then discusses its existence as specific to motion, assuming it holds. He first addresses a potential objection: at the moment of arrival, motion ceases, and that moment is the boundary of time, so during motion the body is moving without potentiality after that time regarding some parts of motion. This is answered by specifying that as long as the mover has not reached the destination, something of motion remains in potentiality. The essence of motion necessitates that its subject has two potentialities: one relative to motion itself, and another relative to the intended goal. The potentiality relative to the goal is common to both meanings of motion: 'cut' (القطع) and 'intermediate' (التوسط). For motion as cutting (transversing a distance), parts are partly actual and partly potential; for motion as intermediacy (being in the distance), it is wholly actual but has potentiality in its relations to the boundaries. The commentator then clarifies that motion is a perfection for the body that is in potentiality, and any part of motion is potential until the end. He then explains the phrase 'so the essence of motion' as the personal essence existing externally. He addresses a further investigation: if we consider the total motion in total time, the description of motion as having parts in potentiality fails; if we consider a part in a part of time, it also fails because after each part another must follow, otherwise the mover would be at the end and motion would cease. Finally, he discusses time: time is not a perfection for the body but for motion, and the property of requiring potentiality does not apply to time because time's reality is not 'leading to another'. The verification is that the specificity is motion's own necessitation for its subject, not time's, and the potential part of time belongs to a different motion. The passage ends with 'so reflect' and 'the second' indicating completion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion at the moment of arrival still requires potentiality.
Correction: At the moment of arrival, motion ceases and there is no potentiality remaining.

Distinguishing Motion as Cut and Intermediate: Actuality and Potentiality
Commentator distinguishes two senses of motion regarding actuality and potentiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between motion as cutting (al-qaṭ') and motion as intermediacy (at-tawassuṭ) regarding how potentiality and actuality are distributed.

Core Idea
In cut motion, parts are partly actual and partly potential; in intermediate motion, it is all actual but potential in relation to the distance.

Having established that motion entails two potentialities, the commentator now explains the crucial difference between the two meanings of motion. For motion as cutting (traversing a distance), some parts are in potentiality and some in actuality simultaneously. For motion as intermediacy (the state of being in the path), the motion itself is fully actual, but potentiality exists only in its relations to the boundaries of the distance.

Guided Path
1
Read the distinction passage
Read the passage comparing the two motions. Notice the key phrases: 'baʿḍu ajzāʾihā bil-quwwah wa baʿḍuhā bil-fiʿl' for cut, and 'kānat bil-fiʿl' for intermediate.
2
Understand the difference in potentiality
For cut motion, potentiality and actuality are in the same substance; for intermediate motion, potentiality is external to its essence.
The commentator says potentiality for intermediate motion is 'bi-nisbatihā ilā ḥudūd al-masāfah'.
3
Connect to the final conclusion
The passage concludes that motion is a perfection for the body in potentiality, and every part of motion is potential until the end.
This reinforces the earlier point.

قوله : ( وثانيتهما أنها تقتضي إلخ) في ثبوت هذه الخصوصية للحركة وكونها خاصة لها على تقدير ثبوتها، لها بحث أما في الأول فلان الحركة تنعدم آن الوصول بلا شبهة، وذلك الآن طرف زمان ،الحركة، ففي زمان الحركة الجسم متحرك مع أنه لا قوة بعد ذلك الزمان بالنظر إلى بعض أجزاء الحركة لأنَّ الزمان تمام زمان ،الحركة، ويمكن أن يقال : هذه الخاصة للحركة بمعنى يكون متحركاً إذا لم يصل إلى المقصد، فإنه إذا وصل إليه فقد انقطعت حركته، وما دام لم يصل فقد بقي من الحركة شيء بالقوة فهوية الحركة مستلزمة، لأن يكون محلها حال اتصافه بها مشتملا على قوتين قوة بالقياس إليها، وأخرى بالقياس إلى ما هو المقصود بها، أما القوة التي بالنسبة إلى المقصود فمشتركة بلا تفاوت بين الحركة بمعنى القطع والحركة بمعنى التوسط فإنَّ الجسم ما دام في المسافة لم يكن واصلا إلى المنتهى، وإذا وصل إليه لم يبق حركة أصلاً، وأما القوة الأخرى ففيها تفاوت بينهما، فإن الحركة بمعنى القطع حال اتصاف المتحرك بها يكون بعض أجزائها بالقوة وبعضها بالفعل فالقوة والفعل في ذات شيء واحد، والحركة بمعنى التوسط إذا حصلت كانت بالفعل ولم يكن هناك قوة متعلقة بذاتها بل بنسبتها إلى حدود المسافة، وتلك النسب خارجة عن ذاتها عارضة لها كما ستطلع عليه، فقد انكشف لك أنَّ الحركة كمال بالمعنى المذكور للجسم الذي هو بالقوة في ذلك ينافي بعض أجزاء الحركة بالقوة، فكل جزء يفرض للحركة يعد حركة بالقوة ما لم يصل إلى المنتهى . قوله فهوية (الحركة أي ماهيته الشخصية الموجودة في الخارج، وإنما قال ذلك لأنَّ : ماهية الكل غير مشتمل عليها . وجه القطع كما صرح به، فهذا البحث إن أورد بالنظر إلى مجموع الحركة الواقعة في مجموع الزمان لم يرد إذ الاتصاف بالمجموع في وقت ما كما ستعرفه في المقصد الثاني، وإن أورد بالنظر إلى جزء من الحركة الواقع في جزء من الزمان لم يتجه أيضاً، إذ لا بد بعد كل جزء يفرض من الحركة جزء آخر منها كيف لا ولو لم يبق شيء من الحركة بالقوة لكان المتحرك حاصلاً في المنتهى، وحينئذ لا حركة كما يظهر بأدنى تأمل، وأما في الثاني فلثبوتها لأجزاء الزمان والزمان وإن لم يكن كمالاً للجسم إذ ليس صفة له لكن كمال للحركة، لأنه مقدار لها قائم بها لا يقال : الحيثية المذكورة معتبر في هذه الخاصة أيضاً، فيخرج الزمان إذ ليس حقيقته التأدي إلى الغير لأنا نقول : لا لاعتبارها ،هاهنا، إذ لا معنى لأن يقال : الحركة من حيث أن حقيقتها لأن يقال : الحركة من حيث أن حقيقتها هي التأدي إلى الغير يقتضي أن يكون شيء منها بالقوة، إذ لا دخل للحيثية المذكورة في هذا الاقتضاء، نعم يمكن أن يجاب بأن الذي الذي عد خاصة للحركة هو اقتضاء ما ذكر لذاته واقتضاء الزمان له لو سلم إنما هو بواسطة انطباقه على الحركة الغير القارة، وكونه مقداراً لها والتحقيق في الجواب أن يقال : الخاصة هي اقتضاء الحركة أن يكون شيء منها لمحلها بعينه بالقوة كما يدل عليه قوله : فهوية الحركة مستلزمة إلخ، وهذا لا يتحقق في الزمان لأنَّ محله ليس إلا الحركة لأنه مقدارها قائم بها، والجزء الذي هو بالقوة من الزمان ليس محله الحركة الأولى لأنها تنقضي بانقضاء الزمان، بل حركة أخرى فليتأمل . الثانية
His saying: ('and the second of them is that it necessitates, etc.') — concerning the existence of this specificity for motion and its being specific to it, assuming its existence, there is investigation. As for the first, because motion ceases at the moment of arrival without doubt, and that moment is the boundary of the time of motion, so in the time of motion the body is moving while there is no potentiality after that time with respect to some parts of motion because the time is the entire time of motion. And it can be said: this specificity for motion means that it is moving as long as it does not reach the destination, for when it reaches it its motion ceases, and as long as it has not reached, something of motion remains in potentiality. So the essence of motion necessitates that its subject, when described by it, contains two potentialities: a potentiality relative to motion itself, and another relative to what is intended by it. As for the potentiality relative to the intended object, it is common without difference between motion in the sense of cutting (al-qaṭ') and motion in the sense of intermediacy (at-tawassuṭ): for the body as long as it is in the distance does not reach the end, and when it reaches it, no motion remains at all. As for the other potentiality, there is a difference between them: for motion in the sense of cutting, when the mover is described by it, some of its parts are in potentiality and some in actuality, so potentiality and actuality are in one thing. Motion in the sense of intermediacy, when it occurs, is actual, and there is no potentiality belonging to its essence but rather to its relation to the boundaries of the distance, and those relations are external to its essence, accidental to it, as you will see. Thus it has become clear to you that motion is a perfection in the mentioned sense for the body that is in potentiality in that it contradicts some parts of motion being in potentiality, for every part supposed of motion is considered motion in potentiality until it reaches the end. His saying: 'so the essence (of motion)' — i.e., its personal essence existing in the external, and he only said that because the essence of the whole is not included in it. The aspect of cutting (al-qaṭ') as he stated, so this investigation if considered with respect to the total motion occurring in the total time is not valid, because the description with the total at some time as you will know in the second objective. And if considered with respect to a part of motion occurring in a part of time, it also does not apply, for after every supposed part of motion there must be another part of it; how not, for if nothing of motion remained in potentiality, the mover would be at the end, and then there would be no motion, as appears with minimal reflection. As for the second (i.e., the second aspect), because it holds for the parts of time, and time, even if it is not a perfection for the body since it is not an attribute of it, is a perfection for motion because it is a measure inhering in it. It cannot be said: the mentioned aspect is considered in this specificity too, so time is excluded because its reality is not leading to another, for we say: no, its consideration here is not, for it is meaningless to say: motion from the aspect that its reality is leading to another necessitates that something of it be in potentiality, since the mentioned aspect has no role in this necessitation. Yes, it can be answered that what is considered specific to motion is the necessitation of what mentioned for its essence, and the necessitation of time for it, if granted, is only by virtue of its correspondence to non-fixed motion and its being a measure for it. The verification in the answer is to say: the specificity is motion's necessitation that something of it be in potentiality for its subject itself, as indicated by his saying: 'so the essence of motion necessitates, etc.', and this is not realized in time because its subject is only motion, since it is its measure subsisting in it, and the part that is in potentiality from time does not have motion as its subject because it expires with the expiration of time, but rather another motion, so reflect. The second.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the second special property of motion: that motion requires something in potentiality. The commentator begins by quoting the matn's second property and then discusses its existence as specific to motion, assuming it holds. He first addresses a potential objection: at the moment of arrival, motion ceases, and that moment is the boundary of time, so during motion the body is moving without potentiality after that time regarding some parts of motion. This is answered by specifying that as long as the mover has not reached the destination, something of motion remains in potentiality. The essence of motion necessitates that its subject has two potentialities: one relative to motion itself, and another relative to the intended goal. The potentiality relative to the goal is common to both meanings of motion: 'cut' (القطع) and 'intermediate' (التوسط). For motion as cutting (transversing a distance), parts are partly actual and partly potential; for motion as intermediacy (being in the distance), it is wholly actual but has potentiality in its relations to the boundaries. The commentator then clarifies that motion is a perfection for the body that is in potentiality, and any part of motion is potential until the end. He then explains the phrase 'so the essence of motion' as the personal essence existing externally. He addresses a further investigation: if we consider the total motion in total time, the description of motion as having parts in potentiality fails; if we consider a part in a part of time, it also fails because after each part another must follow, otherwise the mover would be at the end and motion would cease. Finally, he discusses time: time is not a perfection for the body but for motion, and the property of requiring potentiality does not apply to time because time's reality is not 'leading to another'. The verification is that the specificity is motion's own necessitation for its subject, not time's, and the potential part of time belongs to a different motion. The passage ends with 'so reflect' and 'the second' indicating completion.

Key Terms
قطع
توسط
قوة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both cut and intermediate motion have parts that are partly potential and partly actual.
Correction: For cut motion, parts are partly potential and partly actual; for intermediate motion, the motion itself is fully actual, with potentiality only in its relation to the distance.

Checkpoint
What is the locus of potentiality in motion as intermediacy (tawassuṭ)?
A) In the motion's own essence
B) In its relation to the distance boundaries
C) In its parts
D) None

Key Takeaway
The two meanings of motion differ fundamentally: cut motion has immanent potentiality, while intermediate motion has relational potentiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which sense are you living your spiritual journey? Are you like 'cut motion,' where only some parts of your devotion are actual and others remain potential, waiting for you to take action? What specific part of your faith remains in potentiality that you know you must actualize today?
?
Consider your relationship with Allah: is your intention (niyya) like 'intermediate motion'—fully actual in essence but still progressing toward a higher goal? Or do you treat your deeds as merely partial steps, always 'potential' until some future time? How does this distinction challenge your current sincerity?
?
You read that potentiality in intermediate motion is only relational, not essential. Are you allowing external circumstances—like your job, health, or relationships—to define your potential in deen? If potential is only relational, what is it in your life that you are falsely treating as essential and thus delaying your spiritual motion?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
10 min
Why Time Does Not Possess the Property of Potentiality
Commentator explains that the property is exclusive to motion, not time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the property of necessitating potentiality is specific to motion and does not apply to time, despite time being a measure of motion.

Core Idea
Time is a measure of motion, not a subject of motion, so it cannot have the same potentiality.

The commentator addresses an important nuance: time also has parts, so one might think time also requires potentiality. He explains that time is not a perfection for the body but for motion, and the potential part of time belongs to a different motion. The verification is that the property is motion's own necessitation for its subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on time
Read the passage from 'qāla fahūwa...' to the end. Notice the discussion about time being a measure.
2
Understand why time fails the property
The commentator argues that time's reality is not 'leading to another' and that the property of potentiality in motion comes from its essence, not from time.
Time only has potentiality because it corresponds to non-fixed motion.
3
Verify the specificity
The commentator concludes by affirming that the property is motion's own necessitation for its subject, and time cannot realize it because its subject is motion itself, and the potential part of time belongs to another motion.
Reflect on the final 'so reflect'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time also has the property of necessitating potentiality because it has parts.
Correction: Time's parts correspond to different motions; potentiality in time is derivative and belongs to a different motion.

Checkpoint
Why does time not have the property of necessitating potentiality in the same way as motion?
A) Because time is not a perfection for the body
B) Because the potential part of time belongs to a different motion
C) Because time is a measure
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The property of necessitating potentiality is intrinsic to motion alone, not to time, because time's potentiality relates to a different motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that time itself will bring you closer to Allah, without actively moving in obedience? How does this lesson expose that assumption? Commit to one action today that turns your wait into motion.
?
When you look at your life, do you see yourself as a subject of motion (changing through deeds) or just a container of time? What would change if you saw every moment as an opportunity for motion that carries its own potentiality?
?
The commentator says that time's potentiality belongs to another motion. In your spiritual journey, are you relying on the momentum of past motions or initiating new ones? What specific new motion will you start today?

beginner

Rarefaction and Condensation: The Water Example
Defines rarefaction/condensation and proves them via water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the freezing and melting of water empirically demonstrates rarefaction and condensation without addition or separation of parts.

Core Idea
Water's volume change proves rarefaction and condensation are real.

This lesson introduces the definitions of rarefaction and condensation, then presents a simple but powerful proof using the everyday phenomenon of water freezing and melting. You will see how this example establishes that volume can change without adding or losing parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Start by reading the opening where rarefaction and condensation are defined. Notice the key phrases: 'increase in volume without another body joining' and 'decrease without separation.'
These definitions are crucial for understanding the proofs.
2
Examine the water proof
Read the water example carefully. Observe how the text argues that when water freezes and shrinks, no part separates, and when it melts, no part is added. Ask yourself: how does this show both rarefaction and condensation?
The text uses the terms 'without separation' (bila infisal) and 'without addition' (bila indimam).
3
Connect the proof to the definitions
Reflect on how the water example fits the definitions: shrinkage without separation is condensation, expansion without addition is rarefaction. The example verifies both.
This proof establishes the reality (āniyya) of these phenomena, not their cause.

والأول إما أن يكون بانضمام شيء أو لا، والثاني إما أن يكون بانفصال شيء أو لا ( الأول : التخلخل وهو ازدياد حجم الجسم من غير أن ينضم إليه جسم آخر ويثبته ) أي يدل على ثبوته ( أن الماء إذا انجمد صغر حجمه وإذا ذاب عاد إلى حجمه الأول فبين أي ظاهر مكشوف ( أنه لم يكن انفصل عنه جزء حيث صغر حجمه (ثم عاد ذلك الجزء أو ما يساويه إليه حين عاد هو إلى حجمه الأول بل صغر حجمه بلا انفصال ثم ازداد بلا انضمام فتحقق التخلخل والتكاثف فيه (وأيضاً فالقارورة) الضيقة الرأس ) تكب على الماد فلا يدخلها أصلاً فإذا) مصت مصاً قوياً وسد رأسها بالأصبع بحيث لا يتصل برأسها هواء من خارج ) ثم كبت عليه دخلها وبهذا الطريق يملؤون الرشاشات الطويلة الأعناق الضيقة المنافذ جداً بماء الورد ( وما ذلك ) الدخول لخلاء) حدث فيها ) بأن يخرج المص منها بعض الهواء ويبقى مكان ذلك البعض الخارج خالياً ( لامتناعه ) على رأيهم ( بل لأن المص أخرج بعض الهواء و(أحدث في الهواء ) الباقي ( تخلخلاً فكبر حجمه ) بحيث شغل مكان الخارج أيضاً ( ثم أوجد فيه ) أي في ذلك الهواء المتخلخل ( البرد ) الذي في الماء ( تكاثفا فصغر حجمه ) أو عاد بطبعه إلى مقداره الذي كان له قبل المص ( فدخل فيه ) أي في ذلك الزجاج ( الماء ضرورة امتناع الخلاء فثبت هاهنا التخلخل والتكاثف معاً أيضاً (فهذا) الذي ذكرناه في إثبات التخلخل ( يعطي ) ويثبت (آنيته ) وتحققه، ولا يفيد العلم بعلته
قوله : ( وأيضاً فالقارورة إلخ) وأيضاً الآنية إذا ملئت وسد رأسها وغليت فعند الغليان تنصدع الآنية، وما ذلك إلا لأن الغليان يفيد تخلخلاً في الماء وازدياداً في حجمه بحيث لا تسعه الآنية فتنصدع.
قوله : ( أو عاد بطبعه إلخ يرد على ما ذكره المصنف أنه يقتضي أن لو كبت على الماء الحار لم يدخلها لعدم البرد الموجب للتكاثف، فأورد الشارح المحقق قوله : أو عاد بطبعه إلخ دفعاً للسؤال عن أصل المسألة .
The first [aspect] is either by the joining of something or not, and the second is either by the separation of something or not. The first is rarefaction (al-takhalul), which is the increase in the volume of a body without another body joining it. And it is proven by the fact that water, when it freezes, its volume shrinks, and when it melts, it returns to its original volume. So it is clear and manifest that no part separated from it when its volume shrank. Then that part or its equivalent returned to it when it returned to its original volume. Rather, its volume shrank without separation and then increased without addition, so rarefaction and condensation are verified in it. Also, a narrow-necked bottle: it is inverted over water and does not enter it at all. Then when it is sucked strongly and its mouth is sealed with a finger such that no air from outside connects to its mouth, then it is inverted over it, and it enters it. By this method they fill long-necked, very narrow-opening spray bottles with rosewater. That entry is not due to a vacuum occurring in it by the suction extracting some air and the place of that extracted part remaining empty, because of the impossibility of a vacuum (according to their view). Rather, because the suction extracted some air and produced rarefaction in the remaining air, so its volume increased such that it also occupied the place of the extracted part. Then the cold of the water produced condensation in that rarefied air, so its volume shrank, or it naturally returned to its amount before suction, so the water entered the glass by necessity of the impossibility of a vacuum. Thus rarefaction and condensation are both established here. That which we mentioned in proving rarefaction gives and establishes its actuality and reality, but does not give knowledge of its cause.
His statement: '(Also the bottle, etc.)' Also, a vessel when it is filled and its mouth sealed and boiled, then at the time of boiling the vessel cracks. That is only because boiling produces rarefaction in the water and an increase in its volume such that the vessel cannot contain it, so it cracks.
His statement: '(Or it naturally returned, etc.)' responds to what the author mentioned, that it would imply that if it were inverted over hot water, it would not enter due to the absence of cold that causes condensation. So the verifying commentator brought his statement 'or it naturally returned, etc.' to repel the question about the original issue.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage provides empirical proofs for the existence of rarefaction (takhalul) and condensation (takathuf), which are the first aspect of motion in quantity. It begins by stating that the first aspect (increase) is either by addition of something or without addition, and the second (decrease) is either by separation or without separation. Rarefaction is defined as increase in volume without addition of another body.

Two proofs are given. First proof: Water when frozen shrinks in volume, and when melted returns to its original volume. This shows that no part separated during shrinkage, and no part was added during expansion. Thus rarefaction and condensation occur. Second proof: A narrow-necked bottle is inverted over water but does not fill because air is trapped. When the bottle is suctioned strongly and its mouth sealed, then inverted, water enters. The explanation is not due to a vacuum (void) since vacuums are impossible according to these philosophers, but because suction extracts some air, causing the remaining air to rarely and expand, occupying the extracted space. Then the cold of the water condenses this rarefied air, shrinking it back, allowing water to enter by the necessity of avoiding a vacuum. Thus both rarefaction and condensation are demonstrated. The passage notes that these proofs establish the reality (āniyya) of rarefaction, not its ultimate cause (illa).

Two explanatory comments follow. The first adds another example: a sealed vessel filled with water cracks when boiled, due to rarefaction of water increasing volume. The second comment addresses an objection: if hot water were used, the cold needed for condensation would be absent, so the water would not enter. The commentator responds by noting that the rarefied air could return to its original volume naturally (by its own nature), not just by cold. This defends the proof against the objection.

Key Terms
تخلخل
تكاثف
ازدياد الحجم
انضمام
انفصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that water shrinks because its parts separate (e.g., some water leaves) or that it expands because new parts are added.
Correction: The text explicitly states that no part separates (lam yakun infasala 'anhu juz') and no part is added (bila indimam), so the volume change is due to rarefaction and condensation themselves.

Checkpoint
What does the water example demonstrate about condensation?
A) Water loses parts when freezing.
B) Water shrinks without loss of parts.
C) Water expands by gaining parts.
D) Water's volume change is due to vacuum.

Key Takeaway
The freezing and melting of water proves that rarefaction and condensation are real phenomena, as volume changes occur without addition or removal of parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen water freeze and melt countless times. Did you ever recognize it as a proof of something hidden? What does that tell you about your own attentiveness to the signs Allah places before you? Will you now look at ordinary events with the eyes of a believer seeking proofs?
?
The proof in this lesson denies that parts are added or removed. In your life, do you tend to attribute change to external additions (blaming circumstances) or internal transformations? Could your own personal changes (e.g., in character or faith) happen through rarefaction and condensation of the soul without new 'parts'? What would that mean for how you pursue growth?

The Bottle Experiment: Proving Rarefaction and Condensation
Proves rarefaction and condensation via bottle experiment and addresses vacuum objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the narrow-necked bottle experiment demonstrates rarefaction and condensation, and why the explanation does not rely on a vacuum.

Core Idea
The bottle experiment shows rarefaction and condensation without requiring a vacuum.

This lesson presents the second proof using a narrow-necked bottle and suction. It not only confirms rarefaction and condensation but also addresses a common misconception that a vacuum is created. You'll see how the interplay of rarefaction and condensation allows water to enter the bottle by necessity of avoiding a vacuum.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Read the description of the bottle experiment. Note how the bottle is inverted over water, then suction is applied and the mouth sealed.
The bottle is narrow-necked so that air normally blocks water entry.
2
Follow the explanation
The text explains that water enters not because of a vacuum, but because suction creates rarefaction in the remaining air, expanding its volume, then cold condenses it, shrinking it, allowing water in.
The phrase 'li amtinā' al-khalā'' (due to impossibility of vacuum) shows the negation of vacuum.
3
Consider the objection and response
The passage includes an objection about hot water lacking cold to cause condensation. The commentator responds that the rarefied air can return to its original volume naturally, not just by cold.
This reinforces that the proof does not depend on an external source of cold.

?

What would change in your heart today if you truly accepted that?

Why Matter Lacks Inherent Magnitude
Explains the possibility of rarefaction by matter's absence of inherent magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prime matter's lack of inherent magnitude is the metaphysical basis for the possibility of rarefaction and condensation.

Core Idea
Matter's indifference to magnitude makes rarefaction possible.

This passage dives into the metaphysical underpinnings of rarefaction. The author argues that prime matter (hayula), the substratum of physical bodies, has no magnitude in itself. This absence allows it to take on different magnitudes equally, providing the foundation for expansion and contraction without the addition or subtraction of parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the argument structure: a statement of the cause, an explanation why matter has no magnitude, and an objection and answer.
The passage ends abruptly; the core argument is still complete.
2
Identify the three segments
Segment 1: The cause (li-miyya) is that matter has no magnitude. Segment 2: Explanation – magnitude depends on substantial continuity, which matter lacks. Segment 3: Objection and answer – matter can still be specified to a magnitude? No, because it is indeterminate.
The objection and answer are concise.
3
Reflect on the metaphysical significance
Consider why this matters for physics and theology: if matter had a fixed magnitude, rarefaction would be impossible. This shows how Aristotelian concepts support the observed phenomenon of expansion.
This connects to broader cosmological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وأما لميتة ) أي لمية إمكانه وصحته كما ستعرفه) فهو أن الهيولى ليس لها في ذاتها (مقدار وما لا مقدار له في حد ذاته كان نسبته إلى المقادير كلها على سواء ( فقد
قوله : ليس لها في ذاتها مقدار بناء على ما تقرر في محله أن الهيولى في نفسها ليس بمتصل ولا منفصل ولا واحد ولا متعدد والمقدار تابع للاتصال الجوهري أعني الصورة الجسمية في نفسها ليس له مقدار أصلاً، فما قيل بأنه يجوز أن لا يكون لها مقدار شخص في نفسها فلا ينافي أن يختص بدرجة من المقادير، وأجيب بأنه لا يضر لأن الهيولى إذا لم يقض
قوله : (كان سبته إلى المقادير (كلها إن قيل : مرادهم بأن الهيولى لا مقدار لها في نفسها
(As for the why-ness (li-miyya), i.e., the reason for its possibility and validity as you will know,) it is that prime matter (al-hayula) does not have in itself magnitude. And that which has no magnitude in itself, its relation to all magnitudes is equal. So
His statement: 'It does not have in itself a magnitude' is based on what has been established in its place: that prime matter in itself is neither continuous nor discrete, neither one nor multiple. Magnitude is dependent on substantial continuity, i.e., the corporeal form. In itself it has no magnitude at all. So what was said—that it is possible that it does not have an individual magnitude in itself, so that does not preclude its being specified with a degree of magnitudes—was answered that it does not harm because if prime matter does not determine...
His statement: 'Its relation to all magnitudes is equal'—if it is said: their meaning is that prime matter has no magnitude in itself.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage provides the metaphysical explanation for the possibility of rarefaction, namely that prime matter (hayula) has no inherent magnitude. It begins by indicating that the cause (li-miyya) of the possibility and validity of rarefaction is the fact that matter in itself lacks magnitude. Since it has no magnitude of its own, it can take on any magnitude equally; its relation to all magnitudes is the same. 

The commentary then expands on this: matter in itself (fī dhātihā) is neither continuous nor discrete, neither one nor many. Magnitude (miqdār) is dependent on substantial continuity (al-ittiṣāl al-jawharī), i.e., the corporeal form (al-ṣūra al-jismiyya). Therefore, matter itself has no magnitude at all. An objection is raised: even if matter has no individual magnitude in itself, that does not prevent it from being specified with a particular degree of magnitude. The answer is that this does not harm the argument, because matter, lacking intrinsic determination, can undergo expansion or contraction—the basis for rarefaction and condensation. The passage ends abruptly, but the core idea is clear: the possibility of volume change without addition or subtraction of parts is grounded in matter's indifference to magnitude.

Key Terms

مقدار
اتصال جوهري
صورة جسمية
لمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prime matter has a basic or minimal magnitude in itself.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that matter has no magnitude at all in itself; magnitude is a property of the corporeal form, not matter.

Checkpoint
Why does prime matter (hayula) allow for rarefaction?
A) Because it is made of tiny particles that can separate.
B) Because it has no inherent magnitude, so it can take on different sizes.
C) Because it expands when heated.
D) Because it is composed of atoms.

Key Takeaway
Matter's lack of inherent magnitude is the metaphysical basis for rarefaction and condensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If prime matter has no inherent magnitude, then your very body's size and shape are not essential to you. What does that say about your attachment to your physical appearance? Are you willing to let go of that attachment as something accidental?
?
The passage teaches that matter's relation to all magnitudes is equal. Do you treat certain aspects of your life—like wealth, status, or health—as if they have fixed magnitudes that define you? How can you cultivate a sense of detachment similar to matter's indifference?
?
If the possibility of rarefaction and condensation proves that matter is contingent and dependent on God for its determinations, what does that imply for your own self-sufficiency? Are you living as if you control your own determinations, or are you acknowledging God's absolute control over every aspect of your existence?

Matter's Indeterminate Magnitude as Cause of Rarefaction and Condensation
Explains the causal role of matter in rarefaction and condensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prime matter's lack of inherent magnitude makes rarefaction and condensation possible, and why not all bodies exhibit these changes.

Core Idea
Matter has no size of its own, so it can change size.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains the underlying cause (limiyya) of rarefaction and condensation. The author, al-Jurjani, argues that the possibility of these phenomena lies in the nature of prime matter (hayula) which has no fixed magnitude. However, he also clarifies that other factors can prevent actual change. We will examine both the enabling condition and the exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the emphasis on the word 'limiyya' (why-ness) and the distinction between proof of existence and explanation of cause.
The passage distinguishes between proving that rarefaction exists (anni) and explaining why it is possible (limi).
2
Identify the core concept
Find the sentence: 'فإن الهيولى ليس لها في ذاتها مقدار'. Understand that matter has no inherent magnitude, so it can receive different magnitudes equally.
This is the key premise: lack of fixed size enables change.
3
Understand the exceptions
Read the part about preventers (man'): specific forms in heavens and part-hood in elements. These explain why not all bodies undergo rarefaction/condensation despite matter's flexibility.
The corrector (musahhih) is necessary but not sufficient.

فهذا الذي ذكرناه في إثبات التخلخل يعطي ويثبت آنيته وتحققه، ولا يفيد العلم بعلته. وأما لميته أي لمية إمكانه وصحته كما ستعرفه فهو أن الهيولى ليس لها في ذاتها مقدار وما لا مقدار له في حد ذاته كان نسبته إلى المقادير كلها على سواء. فقد تكون الهيولى في بعض الأشياء كما في العناصر قابلة للمقادير المختلفة تتوارد تلك المقادير عليها بحسب ما يعدها من الأسباب الخارجة عن ذاتها لذلك الوارد عليها من تلك المقادير المختلفة فإذا ورد عليها مقدار أكبر مما كان لها ثبت التخلخل وإذا ورد ما هو أصغر منه ثبت التكاثف. ولا يلزم من كون الهيولى لا مقدار لها في ذاتها أن يكون الكل كذلك أي أن يكون كل الأجسام بحيث تتوارد عليه المقادير المختلفة على سبيل البدل لجواز أن يختص البعض من الأجسام بمقدار معين لا يتعداه إلى غيره لأسباب منفصلة تقتضي اختصاصه بذلك المقدار أو يختص البعض بمقدار معين لأن مادته لا تقبل إلا ذلك المقدار المعين كما هو رأيهم في الأفلاك فإن كل واحد منها له مادة مخالفة في الحقيقة لمادة الآخر، وكل مادة منها لا تقبل إلا مقداراً مخصوصاً عند بعضهم. وبالجملة فهذا الذي ذكرناه من حال الهيولى مصحح للتخلخل والتكاثف ولا يلزم من تحققه أي من تحقق المصحح تحقق الأثر حتى يلزم ثبوت التخلخل والتكاثف في جميع الأجسام، بل يجوز أن يكون مع المصحح مانع يمتنع به تحقق الأثر كالصور النوعية في الأجسام الفلكية فإن كل واحدة منها تقتضي لزومها لهيولاها واختصاصها بمقدار معين وكالجزئية في الأجسام العنصرية فإن الجزء ما دام جزءاً يستحيل أن يكون مقداره مساوياً لمقدار كله، أما إذا انفصل أمكن أن يتصف بمقدار الكل، ولا يجوز الانفصال في أجزاء الفلك عندهم بخلاف العناصر
So this which we have mentioned in the proof of rarefaction gives and establishes its actuality and verification, and does not provide knowledge of its cause. As for its why-ness, i.e., the reason for its possibility and soundness as you will know, it is that hayula (matter) has in its essence no magnitude, and what has no magnitude in itself has a relation to all magnitudes equally. So hayula may exist in some things, as in the elements, receptive to different magnitudes, those magnitudes succeeding upon it according to what prepares it from causes external to its essence for that which comes upon it from those different magnitudes. So when a larger magnitude comes upon it than what it had, rarefaction is established, and when a smaller comes upon it, condensation is established. And it does not follow from the fact that hayula has no magnitude in itself that everything is like that, i.e., that all bodies are such that different magnitudes succeed upon them by way of substitution, because it is possible that some bodies are specified with a fixed magnitude that does not go beyond to another, due to separate causes requiring its specification with that magnitude, or some bodies are specified with a fixed magnitude because their matter does not accept except that fixed magnitude, as is their view regarding the heavens, for each of them has a matter different in reality from the matter of another, and each matter does not accept except a particular magnitude according to some of them. In short, this state of hayula is a corrector (musahhih) for rarefaction and condensation, and it does not follow from its actuality, i.e., from the actuality of the corrector, the actuality of the effect, such that the establishment of rarefaction and condensation in all bodies would be necessary. Rather, it is possible that along with the corrector there is a preventer (mani') by which the effect is prevented, such as the specific forms in the heavenly bodies, for each of them requires its subsistence upon its hayula and its specification with a specific magnitude, and like the part-hood (juz'iyya) in elemental bodies, for as long as a part is a part, it is impossible for its magnitude to equal the magnitude of the whole, but when it separates, it can be characterized by the magnitude of the whole. And separation is not permissible in the parts of the heavens according to them, unlike the elements.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage explains the underlying cause (limiyya) of the possibility of rarefaction and condensation. It states that hayula (prime matter) has no inherent magnitude, so it can receive different magnitudes. This makes rarefaction and condensation possible: when a larger magnitude comes upon matter, volume increases (rarefaction); when a smaller, volume decreases (condensation). However, not all bodies actually undergo these changes because some bodies have other factors that fix their magnitude. For example, the celestial spheres have forms that permanently lock their matter into a specific magnitude, so they do not rarefy or condense. In elemental bodies, parts cannot expand to fill the whole while they are parts, but they can when separated. Thus, the lack of inherent magnitude in matter is a necessary condition (musahhih) for rarefaction and condensation, but not sufficient; there may be preventers (mani'). This nuanced view avoids the false implication that all bodies must be able to change volume.

المقدار

المانع
الصور النوعية
الجزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If matter has no inherent magnitude, then all bodies can change volume.
Correction: Matter's lack of magnitude is a necessary condition, but not sufficient; there may be preventers (e.g., celestial forms) that fix magnitude.

Checkpoint
What is the role of hayula (matter) in making rarefaction and condensation possible?
A) Hayula has a fixed magnitude that changes by addition or subtraction of parts
B) Hayula has no inherent magnitude, so it can receive different magnitudes
C) Hayula is a vacuum that allows expansion
D) Hayula actively causes volume changes by its own power

Key Takeaway
The possibility of rarefaction and condensation is rooted in matter's lack of fixed magnitude, but actual change depends on the absence of preventers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your heart is receptive to both good and bad, like matter is to all magnitudes. What 'external cause' are you deliberately inviting to shape your spiritual magnitude today? When you notice a preventer—a habit or excuse—blocking you from expanding in taqwa, do you break free or remain fixed like a celestial sphere?
?
The passage teaches that matter's lack of fixed magnitude makes change possible, but preventers can stop it. What 'specific form' (an image of yourself, a role, a sin you hold onto) keeps you locked at a certain spiritual size, refusing to receive a larger measure of mercy or a smaller measure of pride? Are you willing to let that form dissolve?
?
Just as part-hood prevents an elemental part from equalling the whole, your identification with a limited 'part' of your story (a past mistake, a current role) may prevent you from realizing your full capacity in Allah's sight. What 'part' are you clinging to that stops you from becoming the whole person of worship He calls you to be?

Refutation of the Latency Theory of Heat and Cold
Jurjani refutes latency theory and three false views on heat/cold change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the latency theory of heat and cold is false according to Jurjani, and the alternative explanation through generation and corruption.

Core Idea
Qualities change by generation and corruption, not by hidden parts emerging.

Jurjani tackles a key debate in natural philosophy: how do qualities like heat and cold arise in a body? Some claimed that parts with these qualities are latent and simply become manifest. Jurjani refutes this with clear examples and then addresses three variants of the mixture theory. The passage establishes the principle that change occurs through generation (kawn) and corruption (fasad), not emergence from latency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style and the use of logical examples.
2
Understand the two refutations of latency
Identify the two examples used to refute latency: the hand in cold water and the spark on a sulfur mountain. Ask yourself how each example disproves latency.
The first shows no perceptible heat inside, the second shows fire cannot be latent.
3
Identify the three false views
The passage mentions three views of those who accept mixture (takhleet). List them and notice how each is refuted with a brief argument.
These views are about how heat/cold change occurs via separation, external entry, or transmutation.
4
Grasp the conclusion on generation
Understand that the correct explanation is generation (kawn) and corruption (fasad), not emergence of latent parts or transmutation without intermediate stages.
Water must become air before it can become fire, as a later discussion will show.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن صح كمون الأجزاء الحارة في الماء البارد جداً ( بل وفي الجمد ) أيضاً ( وأنه ضروري البطلان ومع ذلك ) تنبهك على بطلانه، ونقول : إن صح كمون الأجزاء الحارة في الماء البارد ( فمن أدخل يده فيه كان يجب أن يحس بحره ) أي بحر باطنه (أو يقل برده) بحيث يدرك صاحب اليد التفاوت وهو باطل، إذ ربما يجد باطنه أبرد من ظاهره ( وأيضاً فإنَّ شرراً إذا صادف جبلاً من كبريت صير كله ناراً مشاهدة ) ونعلم بالضرورة بأنَّ ذلك الذي نشاهده فيه من النار كله لم يكن كامناً فيه كيف ولو كان في ذلك الجبل بعض من تلك الأجزاء النارية لا حرقته، فوجب أن يكون حدوث النار فيه بطريق الكون دون البروز من الكمون وذهب جماعة من القائلين بالتخليط إلى أن الحار مثلاً، إذا صار بارداً فقد فارقه الأجزاء الحارة، ومنهم من قال : الجسم إنما يصير حاراً بدخول أجزاء نارية فيه من خارج ومنهم من قال : ينقلب بعض أجزائه ناراً ويختلط بالأجزاء المائية، فهذه الطائفة معترفون بالكون والفساد دون الاستحالة وهذه الأقوال أيضاً باطلة كما لا يشتبه على ذي فطنة. يبطل الأول بمشاهدة الماء على حاله، والثاني بلزوم إطفاء الماء لتلك الأجزاء النارية، والثالث بلزوم الانطفاء ببرد الماء ورطوبته أو مفارقة النارية صاعدة بطبعها، على أنَّ الماء لا يصير ناراً إلا بعد صيرورته هواء كما ستعرفه في بحث الكون والفساد وحينئذ يتصعد بطريق البخار.
If the latency of hot parts in very cold water (and even in ice) is accepted, and that it is necessarily false, (and yet) it alerts you to its falsehood. We say: if the latency of hot parts in cold water is valid, then whoever puts his hand in it must feel its heat—that is, the heat of its interior—or [feel] its coldness so that the owner of the hand perceives the difference, but this is false, since perhaps he finds its interior colder than its exterior. And also, if a spark encounters a mountain of sulfur, it turns it all into observed fire, and we know necessarily that all the fire we observe in it was not latent in it; otherwise, if there were some of those fiery parts in that mountain, it would not have burned it. So it is necessary that the occurrence of fire in it is by way of generation (kawn), not emergence from latency. And a group of those who hold mixture (takhleet) went to the view that when hot, for example, becomes cold, the hot parts part from it. And some of them said: a body only becomes hot by the entry of fiery parts into it from outside. And some of them said: some of its parts are transmuted into fire and mix with the watery parts. So this group acknowledges generation and corruption (kawn wa fasad) without transmutation (istihala). And these statements are also false, as is not hidden from one of intelligence. The first is refuted by observing water in its [same] state; the second by the necessity of water extinguishing those fiery parts; the third by the necessity of extinction by the cold and moisture of water or the fiery parts ascending by their nature, since water does not become fire except after becoming air, as you will learn in the discussion of generation and corruption, and then it ascends by way of vapor.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage refutes the theory of latency of qualities and presents three false views on heat/cold change. The author first argues against latency by two examples: (1) if hot parts were latent in cold water, the hand would feel both hot and cold, but it does not; (2) if a spark hits a sulfur mountain, the resulting fire cannot be latent because it would have already burned it. Therefore, fire arises by generation (kawn), not emergence. Then he lists three views of those who accept mixture (takhleet): (a) hot parts leave, (b) fiery parts enter from outside, (c) some parts transmute to fire. He notes that the third group admits generation and corruption but denies transmutation (istihala). All three are refuted: (a) water remains the same, (b) water would extinguish entering fire, (c) water cannot directly become fire without first becoming air and then vapor. Thus, change is by generation and corruption.

فساد

Misconception: Heat and cold result from latent particles becoming manifest.
Correction: Change of qualities occurs by generation (kawn) and corruption (fasad), not by emergence of latent parts.

Checkpoint

B) A spark hitting a sulfur mountain
C) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The latency theory of qualities is false; change occurs through generation and corruption, not by latent parts emerging.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own spiritual state, do you assume that good qualities are 'hidden' inside you and just need to emerge, or do you recognize that they require generation through effort and divine grace? How does the refutation of latency theory push you to take concrete action rather than wait for hidden goodness to appear?
?
The passage shows that water must become air before it can become fire—change happens step by step, not by skipping stages. In your journey of faith, are you trying to skip necessary stages of growth? What 'air' stage might you be neglecting that is essential before you can become 'fire' in your devotion?
?
The mixture theorists accept generation and corruption but deny transmutation—they hold a partial truth. Is there an area where you accept only part of a truth because the full truth requires a change you are unwilling to undergo? What would it mean for you to fully embrace the reality of transformation (istihala) in your life?

Positional Motion of the Celestial Sphere
Jurjani defines positional motion and notes a historical precedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of positional motion (al-wad') and how it differs from spatial motion, especially in the context of the celestial sphere.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere's motion is positional, not spatial—it changes orientation, not location.

In this passage, Jurjani defines the third category of motion: positional motion. He uses the example of the celestial sphere rotating on its axis: it does not change place, but the orientation of its parts relative to external things changes. This clarifies why philosophers consider the sphere's motion a distinct type from local motion. The passage also notes that al-Farabi recognized this before Ibn Sina.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Carefully read the Arabic passage that defines positional motion and its contrast with spatial motion. Then read the literal translation.
Notice that the sphere's motion does not involve exiting from one place to another.
2
Identify the key change
Find where the text explains what changes during the sphere's motion: the relation of its parts to external things (containing or contained). This change of relation results in a new configuration (hay'a), which is position.
3
Note the historical remark
Observe the comment about Ibn Sina and al-Farabi. The text clarifies that al-Farabi already described the sphere's motion as positional in his 'Uyun al-Masa'il.
This shows that the concept was not novel with Ibn Sina.
4
Compare with spatial motion
Reflect on the difference: spatial motion changes location, positional motion changes orientation without changing place. Consider why this distinction matters for understanding celestial motion.
This distinction is crucial for Islamic cosmology and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : من تلك المقولات ( الوضع كحركة الفلك على نفسه فإنه لا يخرج بهذه الحركة ( عن مكان إلى مكان ( لتكون حركته أينية (و) ) لكن ( يتبدل بها وضعه ( لأنه يتغير بها نسبة أجزائه إلى أمور خارجة عنه، إما حاوية وإما محوية، وإذا تغيرت تلك النسبة تغيرت الهيئة الحاصلة بسببها، وهي الوضع وكلام ابن سينا يوهم أنه الذي وقف على الحركة الوضعية دون من قبله من الحكماء، وليس الأمر كذلك فإنَّ الفارابي قال في عيون المسائل : حركات الأفلاك دورية وضعية ( وفي حركة كل جزء منه أي من الفلك حركة مكانية (نظر) وتأمل ( فمنهم من ) قال : لا بالفعل) بل بالفرض ( فكيف يتحرك ) في الخارج ما لا وجود له فيه (بل ذلك ) أي تحرك جزء الفلك مع كونه مفروضاً (أمر موهوم ومنهم من قال : بتبادل النصفين الأعلى والأسفل وتغير نسبة الأجزاء إلى الأمور الخارجة ) أو المحوية (مع عدم حركتها غير معقول لأن مبدأ هذا التبادل والتغير قائم بتلك الأجزاء، لا بالأمور الخارجة عنها ( فعليك بالتأمل حتى يظهر لك ما هو الحق من هذين القولين فإنّ قلت : إذا كان كل واحد من أجزائه متحركاً حركة مكانية على القول الثاني لزم أن يكون الفلك أيضاً متحركاً حركة مكانية . قلت : ليس يلزم من تحرك الأجزاء عن أمكنتها وأينها أن يكون مجموعها كذلك، وأما الكواكب فيه متحركة حركة أينية على القول بأن المكان هو البعد وتطلق الاستدارة على حركتها كما تطلق على حركة من جزء عليه تسامحاً والأولى ما في عبارة الشفاء اختصاص أجزاء المفروض بجهة مفروضة
The third [category] among those categories is position (al-wad'), like the motion of the celestial sphere upon itself, for it does not exit by this motion from place to place so that its motion would be spatial (ayniyya), but its position changes by it, because its relation [orientation] of its parts to external things changes, either containing or contained. And when that relation changes, the configuration (hay'a) resulting from it changes, which is position. And the statement of Ibn Sina gives the impression that he was the one who recognized positional motion, unlike the philosophers before him, but it is not so, for al-Farabi said in 'Uyun al-Masa'il: the motions of the celestial spheres are circular positional motions. And regarding the motion of every part of it—that is, of the sphere—[there is] a spatial motion? Consider and reflect. Some say: not actually, but hypothetically, so how can something that has no actual existence move in external reality? Rather, that—i.e., the movement of a part of the sphere while being hypothetical—is a fanciful matter. And some say: by the exchange of the upper and lower halves and the change of the relation of the parts to external things or contents, while their [actual] motion is unintelligible, because the principle of this exchange and change is based on those parts, not on external things. So you must reflect until the truth of these two statements becomes clear to you. For if you say: if every one of its parts moves with spatial motion according to the second statement, then it would be necessary that the sphere also moves with spatial motion. I say: it is not necessary from the movement of parts from their places and locations that the whole be likewise. And as for the planets, they move with spatial motion according to the view that place (al-makan) is the dimension (al-bu'd), and rotation is attributed to their motion just as it is attributed to the motion of a part of it—a leniency—and the better is what is in the phrase of al-Shifa': the assignment of hypothetical parts to a hypothetical direction.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage defines the category of position (al-wad') and discusses the motion of the celestial sphere. It explains that the sphere's motion is positional, not spatial, because it does not change place but rather changes the orientation of its parts relative to external things. The author notes that Ibn Sina is often credited with discovering positional motion, but al-Farabi already mentioned it. Then he presents two views on whether the parts of the sphere move locally: (1) some say the parts' movement is only hypothetical, not actual, so it is a fanciful notion; (2) others say the parts exchange positions (upper and lower halves) and change relations without actual local motion, but this is unintelligible because the principle of exchange lies in the parts themselves, not in external factors. The author advises reflection. An objection is raised: if each part moves spatially, the whole sphere would move spatially. The answer: the movement of parts does not necessitate the whole's movement; the whole may remain in place while parts change positions. Finally, planets are said to move spatially, though rotation is loosely attributed to them.

حركة وضعية
حركة مكانية
هيئة
نسبة

Key Takeaway
Positional motion changes the orientation of parts without changing place; the celestial sphere exemplifies this.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage corrects a misconception about Ibn Sina's priority. Two Views on Local Motion of the Sphere's Parts
Jurjani presents and evaluates two views on local motion of the sphere's parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly views on whether the parts of the celestial sphere undergo local motion, and the objection that the sphere would then move locally as a whole.

Core Idea
Do the parts of a rotating sphere move locally? Philosophers disagree, but the whole does not move locally even if parts do.

After defining the sphere's motion as positional, Jurjani raises a more subtle question: do the individual parts of the sphere move with spatial motion? He presents two positions: some say the parts' movement is only hypothetical, not actual; others explain it as an exchange of upper and lower halves without actual local motion. He then answers an objection: if each part moved spatially, the whole sphere would also move spatially—but he argues this does not necessarily follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the two views
Read the Arabic text that presents the two views on the parts' motion. Identify the first view (hypothetical motion) and the second view (exchange of halves).
The first view denies actual local motion; the second tries to explain apparent motion without actual displacement.
2
Understand the objection
Find the objection: if every part moves spatially, then the whole sphere must move spatially. Reflect on why this seems plausible.
3
Consider the answer
Read the author's response: the movement of parts does not entail the movement of the whole. The whole can remain in place while parts change positions relative to each other.
This is a key logical distinction.
4
Note the planets' motion
Observe the final remark about planets: they do move spatially according to some views, but rotation is attributed loosely.
This shows the complexity of celestial motions.
