التغير في شيء فلا يكون مستقلاً بالحركة. قوله : ( وقد عرفت ما فيه قد عرفت اندفاعه بما حررناه . قوله : ( وكذا الملك فإنه إلخ) هذا البيان غير تام لورود النقض بالمنع المذكور سابقاً، وفي
The Fourth Observatory - Third Objective: The occurrence of motion among the categories and their changes. Time (zaman) and it was objected that it is possible that the occurrence of when (mata) to time (zaman) is by itself, not by another time, like the occurrence of priority and posteriority. And he said in Al-Shifa: It seems that the transition in 'when' is instantaneous (duf'iyan), since the transition from year to year and from month to month occurs instantaneously, because the parts of time are continuous with each other and the common dividing point between them is the now (al-an). So if two times are assumed sharing a now, then before that now the subject continues with its 'when' relative to the first time, and after it continues with its 'when' relative to the second time, and that now is the end of the existence of the first and the beginning of the occurrence of the second, so there is no gradualness (tadrij) in the transition. And it is objected that the separating line between parts of distance are indivisible limits, so the transition from some of those parts to others is also instantaneous, but if two places are assumed with a divisible distance between them, the transition from one to the other is gradual; so likewise the case in transition from one time to another time between which there is a time, like dawn and sunset for example, then it is also gradual, not instantaneous. Then he said in Al-Shifa: It seems that the case of 'when' is like the case of relation (al-idafa), that the transition in it is secondary to the transition in something else among quantity (kam) or quality (kayf); so the change occurs in that thing first, and time is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of that change, and so due to it the change occurs in it. And to this he pointed by his saying: (It) i.e. 'when' (is like relation) in accepting motion in a secondary way, (because it is a relation dependent on its subject) so it is not independent (yastaqill) in concept (al-mafhumiyya) and change, and you have known what is in it. (And likewise possession (al-milk)) for it also is a relative category dependent on its subject in change and stability. (As for) the two categories (that... ) His statement: (Then he said in Al-Shifa: It seems that is (ilkh)) He intends that the transition occurring in 'when' is in two ways: one of them is due to the transition of time itself and its lack of stability, and that is instantaneous because time is one continuous in itself and its 'when' in this respect is one with no multiplicity let alone transition; and if a division is assumed, the common boundary between the two times is the now, so the transition occurring in the things that occur due to the transition of time and its lack of stability is instantaneous. And by this the aforementioned objection is repelled by his saying: And it is objected to it (ilkh) , and it is clear. And the second way is the transition occurring due to the change of the things occurring in it, and this transition since it involves two changes, the change in it is momentary; so the transition from 'when' is momentary, and if it is gradual, then it is gradual. So the summary of the shaykh's speech in Al-Najat is that motion does not occur in 'when' essentially (bi al-dhat), and in Al-Shifa that the transition occurring in it due to the change of time and its lack of stability is instantaneous, and the transition occurring in it due to the change of the things occurring in it is secondary to what is in the change, that there is no confusion from the shaykh's speech, and that he is not undecided about the occurrence of motion in 'when' as the wording of the text (al-matan) might suggest and as some deficient people believed. His statement: (So it is not independent in concept) meaning that the occurrence of something is not conceived except after considering the change in something, so it is not independent in motion. His statement: (And you have known what is in it; you have known its repulsion by what we have edited. His statement: (And likewise possession (al-milk) that is (ilkh)) This explanation is not complete because of a counterexample by the prevention mentioned earlier, and in (wa fi)...
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on whether motion occurs in the category of 'when' (متى) and 'possession' (الملك). It begins with an objection: perhaps the occurrence of 'when' to time is intrinsic and not dependent on another time, like priority and posteriority. The author then quotes Ibn Sīnā's al-Shifā', which suggests that transition in 'when' is instantaneous because time's parts are continuous, sharing the 'now' as a common boundary. However, a counter-objection is raised: if there is an interval between two times (like between dawn and sunset), the transition is gradual, not instantaneous. Ibn Sīnā then compares 'when' to 'relation' (الإضافة): motion in it is secondary, following a change in another category (quantity or quality). The commentary explains that transition in 'when' occurs in two ways: (1) due to the flux of time itself, which is instantaneous (because time is a continuum and its own 'when' is singular), and (2) due to changes in the things occurring in time, which may be gradual. Thus, motion in 'when' is not essential but secondary. The passage also notes that 'possession' (الملك) is similarly a relative category. The author concludes that Ibn Sīnā's position is unambiguous: motion does not occur in 'when' essentially, and the apparent confusion arises from misunderstanding the text.

Key Terms
متى
الزمان
الآن
دفعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that motion in 'when' is always gradual because time flows gradually.
Correction: Ibn Sina argues that due to the continuous nature of time and the role of the 'now', the transition from one time to another is instantaneous, not gradual.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the transition in 'when' from year to year instantaneous?
A) Because years are indivisible units
B) Because time's parts are continuous and share the 'now' as a boundary
C) Because the subject does not change
D) Because motion in 'when' is impossible

Key Takeaway
Transition in the category of 'when' can be instantaneous due to time's continuity and the 'now' acting as a boundary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the 'now' as the boundary between your past and future, are you aware that your spiritual state can change in an instant? Is there a specific 'now' in your life where you are avoiding an instantaneous decision that could transform your relationship with Allah?
?
Ibn Sina argues that transition in 'when' can be instantaneous because time's continuity hides the abrupt shift. In your own life, do you use the seeming continuity of time to delay repentance, thinking gradual change is sufficient? What would it mean for you to treat the next 'now' as the moment of decisive spiritual transition?
?
The 'now' is both an end and a beginning. If you knew that your current 'now' were the end of your time in this world, would you be ready to face its beginning in the next? What one thing would you immediately change to make that transition a success?

The Counter-Objection and Gradual Transition
Presents the counter-objection using spatial analogy to argue for gradual transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the counter-objection that transition in 'when' can be gradual when there is an interval between times.

Core Idea
When times are separated by an interval, transition in 'when' becomes gradual.

After the claim of instantaneity, a counter-objection arises: just as spatial transitions can be gradual when there is distance, temporal transitions can be gradual when there is an interval between two times. This lesson explores that objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the counter-objection
Read the Arabic passage that presents the objection using the analogy of spatial distance. Identify the key analogy: places with divisible distance lead to gradual transition.
2
Compare spatial and temporal transitions
The objection parallels how spatial motion from one place to another is gradual if the intervening distance is divisible. Similarly, transition from dawn to sunset is gradual because there is time between them.
3
Understand the consequence
This objection shows that transition in 'when' is not always instantaneous; it can be gradual when the two times are not adjacent.
This prepares for the resolution in the next lesson.

?

?

Motion in 'When' as Secondary
Presents Ibn Sina's comparison of 'when' to relation and the two-way resolution, plus the extension to possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the resolution that motion in 'when' is secondary, either following time's own flux (instantaneous) or changes in the subject (gradual), and that 'possession' shares this secondary nature.

Core Idea
Motion in 'when' and 'possession' is secondary, dependent on changes in other categories.

Ibn Sina resolves the debate by comparing 'when' to relation (الإضافة): motion in it is not essential but follows changes elsewhere. This lesson covers that comparison, the two ways of transition, and the similar status of 'possession' (الملك).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on relation
Read the Arabic passage where Ibn Sina likens 'when' to relation. Notice the claim that motion in it is secondary (تابع) and that it is not independent in concept.
2
Understand the two ways
The commentary explains two ways: (1) transition due to time's own flux (instantaneous), and (2) transition due to changes in the subject (gradual).
This reconciles the instantaneous and gradual views.
3
Apply to possession
The same reasoning applies to the category of 'possession' (الملك), which is also a relative category.

The Fourth Observatory - Third Objective: The occurrence of motion among the categories and their changes. Time (zaman) and it was objected that it is possible that the occurrence of when (mata) to time (zaman) is by itself, not by another time, like the occurrence of priority and posteriority. And he said in Al-Shifa: It seems that the transition in 'when' is instantaneous (duf'iyan), since the transition from year to year and from month to month occurs instantaneously, because the parts of time are continuous with each other and the common dividing point between them is the now (al-an). So if two times are assumed sharing a now, then before that now the subject continues with its 'when' relative to the first time, and after it continues with its 'when' relative to the second time, and that now is the end of the existence of the first and the beginning of the occurrence of the second, so there is no gradualness (tadrij) in the transition. And it is objected that the separating line between parts of distance are indivisible limits, so the transition from some of those parts to others is also instantaneous, but if two places are assumed with a divisible distance between them, the transition from one to the other is gradual; so likewise the case in transition from one time to another time between which there is a time, like dawn and sunset for example, then it is also gradual, not instantaneous. Then he said in Al-Shifa: It seems that the case of 'when' is like the case of relation (al-idafa), that the transition in it is secondary to the transition in something else among quantity (kam) or quality (kayf); so the change occurs in that thing first, and time is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of that change, and so due to it the change occurs in it. And to this he pointed by his saying: (It) i.e. 'when' (is like relation) in accepting motion in a secondary way, (because it is a relation dependent on its subject) so it is not independent (yastaqill) in concept (al-mafhumiyya) and change, and you have known what is in it. (And likewise possession (al-milk)) for it also is a relative category dependent on its subject in change and stability. (As for) the two categories (that... ) His statement: (Then he said in Al-Shifa: It seems that is (ilkh)) He intends that the transition occurring in 'when' is in two ways: one of them is due to the transition of time itself and its lack of stability, and that is instantaneous because time is one continuous in itself and its 'when' in this respect is one with no multiplicity let alone transition; and if a division is assumed, the common boundary between the two times is the now, so the transition occurring in the things that occur due to the transition of time and its lack of stability is instantaneous. And by this the aforementioned objection is repelled by his saying: And it is objected to it (ilkh) , and it is clear. And the second way is the transition occurring due to the change of the things occurring in it, and this transition since it involves two changes, the change in it is momentary; so the transition from 'when' is momentary, and if it is gradual, then it is gradual. So the summary of the shaykh's speech in Al-Najat is that motion does not occur in 'when' essentially (bi al-dhat), and in Al-Shifa that the transition occurring in it due to the change of time and its lack of stability is instantaneous, and the transition occurring in it due to the change of the things occurring in it is secondary to what is in the change, that there is no confusion from the shaykh's speech, and that he is not undecided about the occurrence of motion in 'when' as the wording of the text (al-matan) might suggest and as some deficient people believed. His statement: (So it is not independent in concept) meaning that the occurrence of something is not conceived except after considering the change in something, so it is not independent in motion. His statement: (And you have known what is in it; you have known its repulsion by what we have edited. His statement: (And likewise possession (al-milk) that is (ilkh)) This explanation is not complete because of a counterexample by the prevention mentioned earlier, and in (wa fi)...
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on whether motion occurs in the category of 'when' (متى) and 'possession' (الملك). It begins with an objection: perhaps the occurrence of 'when' to time is intrinsic and not dependent on another time, like priority and posteriority. The author then quotes Ibn Sīnā's al-Shifā', which suggests that transition in 'when' is instantaneous because time's parts are continuous, sharing the 'now' as a common boundary. However, a counter-objection is raised: if there is an interval between two times (like between dawn and sunset), the transition is gradual, not instantaneous. Ibn Sīnā then compares 'when' to 'relation' (الإضافة): motion in it is secondary, following a change in another category (quantity or quality). The commentary explains that transition in 'when' occurs in two ways: (1) due to the flux of time itself, which is instantaneous (because time is a continuum and its own 'when' is singular), and (2) due to changes in the things occurring in time, which may be gradual. Thus, motion in 'when' is not essential but secondary. The passage also notes that 'possession' (الملك) is similarly a relative category. The author concludes that Ibn Sīnā's position is unambiguous: motion does not occur in 'when' essentially, and the apparent confusion arises from misunderstanding the text.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That motion in 'when' is always instantaneous or always gradual.
Correction: The passage shows it can be either, depending on whether the change is due to time's own flux (instantaneous) or due to changes in the subject (gradual).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two ways transition can occur in the category of 'when'?
A) Essential and accidental
B) Instantaneous due to time's flux and gradual due to subject change
C) Primary and secondary
D) In time and in space

Key Takeaway
Motion in 'when' and 'possession' is secondary: it can be instantaneous when following time's own flux, or gradual when following changes in the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have experienced time slowing down or speeding up. How does this lesson's distinction between time's own flux (instantaneous) and changes in your life (gradual) explain that experience? Are you more aware of the underlying changes or just the feeling of time?
?
Consider something you possess that you are deeply attached to—a relationship, a skill, or an object. This lesson teaches that possession is a relative category dependent on its subject. How does that truth challenge your sense of ownership? Can you loosen your grip and see your possessions as secondary to your own changeable self?
?
Ibn Sina teaches that motion in 'when' is not essential but secondary. This means your perception of time passing is not about time itself but about changes in you and your world. If that is true, how might you stop feeling rushed or anxious about time? What would it mean to focus instead on the primary changes—your character, your devotion, your love for Allah—that are the real source of time's meaning for you?

Causality and Created Things

Why Continuous Motion Is Impossible in Action and Passion
Author refutes motion in action and passion with a logical argument and responds to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why continuous motion cannot occur in the categories of action (أن يفعل) and passion (أن ينفعل), and why a moment of rest is necessary between contrary states.

Core Idea
Continuous motion between opposites requires a rest because simultaneous direction to opposites is impossible.

In this passage, the author refutes the claim that motion occurs in the categories of action and passion. He argues that transitioning from heating to cooling cannot be continuous because the subject cannot be directed to both opposites at once; thus, there must be a moment of rest. This argument is central to understanding why certain changes are not true motions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the issue
Note that some philosophers affirm motion in the categories of action (أن يفعل) and passion (أن ينفعل). The author will refute this claim.
These categories involve being acted upon or acting.
2
Follow the main argument from heating to cooling
The author uses the example of transitioning from heating to cooling. If heating remained during the transition, the subject would be simultaneously directed to both opposites (heat and cold), which is impossible. Therefore, heating must cease before cooling begins, implying a moment of rest.
This is the core logical argument.
3
Consider the objection about intensity
An objector suggests that transition from intense heating to weak heating does not involve opposites, so the argument fails. The author responds that difference in intensity constitutes a difference in kind, so intense and weak hotness are two types that cannot coexist in one subject, thus still involve direction to opposites.
This rebuttal strengthens the original argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that motion in action and passion is impossible because any transition between states would either require simultaneous opposites or a break in continuity, both of which negate continuous motion.
This aligns with the broader kalam view on motion.

The Fourth Observatory - Third Objective: The occurrence of motion among the categories and their changes. That an action (an yaf'al) and that a passion (an yanfa'il). Then some affirmed motion in them and he refuted (the statement of this affirmer) (by saying that the one transitioning from heating (al-tasakhkhun) to cooling (al-tabarrud) for example, his heating does not remain, otherwise there would be a direction to both opposites together) because cooling is a direction to coldness and heating is a direction to hotness, and it is impossible for one thing at one time to be directed to two opposites. And if the heating does not remain, then cooling does not exist except after the cessation of heating; (so between them is a time of rest (sukun) like between two contrary motions in place (al-ayniyya), so there is no continuous motion from heating to cooling. And likewise the case in heating (tas'khin) and cooling (tabrid). And it may be said that heating has different degrees in intensity (al-quwwa)... Al-Shifa as for his statement: al-jidda (vigor) then I have not verified it up to this point, and what is said is that this category indicates the relation of the body to what encompasses and accompanies it in transition, so the change of this relation in the first way is only by the container (al-hawi) and in place (al-makan), so there is no motion in it by itself in my opinion, and it is objected that the change of the containing surface is a motion in place (haraka ayniyya) of the surroundings necessitating the change of that which is surrounded, so it is like two motions, essential, and there is not here one motion attributed to one of them essentially and to the other accidentally. And the answer is that the intended meaning of essential and accidental motion here is not what is well-known, but rather the motion occurring in the category itself by its essence and the motion occurring in it as secondary to the other, even if it is essential. His statement: (Then some affirmed (ilkh)) They said: Indeed a thing may not act nor be acted upon, then gradually it becomes so that it acts and is acted upon, so that it acts and is acted upon is the goal of that amount at that time, like blackness, for the goal of blackness is that the thing may change from not being acted upon by a space to being acted upon by a space, and that is little. And that passion (al-infi'al) may be slow, so it gradually becomes faster and intensifies, and vice versa. The answer to the first is that that motion is not in act and passion but in acquiring the state (al-hay'a) by which it is correct to act and be acted upon. And to the second, what will come later that there must be an intervening rest in that transition, otherwise there would be direction to both opposites. And to the third, that that is a transformation from actual speed gradually, not in that it acts and is acted upon. His statement: (And it is impossible for one thing (ilkh)) It is not hidden that the implication of what the commentator mentioned is the conjunction of direction to hotness with the conjunction of direction to coldness, and there is no opposition between direction to something and direction to its opposite. So the correct is what is in Al-Shifa that if heating remained at the time of transition to cooling, and it is known that transition to cooling is from the nature of cooling and cold, taken from the nature of cooling, then it would be necessary that when a short (ilkh)... intending to repudiate together, and this is impossible. His statement: (And it may be said) means that this explanation only applies in the case of transition from heating to cooling, but in the case of transition from intense heating to weak heating, there is then no direction to two opposites. And in it that difference in intensity and weakness causes difference in kind (al-naw'), so intense and weak of hotness are two types under absolute hotness, they do not coexist in one locus (mahall), so direction to them is direction to the two well-known opposites, even if it is not direction to the two true opposites due to lack of ultimate difference between them.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that motion occurs in the categories of action (أن يفعل) and passion (أن ينفعل). The argument begins by considering the transition from heating to cooling. If the heating remained during the transition, the subject would be simultaneously directed to both opposites (heat and cold), which is impossible. Therefore, the heating must cease before cooling begins, implying a moment of rest between them, like the rest between two contrary motions in place. Thus, there is no continuous motion from heating to cooling. The passage then addresses an objection: heating has degrees, so transition from intense heating to weak heating does not involve opposites. The reply is that difference in intensity constitutes a difference in kind, so intense and weak hotness are two types under absolute hotness and cannot coexist in one subject; thus, direction to them is direction to well-known opposites. The commentary also discusses an objection and answer regarding the category of possession (الملك), but the focus remains on action and passion. The overall conclusion is that motion in these categories is impossible because it would require simultaneous direction to contraries or a break in continuity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That transition from one state to its opposite (e.g., heating to cooling) can occur as a continuous motion without interruption.
Correction: The passage shows that such a transition requires a moment of rest between the two states because the subject cannot be simultaneously directed to two opposites. Hence, it is not a single continuous motion.

Key Takeaway
Continuous motion in the categories of action and passion is impossible because any transition between opposite states requires a moment of rest to avoid simultaneous direction to opposites.

Causality and Created Things

Three Additional Proofs: Commonality, Direction, and Purpose
Presents three additional proofs against corporeality as cause of natural motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three distinct arguments against corporeality being the cause of natural motion: the argument from lack of universality, the argument from diversity of direction, and the argument from purpose.

Core Idea
Corporeality cannot be the cause of motion because not all bodies move, they move in different directions, and motion ceases upon attaining its purpose.

After establishing the first proof, the author presents three additional arguments that further undermine the claim that motion is caused by corporeality. Each proof highlights a different aspect where the observed behavior of bodies contradicts what would be expected if corporeality were the cause. These proofs build on the shared nature of corporeality and the specific, varied, and purposeful character of natural motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the repeated 'وأيضاً' (and also) signaling each new proof.
The style is concise; each proof is a single conditional or observation.
2
Identify the second proof (commonality)
The second proof argues that corporeality is common to all bodies, yet motion is not universal—some bodies are always at rest. If corporeality caused motion, all bodies would necessarily move, contradicting observation.
This proof relies on the absence of motion in some bodies.
3
Identify the third proof (direction)
The third proof argues that if corporeality were the cause, all bodies would move in the same natural direction. But we observe different directions (upward for fire, downward for earth), so the cause cannot be the same for all.
The author notes that both the second and third proofs depend on the assumption that corporeality is shared by all bodies.
4
Identify the fourth proof (purpose)
The fourth proof argues that if motion were caused by corporeality, it would be for a purpose and would cease upon attaining that purpose. But natural motion is continuous unless obstructed, indicating a different cause.
This proof is independent of the commonality assumption.
5
Reflect on the cumulative force
Consider how each proof independently contradicts corporeality as cause. Together, they form a powerful cumulative case.
The author mentions that discussion of the shared assumption will come later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فالجسمية عامة للأجسام كلها والحركة مختصة ببعضها غير عامة لها فإن من الأجسام ما هو ساكن دائماً. وأيضاً فيلزم على تقدير كون الجسمية علة اتحادها في الجهة أي اتحاد الأجسام كلها في جهة الحركة الطبيعية واللازم باطل لأن جهات الحركات الطبيعية مختلفة فبعضها إلى الفوق وبعضها إلى التحت وهذان الدليلان مبنيان على اشتراك الجسمية بين جميع الأجسام وسيأتي الكلام عليه. وأيضاً فلأنها أي الحركة التي علتها الجسمية إما لمطلوب فتنقطع.
And also: corporeality is common to all bodies, while motion is specific to some, not common to all, for among bodies are those that are perpetually at rest. And also: if corporeality were the cause, it would necessitate their unity in direction, i.e., all bodies would be united in the direction of natural motion, but the consequent is false because the directions of natural motions are different—some upwards and some downwards. And these two proofs are based on the commonality of corporeality among all bodies, and discussion of it will come later. And also: because it, i.e., motion whose cause is corporeality, is either for a purpose and thus ceases.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents three additional proofs against corporeality being the cause of natural motion. The second proof: corporeality is a universal attribute of all bodies, but natural motion is not universal—some bodies are always at rest. If corporeality were the cause, every body would necessarily move, but that is not observed. The third proof: if corporeality were the cause, all bodies would move in the same natural direction, but we see some moving upward (like fire) and others downward (like earth). Therefore, the cause cannot be the same for all. The author notes that these two proofs rely on the assumption that corporeality is shared by all bodies, which will be examined later. The fourth proof: if motion were caused by corporeality, it would be for a purpose and would cease upon attaining that purpose, but natural motion is continuous unless obstructed, implying a different cause.

Key Terms
الجسمية
الحركة الطبيعية
الجهة
المطلوب
السكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second and third proofs are essentially the same argument.
Correction: They are distinct: the second proof addresses the lack of universality (some bodies are always at rest), while the third proof addresses the diversity of direction (some move up, some down).

Checkpoint
What assumption do the second and third proofs share?
A) That all natural motions are straight.
B) That corporeality is common to all bodies.
C) That motion is always for a purpose.
D) That rest is impossible for bodies.

Key Takeaway
The diversity and specificity of natural motion—not all bodies move, they move in different directions, and motion ceases upon attaining its purpose—demonstrate that the cause of motion cannot be the shared attribute of corporeality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see that not all bodies move; some are perpetually at rest. Is there an area of your life where you have assumed spiritual or moral 'motion' happens automatically simply because you are a believer? Where is Allah calling you to recognize that effort and divine aid are needed, not mere existence? Commit to one concrete action this week to seek that aid.
?
The diversity of directions—some upwards, some downwards—shows that Allah gives each created thing its own specific purpose and nature. Are you content with the unique purpose Allah has placed in you, or do you secretly wish you were moving in someone else's direction? What would it mean for you to embrace your own 'natural motion' toward Allah?
?
If motion for a purpose ceases upon attainment, consider: what are you moving toward in life? Is your ultimate purpose something that, once achieved, would leave you motionless? Or is your purpose the continuous pursuit of Allah's pleasure, which never ends? Pause and ask Allah to make your heart's motion ever toward Him.

Two Proofs: Motion Not Caused by Body-Essence
Two proofs that natural motion is not caused by the body-essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments proving that natural motion is not caused by the body-essence (al-jismiyya), and why the commonality of essence is central to these proofs.

Core Idea
A cause common to all things cannot explain differences in motion.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents two concise proofs that natural motion—whether upward or downward—cannot be caused by the body-essence (al-jismiyya), a property shared by all bodies. The proofs rely on the principle that a common cause would produce a common effect, but motion is not common in either occurrence or direction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: two distinct proofs followed by a comment on their shared premise.
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof: body-essence is common to all bodies, but natural motion is specific to some—some bodies are perpetually at rest. If essence were the cause, motion should be universal, but it's not.
Common cause → common effect; lack of common effect disproves common cause.
3
Understand the second proof
The second proof: if essence were the cause, all natural motions would share the same direction. In fact, directions differ (upward for fire, downward for earth). Since the consequent is false, the premise (essence as cause) is false.
4
Note the shared premise
The commentator points out that both proofs depend on the claim that body-essence is common (ishtirāk) among all bodies. Later discussion will examine this claim.
This is a crucial assumption that may be challenged.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these proofs matter in kalam: they help establish that motion requires a cause distinct from the body's essence, pointing toward a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

For among bodies there is that which is perpetually at rest. Also, it follows on the supposition that the body-essence is the cause (their unification in direction), i.e., the unification of all bodies in the direction of natural motion, (and the consequent is false) because the directions of natural motions are different: some are upward, some are downward. And these two proofs are based on the commonality (ishtirāk) of body-essence among all bodies, and discussion of it will come.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs that natural motion is not caused by the essence (al-jismiyya). The first proof argues that body-essence is common to all bodies, yet motion is not common—some bodies are perpetually at rest. Therefore, the essence cannot be the cause, for a common cause should produce a common effect. The second proof: if body-essence were the cause, all bodies would share the same direction of natural motion, but in fact directions differ (upward for fire, downward for earth). The consequent is false, so the premise is false. The commentator notes that both proofs depend on the claim that body-essence is common to all bodies, a point to be discussed later.

Key Terms
الجسمية
الحركة الطبيعية
اشتراك
اتحاد الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that body-essence could be a conditional cause of motion—causing motion only in some bodies or with different directions, rather than necessitating uniform and universal motion.
Correction: The proofs assume that a common cause (essence) would produce a common effect (motion in all bodies, same direction). If essence were only a conditional cause, the argument would need further refinement. The commentator hints that the commonality premise itself will be discussed, which might address such objections.

Checkpoint
Why does the second proof conclude that body-essence cannot be the cause of natural motion?
A) Because some bodies never move.
B) Because if it were the cause, all bodies would move in the same direction, but they move in different directions.
C) Because body-essence is not permanent.
D) Because motion is a property of nature, not essence.

Key Takeaway
The body-essence cannot be the cause of natural motion because it is common to all bodies, yet motion is neither universal nor uniform in direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that a common cause cannot explain differences in motion. Now think: Do you ever blame your 'nature' or 'essence' for your spiritual laziness or lack of change? If your nature is common to all humans, how can it explain why you are stagnant while others are moving upward? What does this force you to admit about your choices?
?
The second proof uses differing directions to disprove a single cause. In your life, your desires pull you in many directions (upward toward Allah, downward toward dunya). If a single cause (your essence) cannot explain this pull, then what is the real cause behind each direction? Who is the true mover of your heart?
?
The passage ends by noting that both proofs depend on the commonality of body-essence. If that commonality is later questioned, the proofs would fail. This shows that arguments rest on premises. In your faith, what are the premises you hold unquestionably? Are there any that, if shaken, would collapse your understanding of Allah's attributes? Are you ready to examine them honestly?

Third Proof: Bifurcation Argument Against Body-Essence
Third proof: bifurcation argument against body-essence as cause of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that natural motion cannot be caused by body-essence through a bifurcation argument, and how the objection about perpetual motion is addressed.

Core Idea
Motion caused by essence leads to absurdity whether it has a purpose or not.

This lesson presents the third proof against the view that the body's essence is the cause of its natural motion. The argument uses a logical bifurcation: motion either has a purpose or not, and both options lead to impossibility. It also addresses the objection that some bodies might move perpetually. This proof is central to the kalam critique of natural motion as a necessary property of bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the bifurcation passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Identify the two main branches: motion with a purpose and motion without a purpose.
Notice how each branch leads to a different absurdity.
2
Analyze the first branch
Understand why motion with a purpose leads to violation of causality when the purpose is attained. The author also refutes an objection about perpetual motion.
The key concept is 'complete cause' (علة تامة) cannot have its effect absent.
3
Analyze the second branch
Motion without a purpose either goes to all directions (impossible) or to some directions without reason (preponderance without a preponderant). Both are absurd.
This branch relies on the principle that every choice requires a preponderant.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Since both branches lead to impossibility, the assumption that body-essence is the cause of motion is false.
This proof is a classic reductio ad absurdum in kalam.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that natural motion is not caused by the body-essence. The proof uses a bifurcation: either motion caused by essence has a purpose or not. If it has a purpose, then when the purpose is attained, motion stops, but the essence (cause) remains—so the effect (motion) would be absent while the cause remains, which is a violation of the principle that a complete cause cannot have its effect absent. If motion has no purpose, then the body would move either to all directions at once (impossible) or to some specific directions without reason (preponderance without a preponderant, also impossible). Thus both branches lead to absurdity, so the assumption that body-essence is the cause is false. The commentary then addresses several objections: (1) Some argue that maybe motion never stops because some bodies are never observed to rest. The reply: even if some bodies are perpetually moving, the argument still holds for those that do rest (the first branch). (2) The objection that the third proof, like the second, depends on the commonality of body-essence, but the first proof does not. (3) A discussion about whether natural motion can be circular or rectilinear and the implications for permanence. The commentator also discusses the possibility of multiple purposes and the need for a conscious cause for renewal of purpose. The passage ends with an analysis of the bifurcation and a refutation of the idea that motion could cease while the cause remains, emphasizing that motion does not stop from a complete cause unless the purpose is attained and there is no other purpose.

Key Terms
العلة التامة
المطلوب
الترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof fails if some bodies are perpetually moving and never rest.
Correction: The proof still applies to bodies that do come to rest; the existence of perpetual motion in some bodies does not invalidate the argument for those that rest. Moreover, perpetual motion can be explained by continuous purposes.

Checkpoint
What are the two branches of the bifurcation in the third proof?
A) Motion is either natural or forced.
B) Motion either has a purpose or does not.
C) Motion is either rectilinear or circular.
D) Motion is either from essence or from nature.

Key Takeaway
The third proof uses a logical bifurcation to show that motion cannot be caused by body-essence, as both possibilities lead to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself stuck in a habit or pattern you cannot break, do you ever consider that the cause might not be your inner nature (essence) but something else? What specific step can you take today to identify a different cause for a recurring struggle in your life?
?
The argument shows that if motion had no purpose, it would be absurd. Reflect on your daily actions: are there any motions or routines you engage in without purpose? How can you redirect them toward a meaningful goal that aligns with your ultimate purpose?
?
The proof demonstrates that a complete cause must produce its effect. Is there a good deed or spiritual state you hope to achieve that you are waiting for a 'complete cause' to appear? What is stopping you from becoming that cause yourself starting now?

Why Nature Alone Cannot Cause Motion
Proof that nature alone cannot cause motion; conclusion states the actual cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that nature alone cannot be the cause of natural motion, because nature is permanent while motion is transient.

Core Idea
Permanent cause cannot produce transient effect.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the fourth proof against the idea that nature alone causes motion. He argues that since nature is permanent, if it were the sole cause, motion would be permanent as well, but motion is temporary and renewing. Therefore, the true cause must include an additional factor: a non-appropriate state.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of the proof.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof rests on two premises: (1) nature is permanent, and (2) motion is not permanent (it is renewing and perishing). Find these in the text.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the cause is not nature alone, but nature together with a non-appropriate state. Reflect on why this solves the problem.
Also note the consequence: if nature alone were the cause, rest would be impossible and natural places would vanish.

His saying: (Because it is permanent etc.) This reasoning also applies to denying that body-essence is the cause of motion, but there is discussion because the existent that needs a cause is motion in the sense of the intermediate state, which is a single continuous thing from the beginning of the distance to its end, and what changes is its relation to the boundaries of the distance. If he meant that the permanence of the cause of motion necessitates the permanence of its effect in all its states including position, location, etc., this is denied; neither necessity nor proof indicates it. How? Motion must have a ground (muqtaḍ) absolutely. If that ground is stable in essence, this intention becomes false; if it is not stable, we transfer the discussion to its ground, for everything not stable in essence is absolutely in need of a ground, because it cannot be necessary, and infinite regress is impossible. So it must end in something not stable whose ground is stable. So reflect. His saying: (It is nature with its accompaniment etc.) Al-Imam objected in al-Mulakhkhaṣ: If you allow that nature necessitates motion on condition of the removal of an appropriate state, then the same should be allowed for body-essence. Al-Katibi responded: One may say: This statement does not harm the prover, because his aim is to show that the mover is not the body itself. According to the way you mentioned, the mover would not be the body inasmuch as it is a body, but the body with the removal of an appropriate state. Yes, if the aim were to prove a power subsisting in the body that moves it, it would be as he said, but that is not the aim. Commentary
This section presents the final proof that nature alone cannot be the cause of natural motion, and then states the correct cause. The proof: nature is permanent, but motion is transient (renewing and perishing). If nature alone were the cause, motion would be permanent as long as nature exists, which would make rest impossible for natural movers and eliminate natural places. Hence, the cause must be nature plus a non-appropriate state (ḥāla ghayr mulā'ima). The commentary then addresses several points. First, an explanation of why body-essence plus the non-appropriate state is not considered the cause: specific effects require specific causes, and body-essence is common. Second, a discussion about the permanence argument: the existent requiring a cause is motion as a continuous intermediate state, not its changing relations. If one claims that a permanent cause necessitates a permanent effect in all respects, that is denied. Motion must have a ground (muqtaḍ). If the ground is stable, the claim fails; if the ground is not stable, it requires a further ground, leading to a necessary end. Third, an objection from Imam al-Razi in al-Mulakhkhaṣ: if nature can cause motion under a condition (removal of appropriate state), then body-essence could also cause motion under the same condition. Al-Katibi replies that the aim is not to prove a moving power in the body, but to show that the mover is not the body itself. The body with the removal of an appropriate state is not the same as the body in itself. Thus the objection does not refute the argument.

Key Terms
الطبيعة
الحالة غير الملائمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that since nature is inherent in bodies, it must be the cause of their motion.
Correction: The proof shows that nature's permanence contradicts motion's transience, so nature alone is insufficient.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that nature alone cannot be the cause of natural motion?
A) Because nature is not permanent.
B) Because motion is permanent.
C) Because nature is permanent while motion is transient.
D) Because nature is common to all bodies.

Key Takeaway
Nature alone cannot be the cause of motion because a permanent cause would produce a permanent effect, but motion is transient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your nature is permanent—your desires, your ego. But your faith, your repentance, your good deeds come and go. If your permanent nature could produce these transient states, you would never fall into sin. But you do. So who is the true cause of every moment of your faith? Will you rely on your own nature, or turn to the One who gives and takes?
?
Look at your life: your diligence in prayer today is not the same as yesterday. If your nature alone were enough, your worship would be constant. It is not. What does this transient state of your heart reveal about your need for Al-Muqallib al-Qulub (the Turner of Hearts)? Will you ask Him to stabilize your heart on His obedience?
?
The passage says a permanent cause necessitates a permanent effect. But your soul's state changes. What does this tell you about the limitations of your own created nature? Are you content to let your nature drift, or will you seek the external cause—Allah—for every good state, and beg Him not to leave you to your own nature for even a blink of an eye?

The Ground (Muqtaḍ) Argument: Stable vs Unstable
Discussion of the permanence argument and the muqtaḍ dichotomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between stable and unstable grounds and how the argument leads to a necessary end.

Core Idea
Every non-stable thing requires a stable ground.

This section delves into the permanence argument, discussing that motion requires a ground (muqtaḍ). If that ground is stable, then the original claim fails; if it is unstable, it itself requires a ground, leading to an infinite regress that is impossible, so we must reach a stable ground. This is a classic kalam argument for a necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of 'muqtaḍ'
It means the ground or cause that necessitates something. Here it refers to the cause of motion.
2
Follow the dichotomy
The ground is either stable in essence (qār al-dhāt) or not. If stable, then the original claim fails. If not stable, we transfer to its ground, and so on.
Pay attention to the logic: stable → false; unstable → regress → impossible → must end with unstable grounded in stable.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that it must end in something non-stable whose ground is stable, implying a first cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليست ) علة الحركة الطبيعية هي (الطبيعة) وحدها (أيضاً لأنها ثابتة ) مستمرة ) فيلزم ثبات (معلولها الذي تقتضيه لذاتها ( والحركة ليست ثابتة ) لما عرفت من أنها متجددة منقضية، ويلزم أيضاً دوام الحركة بدوام الطبيعة فيمتنع السكون على الأجسام المتحركة بالطبع فلا يكون شيء من الأمكنة طبيعياً (بل حالة غير ملائمة ) أي بل العلة للحركة الطبيعية هي الطبيعة مع مقارنتها لحالة هي غير ملائمة قوله : (هي الطبيعة إلخ وإنما لم تكن الجسمية مع الحالة الغير الملائمة علة لها لأن الآثار المختصة لكل جسم لا بد لها من علة مختصة . أن تكون مستقيمة ولو سلم، فالكلام هاهنا في الاستدلال على لزوم الانتهاء بناء على حصول المطلوب . قوله : ( لأنها ثابتة إلخ ) هذه العلة جارية في عدم كون الجسمية علة للحركة لكن فيه بحث لأن الموجود المحتاج إلى العلة هو الحركة بمعنى التوسط وهو أمر واحد مستمر من أول المسافة إلى آخرها والمتغير نسبتها إلى حدود المسافة وإن أراد أن دوام علة الحركة يستلزم دوام معلولها بأحواله من وضعه ،وأينه، وغير ذلك فهو ممنوع لا يدل عليه ضرورة ولا برهان كيف والحركة لا بد لها من مقتض البتة فإن كان قار الذات ظهر بطلان هذه الإرادة وإن كان غير قار ننقل الكلام إلى مقتضيه، إذ كل غير قار الذات مفتقر البتة إلى مقتض لامتناع كونه واجباً والتسلسل محال فيلزم الانتهاء إلى شيء غير قار يكون مقتضية قارا، فتأمل. قوله : (هي الطبيعة مع مقارنتها إلخ) اعترض الإمام في الملخص بأنكم إذا جوزتم اقتضاء الطبيعة للحركة بشرط زوال حالة ملائمة فليجز مثل ذلك في اقتضاء الجسمية، قال الكاتبي مجيباًء عنه : ولقائل أن يقول : هذا الكلام لا يضر المستدل لأن غرضه أن يبين أن المحرك ليس هو الجسم لذاته وعلى الوجه الذي ذكرتم لا يكون المحرك هو الجسم من حيث هو جسم بل الجسم مع زوال حالة ملائمة، نعم لو كان الغرض إثبات قوة قائمة بالجسم محركة إياها كان الأمر كما ذكره، وليس الغرض ذلك وأنت خبير بأنه لا يلزم من عدم كون الجسم من حيث هو علة
And (the cause of natural motion is) not (nature) alone (also because it is permanent) continuous, so permanence would be required (for its effect which it necessitates by its essence) (and motion is not permanent) because you know it is renewing and perishing. And it would also require the permanence of motion with the permanence of nature, so rest would be impossible for bodies that move naturally, so no place would be natural. (Rather a non-appropriate state) i.e., rather the cause of natural motion is nature with its accompaniment by a state that is non-appropriate. His saying: (It is nature etc. And the reason why body-essence with the non-appropriate state is not a cause for it is that the specific effects of each body must have a specific cause. That it be rectilinear, even if granted, the discussion here is about inferring the necessity of termination based on the attainment of the purpose.) His saying: (Because it is permanent etc.) This reasoning also applies to denying that body-essence is the cause of motion, but there is discussion because the existent that needs a cause is motion in the sense of the intermediate state, which is a single continuous thing from the beginning of the distance to its end, and what changes is its relation to the boundaries of the distance. If he meant that the permanence of the cause of motion necessitates the permanence of its effect in all its states including position, location, etc., this is denied; neither necessity nor proof indicates it. How? Motion must have a ground (muqtaḍ) absolutely. If that ground is stable in essence, this intention becomes false; if it is not stable, we transfer the discussion to its ground, for everything not stable in essence is absolutely in need of a ground, because it cannot be necessary, and infinite regress is impossible. So it must end in something not stable whose ground is stable. So reflect. His saying: (It is nature with its accompaniment etc.) Al-Imam objected in al-Mulakhkhaṣ: If you allow that nature necessitates motion on condition of the removal of an appropriate state, then the same should be allowed for body-essence. Al-Katibi responded: One may say: This statement does not harm the prover, because his aim is to show that the mover is not the body itself. According to the way you mentioned, the mover would not be the body inasmuch as it is a body, but the body with the removal of an appropriate state. Yes, if the aim were to prove a power subsisting in the body that moves it, it would be as he said, but that is not the aim. And you are aware that it does not follow from the fact that the body inasmuch as it is a body is not the cause...

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the ground is unstable, the regress continues indefinitely.

According to the argument, what is the conclusion about the ground of motion?
A) It must be stable.
B) It must be unstable.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is in constant motion—changes in mood, faith, focus. The argument says all motion depends on a stable ground. What is the stable ground you are actually relying on? Is it truly Allah, or are you depending on something that is itself unstable? If the ground of your stability is unstable, you will never find rest. Will you commit to grounding your heart in the only Stable One today?
?
Infinite regress is impossible. Every chain of dependency must end somewhere. Look at your dependencies: on people, plans, possessions, even your own efforts. Are any of these stable in themselves? The argument forces you to trace every dependency back to the Necessary Being. Are you willing to examine your life's chain of dependencies and cut off those that rely on the unstable?
?
The passage says motion must have a muqtaḍ—a ground. Your spiritual motion (prayer, repentance, growth) also requires a ground. What is the muqtaḍ of your worship? Is it habit? Fear? or a genuine grounding in Allah's stability? If your worship's ground is unstable, it will not last. How will you ensure your entire worship is grounded in the stable essence of Allah alone?

Objection and Response: Al-Razi’s Challenge
Objection from al-Razi and response by al-Katibi regarding the conditionality of causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's objection to the use of 'non-appropriate state' and al-Katibi's response clarifying the aim of the proof.

Core Idea
The proof aims to show the mover is not the body itself, not to deny conditional causation.

Imam al-Razi raises a clever objection: if nature can cause motion when a condition (removal of appropriate state) is met, then why not body-essence similarly? Al-Katibi responds by clarifying that the prover's aim is to show that the mover is not the body as such, not to prove a distinct power. This highlights the precision needed in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Razi's objection
He argues that if nature can be a cause under a condition, then body-essence should also be allowed as a cause under a condition.
2
Read al-Katibi's response
He says the objection does not harm the prover because the prover only wants to show that the mover is not the body itself.
3
Consider the nuance
If the aim were to prove a power subsisting in the body, then the objection would apply, but that is not the aim.

According to al-Katibi, why does al-Razi's objection not harm the prover?

?

?

?
Can you recall a time when you were certain of your own reasoning, only to realize later that your aim was different from what you thought? How does this passage's distinction between aims challenge you to examine your own intentions in debates?

Refuting Body and Nature as Sole Causes of Natural Motion
Refutes body and nature as causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why natural motion cannot be caused solely by the body (jismiyya) or by nature (tabī'a) alone, and how these refutations pave the way for a composite cause.

Core Idea
Both body and nature as sole causes lead to logical impossibilities — the cause must be composite.

The author begins by clearing the ground, showing that two common explanations for natural motion are untenable. If bodyness alone caused motion, we would see motion persisting even after the desired state is reached, or else random motion. If nature alone caused it, motion would be constant and rest impossible. These contradictions force us to seek a more nuanced cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of body alone
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'الحركة الطبيعية ليست هي الجسمية' and its translation. Notice the two options: either motion persists after the desired is attained (causing lag) or the body moves to all directions or some without preference.
The second option is a classic argument: preference without preponderant.
2
Read the refutation of nature alone
Read the passage starting 'وليست علة الحركة الطبيعية هي الطبيعة وحدها'. The author argues that if nature alone caused motion, motion would be constant because nature is constant. Also, rest would be impossible for any naturally moving body.
This refutes the idea that nature is the sole cause.
3
Summarize the two refutations
In your own words, explain why natural motion cannot be caused by body alone or nature alone. What would be the consequences if either were true?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الرابع : الحركة الطبيعية ليست هي الجسمية. الحركة (عنده) أي عند حصول ذلك المطلوب مع بقاء الجسمية التي هي علتها فيلزم التخلف أي تخلف المعلول عن علته وإما لا لمطلوب فتحرك الجسم حينئذ إما إلى جميع الجهات معا وأنه محال بالضرورة وإما إلى بعضها وأنه ترجيح بلا مرجح وليست علة الحركة الطبيعية هي الطبيعة وحدها أيضا لأنها ثابتة مستمرة فيلزم ثبات معلولها الذي تقتضيه لذاتها والحركة ليست ثابتة لما عرفت من أنها متجددة منقضية، ويلزم أيضا دوام الحركة بدوام الطبيعة فيمتنع السكون على الأجسام المتحركة بالطبع فلا يكون شيء من الأمكنة طبيعياً بل العلة للحركة الطبيعية هي الطبيعة مع مقارنتها لحالة غير ملائمة لها فإن تلك الحركة تترك طبعا طلبا للملائم أما في الأين فكالحجر المرمي إلى فوق، وأما في الكيف فكالماء المسخن قسرا، وأما في الكم فكذابل ذبولاً مرضياً، فإن هذه الحالة المنافرة ما دامت باقية كانت الطبيعة محركة للجسم لترده إلى الحالة الملائمة وتختلف أجزاء الحركة بحسب اختلاف القرب والبعد من تلك الحالة المطلوبة، فإذا أوصلته الطبيعة إليها انقطعت الحركة الطبيعية لانتفاء أحد جزأي علتها، أعني مقارنة الحالة الغير الملائمة هكذا قالوا ويتجه عليهم أن يقال: الملائم غاية مطلوبة ولا تتصور الغاية إلا في الحركة الإرادية إذ لا بد من الشعور بالغاية حتى يمكن طلبها، فلا تكون الحركة الطبيعية التي لا شعور معها طلبا للملائم، وإذا لم يكن للطبيعة مطلوب بقي أن تكون هي الحالة التي لا تلائمها مقتضية للحركة وفيه إشكال إذ ليس الحركة الطبيعية إلى جهة حينئذ أولى من الجهة الأخرى وقد يجاب بأن ثبوت الغاية لا يتوقف على الشعور والإرادة وتلخيصه أن الفعل إذا ترتب عليه أمر ترتباً ذاتياً يسمى غاية له فإن كان له مدخل في إقدام الفاعل على ذلك الفعل يسمى غرضاً بالقياس إليه وعلة غائية بالقياس إلى الفعل، فالعلة الغائية هي المحتاجة إلى الشعور دون الغاية فإنها قد ثبتت بلا شعور، إذ لا بعد في أن يكون بعض الأمكنة ملائما لبعض الأجسام، فإذا فرض خارجا عن مكانه الملائم له اقتضى طبيعة الحركة إليه وتكون هذه الحركة طلبا طبيعيا لذلك.
The fourth observation - the fourth purpose: Natural motion is not identical to bodily motion. Motion (according to him) means when that desired is attained while the bodyness which is its cause remains, then there is necessary lag, i.e., the effect lagging behind its cause. Or if there is no desired, then the body moves at that time either to all directions simultaneously, which is impossible by necessity, or to some of them, which is a preference without a preponderant. Nor is the cause of natural motion nature alone, because it is constant and continuous, so its effect which it necessitates by its essence must be constant, but motion is not constant, as you know it is renewing and passing away. Also, motion would be perpetual with the perpetuity of nature, so rest would be impossible for bodies moving naturally, and no place would be natural. Rather, the cause of natural motion is nature together with its conjunction with a non-congenial state, for that motion leaves naturally seeking the congenial—whether in place, like a stone thrown upward; or in quality, like water heated forcibly; or in quantity, like something withering a morbid withering. For as long as this repelling state remains, nature moves the body to return it to the congenial state, and the parts of motion differ according to the difference of nearness and distance from that desired state. When nature brings it to it, natural motion ceases due to the absence of one of the two parts of its cause, namely the conjunction of the non-congenial state. Thus they said. And it is directed against them that one says: The congenial is a desired goal, and the goal is only conceivable in voluntary motion, for there must be awareness of the goal so that it can be sought. Therefore natural motion, which has no awareness, cannot be a seeking of the congenial. And if nature has no desired, then it remains that the non-congenial state is the necessitator of motion. But there is a problem: natural motion to a direction at that time is not more preferable than another direction. It may be answered that the existence of a goal does not depend on awareness and will. The summary is that if an effect is essentially ordered upon an act, it is called a goal for it; if it has an input in the agent's undertaking that act, it is called a purpose relative to him and a final cause relative to the act. So the final cause is what needs awareness, not the goal, for it may be established without awareness. There is no impossibility in some places being congenial to some bodies; so if it is assumed to be outside its congenial place, the nature of motion to it is necessitated, and this motion becomes a natural seeking of that.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a proof that natural motion has a composite cause, not the body alone or nature alone. The author first refutes the idea that bodyness alone causes motion: if it did, motion should cease only when the desired is attained, but the bodyness (cause) remains, leading to the impossibility of the effect lagging behind the cause; also, if the body had no desired, it would move to all directions or just some without preference, both impossible. Then he refutes that nature alone is the cause: nature is constant, so its effect should be constant, but motion is not constant; also, nature would produce perpetual motion, preventing rest. The correct cause is nature together with a non-congenial state. When a body is in a state that opposes its nature, nature impels it toward the congenial state, and motion ceases when that state is reached. However, an objection arises: the congenial state is a goal, and goals require awareness, which natural motion lacks. The author answers that a goal (ghāyah) does not require awareness; only a purpose (gharaḍ) does. The goal is simply the essential consequent of the act. Thus, the non-congenial state is the efficient cause, and the congenial state is the goal without consciousness.

Key Terms
الجسمية
الطبيعة
الحركة الطبيعية
التخلف
الترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural motion can be explained by a single cause like the body's inherent nature or nature alone.
Correction: The passage shows that both lead to logical contradictions, so the cause must be composite.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of body alone, what would happen if the desired state is not attained?
A) The body would remain motionless.
B) The body would move to all directions simultaneously or to some without preference.
C) The body would move only to the natural direction.
D) The body would cease to exist.

Key Takeaway
Natural motion cannot be caused solely by the body or by nature alone; both lead to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe a leaf falling or water flowing, do you attribute it to 'just nature'? This argument shows that nature alone cannot account for the beginning and end of motion. How does this realization press you to see the hand of Allah in every moment of movement? Take a minute to silently acknowledge Allah's constant creation (khalq jadīd) every time you notice something move today.
?
If your own actions were solely caused by your nature, you would have no real choice. But the argument demands a composite cause, implying the need for a choosing agent. When did you last act out of pure habit rather than conscious intention? Ask Allah to make you mindful of your choices, and commit to one action today that you will perform deliberately, not by rote.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
The True Cause: Nature with a Non-Congenial State
States correct cause and addresses objection about goals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct cause of natural motion (nature plus non-congenial state) and how the objection about goals is resolved by the distinction between goal (ghāyah) and purpose (gharaḍ).

Core Idea
Natural motion arises from nature seeking a congenial state when displaced — no consciousness needed, only a natural goal.

After refuting flawed explanations, the author presents the correct view: natural motion is caused by nature when it is combined with a non-congenial state. This explains why bodies move towards their natural places when forced out of them. However, an objection arises: seeking a goal requires awareness, which natural motion lacks. The author resolves this by distinguishing between a goal (ghāyah) that is an essential consequence and a purpose (gharaḍ) that requires consciousness.

Guided Path
1
Read the positive statement
Read from 'بل العلة للحركة الطبيعية' to 'هكذا قالوا'. Notice the examples: stone thrown upward, water heated, withering plant. Understand that the non-congenial state triggers motion towards the congenial.
2
Read the objection
Read from 'ويتجه عليهم أن يقال' to the end. The objection claims that natural motion cannot be a seeking of the congenial because there is no awareness. Also, if there is no desired, the non-congenial state alone would cause motion but without direction preference.
3
Read the answer
The answer distinguishes goal (ghāyah) from purpose (gharaḍ). A goal can exist without awareness if it is an essential consequent. So the congenial state is a goal, not a purpose. Natural motion is a natural seeking without consciousness.

What distinction does the author use to resolve the objection that natural motion cannot seek a goal without awareness?
A) The distinction between body and nature
B) The distinction between goal (ghāyah) and purpose (gharaḍ)

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume that any meaningful direction in your life must be consciously chosen. But this lesson shows that a goal can exist without awareness. Where in your life might you be moving toward a goal you have never consciously set—perhaps returning to a natural state of fitrah? Will you take time today to examine what 'congenial state' your soul is actually seeking, even if you haven't admitted it?
?
When you face discomfort—like a stone thrown upward or water heated—your immediate reaction is to resist or escape. Yet this discomfort is the very thing that sets nature in motion toward its authentic state. What persistent non-congenial state in your life might actually be God's way of returning you to your true home? Will you welcome it rather than fight it?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Natural Motion: Definition and the Objection from Consciousness
Defines natural motion and answers the objection about consciousness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of natural motion as the inclination of a body to return to its suitable state, and how the objection that it requires consciousness is answered by distinguishing between an end (ghaya) and a purpose (gharad).

Core Idea
Natural motion seeks a goal, but not consciously — the goal is an essential consequence, not a motive.

In this passage, the author defines natural motion and addresses a key objection: if natural motion 'seeks' a suitable state, doesn't that require consciousness? To answer, he introduces a crucial distinction between a goal that is merely a result and a purpose that motivates an agent. This distinction preserves the teleological character of natural motion without attributing consciousness to inanimate matter.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition and examples of natural motion
Read the passage's opening definition and the examples for place, quality, and quantity. Notice how natural motion is a response to being removed from a suitable state.
The examples help ground the abstract concept.
2
Follow the mechanism: nature moves until the suitable state is reached
The motion continues as long as the repellant state persists. When the suitable state is attained, motion ceases. This is like a cause with two parts: the unsuited state is present, then absent.
3
Examine the objection: why natural motion cannot be a seeking
The objection argues that seeking a goal requires consciousness. Without consciousness, the motion would be random. Identify the problem.
This is a common critique of teleological explanations in nature.
4
Grasp the answer: distinction between end (ghaya) and purpose (gharad)
The answer clarifies that an end can exist without consciousness; only a purpose (a goal that motivates the agent) requires consciousness. Natural motion has an end, not a purpose.
This preserves the causal structure without implying consciousness.
5
Apply the distinction to the example
Consider a stone falling to its natural place. The stone's motion has an end (reaching the ground) but no purpose. The stone does not 'want' to fall; it simply does.
This shows the explanatory power of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الرابع : الحركة الطبيعية ليست هي الجسمية ملائمة لها فإن تلك الحركة تترك طبعاً طلباً للملائم أما في الأين فكالحجر المرمي إلى فوق وأما في الكيف فكالماء المسخن قسراً وأما في الكم فكالذابل ذبولاً مرضياً فإن هذه الحالة المنافرة ما دامت باقية كانت الطبيعة محركة للجسم لترده إلى الحالة الملائمة وتختلف أجزاء الحركة بحسب اختلاف القرب والبعد من تلك الحالة المطلوبة فإذا أوصلته الطبيعة إليها انقطعت الحركة الطبيعية لانتفاء أحد جزأي علتها أعني مقارنة الحالة الغير الملائمة هكذا قالوا ويتجه عليهم أن يقال الملائم غاية مطلوبة ولا تتصور الغاية إلا في الحركة الإرادية إذ لا بد من الشعور بالغاية حتى يمكن طلبها فلا تكون الحركة الطبيعية التي لا شعور معها طلباً للملائم وإذا لم يكن للطبيعة مطلوب بقي أن تكون هي الحالة التي لا تلائمها مقتضية للحركة وفيه إشكال إذ ليس الحركة الطبيعية إلى جهة حينئذ أولى من الجهة الأخرى وقد يجاب بأن ثبوت الغاية لا يتوقف على الشعور والإرادة وتلخيصه أن الفعل إذا ترتب عليه أمر ترتباً ذاتياً يسمى غاية له فإن كان له مدخل في إقدام الفاعل على ذلك الفعل يسمى غرضاً بالقياس إليه وعلة غائية بالقياس إلى الفعل فالعلة الغائية هي المحتاجة إلى الشعور دون الغاية فإنها قد ثبتت بلا شعور إذ لا بعد في أن يكون بعض الأمكنة ملائماً لبعض الأجسام فإذا فرض خارجاً عن مكانه الملائم له اقتضى طبيعة الحركة إليه وتكون هذه الحركة طلباً طبيعياً لذلك
Observation Four - Fourth Topic: Natural motion is not the corporeal [motion] but the suitable for it. For that motion leaves naturally seeking the suitable. As for place, like a stone thrown upward; as for quality, like water heated by force; as for quantity, like a plant withering from disease. For this repellant state, as long as it remains, nature moves the body to return it to the suitable state. And the parts of the motion differ according to the proximity and distance from that sought state. When nature brings it to it, the natural motion ceases due to the absence of one of the two parts of its cause, namely the presence of the unsuited state. Thus they said. And an objection is directed against them: the suitable is a sought end, and the end is only conceivable in voluntary motion, because consciousness of the end is necessary for it to be sought. So natural motion, which has no consciousness, cannot be a seeking of the suitable. And if nature has no sought thing, then [the motion] would be necessitated by the unsuited state itself. And in it there is a difficulty: then natural motion towards one direction is not more appropriate than another. And it may be answered that the existence of the end does not depend on consciousness and will. The summary of it is: if an action is essentially followed by something, that thing is called its end. If it has a role in the agent's undertaking the action, it is called a purpose in relation to the agent and a final cause in relation to the action. So the final cause is what needs consciousness, not the end, for it may be established without consciousness, since it is not far-fetched that some places are suitable for some bodies. So if it is assumed outside its suitable place, the nature necessitates motion towards it, and this motion is a natural seeking of that.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage defines natural motion and addresses an objection. First, it describes natural motion as the tendency of a body to return to its natural state when removed from it, with examples in place, quality, and quantity. The motion continues until the suitable state is reached. Then an objection is raised: the suitable state is a goal (غاية), and goals require consciousness and will, so natural motion cannot be a seeking of a goal. If nature has no goal, the motion could be in any direction, which is problematic. The answer distinguishes between a goal (غاية) and a purpose (غرض). A goal is an essential consequence of an action and does not require consciousness; it is simply what the action tends to. A purpose is a goal that motivates the agent, requiring consciousness. For example, a body out of its natural place will naturally move back to it, and this motion is a seeking of that place, but it does not require consciousness. Thus the objection is resolved.

Key Terms
حركة طبيعية
غاية
غرض
علة غائية
شعور
إرادة
طلب الملائم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural motion, because it seeks an end, implies that nature has consciousness or will.
Correction: The passage shows that an end (ghaya) can exist without consciousness; only a purpose (gharad) requires consciousness. Natural motion has an end, not a purpose.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between a غاية (end) and a غرض (purpose)?
A) Both require consciousness
B) The غاية requires consciousness, the غرض does not
C) The غرض requires consciousness, the غاية does not
D) Neither requires consciousness

Key Takeaway
Natural motion is the body's inclination to return to its suitable state, and this seeking does not imply consciousness because the end (غاية) is an essential consequence, not a conscious purpose (غرض).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a habit or automatic reaction you have (like turning off an alarm or adjusting your posture). In light of the distinction between غاية and غرض, is that habit seeking an end without conscious purpose? Could neglecting awareness of such ends be pulling you away from your natural fitra? What would it look like to consciously align those automatic motions with your purpose?
?
This passage argues that ends can exist without a conscious agent. Where in your life are you being moved toward an end you haven't consciously chosen? Are you currently in a 'repellant state' that nature is trying to correct, but you're resisting? What would surrendering to that correction require?
?
If natural motion does not need consciousness to be directed, what does that say about the One who designed such nature? How does this understanding of innate inclination challenge your belief about your own autonomy? Are there areas where you insist on 'conscious purpose' but God's design already has an end for you?
